The RED PAGE
Captains Courageous
Emotion recollected in tranquillity was
Wordsworth’s ■' recipe for poetry, and the
sailor, after the voyage is over, remembers
the thrill of danger and the majesty ot storm,
and sees some of the romance of the sea,
however much he hated the life at the time.
Occasionally he gets poetical when talking
about it over pipe and glass; but few can
write well enough to impart the thrill to
landsmen readers. Marryat, Herman Mel-
ville, Conrad, Masefield and Clark Russell;
Dana in Two Years Before the Mast, and
Bullen in his first book —that’s about all.
Here are two more.
Maurice Drake is not so good a writer as
any of his forerunners; but he has lived
through adventures which are novel and
quite as exciting as any in sea books. Some
of these are told in Wrack and The Salving
of a Derelict (Duckworth). They are stories
of modern sailoring on iron ships, with the
reek of oil and steam and bilge, and, over
and above the usual dangers of the sea, the
peculiar risks of salvage work. The crews
are picked men ; but no insurance company
will take their “lives.” There is a chance
of rich reward, and they get “ the crowded
hour” pretty frequently. A shipowner in
Wrack states the case :
It’s living, yon—a man’s life... . Wanderin’ —
they never know where they’ll be ordered next.
p a y?—there’s fitters on the Electra drawin
twelve pounds a week when they’re workm iv ry
tide. Risk?—that shallow-draught wooden packet
joust skates around all th’ worst coasts in tn
world chook-a-block full of steam gear an high-
powered explosives. Wherever theer’s danger
tricky tides, or bad flaws o’ wind, or rock, or
shoal—wherever theer’s wrecks, theer lies her
work. ,
Drake gives the strength of this m parts
of both books, but Wrack is a story with a
love interest which ends unsatisfactorily.
. * * *
The Brassbounder, by David W. Bone
(Sydney Book Club) is an account of one
voyage from Glasgow, round the Horn to
’Frisco, and back again. r lher© is no plot,
no girl he left behind him, no secondary in-
terest. It is just an apprentice’s version of
life on a sailing ship of the present day, yet
it recalls the charm of the early voyages,
and is the best book about the ways of men
on “ a ship of sticks and strings ” that has
been written for many a year.
From the time of the first capstan chantey:
Blow, boy-s, blow—for Cahfor-ny , ok 1
For there's lots of gold, so I’ve been told
On the banks of Sa-oramen-to l
the voyage is described in episodes, the
talk of the men is given, each in his own
linoo, and you get to know most of them be-
fore they’re done with. One man stands
out—the Old Man, the canny, good-natured
Scotch captain, who loves his ship and looks
after the owner’s interests. When a sea-
man’s leg is broken, he sets it. When the
ship’s bow is smashed by an iceberg, he
mends it. He chaffers with the tugboat
captain off the Golden Gate, and beats him
down from 600 to 300 dollars for the tow,
all the time desperately hiding the fact that
the cargo is on fire; and, not till the ship is
I berthed, does the tug man find that he
missed 3000 dollars salvage. ■ In one fine
scene the old man smooths down a discon-
tented crew, who object to going back from
’Frisco three men short. ,
Almost at the journey’s end, the pilot
makes a mistake and the captain saves the
ship from the rocks:
A gallant fight l At theweather gangway
stood Old Joek, legs apart and sturdy, talking to
h '“ B Stand, good spars,” he would say, casting
longing eyes aloft. Or, patting the ta * al .l v^ h
his great sailor hands, Up tae it, ye bitch .
Un ' f Up 11!” as, raising her head, streamingm
cascade from a sail-pressed plunge, she turned to
meet the next great wall of water that set against
her .. . Obedient to the helm and the mate s
ready hand at the driver sheets—she flew off, free
of the wind and sea-tearing past tbe iower ng
rocks a cable’s length to leeward. ... And high
above the tumult of the waters and the loud, glad
cries of us, the hoarse choking voice of the man
who backed his ship. ... „ . n j
“Done it, ye bitch!”—a now trembling hand
at his old grey head. “Done it! Weathered, by
Goad!”
Bone’s method is objective; he makes you
see the thing described, and says little about
his own sensations —except that he says,
when at work in freezing, stormy weather,
he would have preferred a job as railway
porter. Why does a sailor go back, alter
vowing in sodden misery on many of the
nights described, that he will never go to sea
any more ?
Pride. Rotten pride! We come home from
our first vovage sick of it all. . . . Woudgiveup
hut for pride. . . . Afraid to be called stuck
sailors afraid of the sneers of our old school-
mates.’ So we come home in a great show of
braverv, and swagger about in our brass-bound
uniforms, and lie bravely about the fine times we
had . . . out there !
* * * *
Oh, leave ’r, Johnny, leave ’r like a man
(An’ leave V, Johnny, leave ’r);
Oh ! Leave ’r, Johnny, leave 'r when ye can
(An it’s time—for us—t’ leave ’rl)
There were a few fine times, and Bone does
justice to them. His style is simple and
direct,' rising fully to the occasion in some
descriptions of the old barque battling
through rough weather, and holding the
reader to the end,

Barkers , by E. Lacon Watson (Murray).
An old London publishing house which was
peacefully decaying is rushed into new ven-
tures—fiction and a magazine, by a light-
hearted young man. He draws his friends
into the concern, and though they do not
make the new business succeed, Barkers
makes them. The “ shop ” talk is interest-
ing, and one man touches on a fascinating
problem:
What does sell a novel ? After all these years,
I confess myself absolutely unable to decide this
knotty point. Assuredly the most flaring reviews
will often fail to do so, and the most expensive
advertisement. Some pin their faith to. the
chatter of women over the tea table ; but this is
more likelv to be the effect of popularity than the
cause. Stuart Mitchell had a theory some years
ago that big sales were due to some hitherto un-
discovered bacillus. Readers became infected
with a new book as with a disease, and when the
gallant microbes once obtained a footing they
spread with a rapidity impossible to forecast and
difficult to check. A mammoth fortune awaits
the gentleman who first isolates the micrococcus
of popularity, and retails specimens to needy or
ambitious authors.
“I always thought you were more than yon
seemed,” she murmured. Looking up at him mis-
ohievously, she added : “So you deceived ra® 1
marry a title after all ? ” He looked down proudly
at her as he replied with his frank smile: Hut i
wooed you as a poor man. You are mine by
right of conquest.”
As you may imagine, she is the daughter of
an American multi-millionaire; he is an
Englishman as beautiful as Apollo, lhey
survived the wreck and lived in idyllic but
perfectly proper conditions on a desert
island—so Arthur Hornblow says m By
Bight of Conquest (G. Robertson and Co.).
A book on fairies by one who does not
believe in them, or pretend to believe in
them, is not likely to be very attractive, nor
is an account of The Origins of Popular
Superstitions and Customs, by a man like 1.
Sharper Knowlson, whose name suggests
ultra-cuteness. He deals only with those
superstitions which are still in force, and
explains why people regard it as unlucky to
walk under a ladder on Friday the thirteenth
if the hodman happened to spill the salt at
breakfast.
G. B. Lancaster
There is much to admirb in G. B. Lan-
caster’s vigorous stories of strong men who
go through great emotional crises and do
small things in a big way. Jim of the
Ranges (Constable) is a shade better than its
predecessors. The action takes place in the
Australian bush—Gippsland, apparently—
and the reader views some bits of it as if
by flashlight. The characters are painted in
strong colors; they are human, all-too-
human some of them; but, occasionally,
they do and say things no bushman should
be made responsible for in print. One man
“ia now hoofing the pad for and
another goes to the bar and orders Three
star; yes, a long sleever.” Other details are
inaccurate, and betray the rapid impression-
ist. There is a tense, strained air about the
writing, which is G. B. Lancaster s worst
fault. There is no doubt, however, about
the force ‘and originality of her phrasing.
You get it in this description of the bush as
seen for the first time by a town boy :
The track slewed sharp round, bringing windy
I scents out of the distances and a sight that rushed
the Kid’s heart into his mouth. Half the world
lay at gaze below; aloof, sternly apart as are the
mountains of the moon. Deep down a torrent
shredded out its white flesh in the black rocks;
but across the chasm a riot of sunshine lay, golden
and glorious. And the distance was sheen of bush
and shimmer of rock. It was a network of spurs,
hazy as the purple bloom on ripe fruit. And last,
it was the pure snow ranges withdrawn among
their clouds as a young soul is withdrawn from the
careless touch.
Here are some other characteristic sen-
tences:
On the ranges the last rays paled to ash, and all
earth’s color was sucked up into the sky. The
fluttering pink in the bush-gully below was quick
come and quick gone as a young girl s blush ; but
in the western skv behind the farthest snow-cone
floods of strong crimson pumped up and down
like a man’s heart-blood.
The women are rather crude types— Roseen,
who knew that “to be in Jove is not a con-
dition but an art,” does not ring true. But,
with all faults, Jim of the Ranges is to be
read and valued.

The miniature volumes in what is called
vest-pocket size ” are popular at Christmas
time, and are suitable for short specimens
of verse or little anthologies. Australians can
now get nuggets of their own verse to send
to friends abroad. At least, some of them
I are nuggets j others are quartz with a few
specks. There’s Victor J . Daley, for instance
pieces from At Dawn and Dusk, and his later
work —all gold. Bush Ballads, from Gordon
and others- —say 15 carat. Poems, by Bernard
O’Dowd, is an admirable selection from his
four books, and to any who know not
O’Dowd it will be a fine introduction. The
selection from Henry Kendall, in similar
form, is inadequate, though the readei gets
a taste of his quality in “ Orara, '‘Outre
Mer” and “To a Mountain.” The name of
the late Jennings Carmichael has been
brought before Australia lately in connec-
tion with her children, and a bundle of her
verses has a momentary interest. 11 illiam
Gay is represented by a few good sonnets,
and some other verses of slender value. The
breezy, salt-junk bard, Edwin J. Brady,
appears to disadvantage in Bushland Ballads,
reprinted for the first time. His Muse
wants a lot of room to kick about in ; but is
more at home on shipboard than in the
bush. The price of these at Angus and
Robertson’s is Is. 3d. each.
I Another Australian magazine has ventured
ut into the perilous seas. It was launched at
>erth in October, and is named TheLecumn
Lioness), after" the sturdy Dutch ship which
klited the south-western cape in 1G22, and turned
way north. The cover design, from a woodcut
iv A. G. Plate, is the most artistic part of INo. l.
t shows a hungry-looking lioness in a threatening
ttitude, which may be taken as a warning to
ontributors or to Fat. The verse and articles
.re only of moderate interest; but this is the first
Australian monthly magazine, and it may yet Jie
aden with the literary wealth which, like
)ther gold, awaits the bold and lucky prospector.

Under the vague title of Cosmos and Common-
wealth, one Dominic Healy; of Maryborough (Q.),
ets loose some wild notions about immigration
rnd other matters. He has no time for lm-
□erialism, which is fostered in Australia by the
daily press—“or, rather, that reflex of London
opinion commonly called the Australian
and England’s security is not essential to Aus-
tralia. “The English bulldog is not the otdy
piece of meat in the universal sausage. Australia
should reach out beyond England to the cosmos.
It is the first duty of Australians to people their
country with a white race, “not white m the
narrow racial sense, but white in conscience, pure
of heart.” (Imagine a pure heart test for immi-
grants !) He views with equanimity a possible
infusion of “the noble Japanese,” under the white
C< Eventua'lly the amorous Australian youth might learn
to indite impassioned sonnets to his mistress s eyebrow
even were it possessed of a little Oriental slant.
Dominic dirige nos / 0 Lord, deliver us !

G-. K. Chesterton is fading in radiance as
he. gains in popularity. In his latest exploit
the writing of detective yarns for 1 he Story-
teller—be shows some evidence of failing
cleverness. In the first yarn—“ The Inno-
cence of. Father Brown ”-a marvellous
French detective chases a miraculous Frencfi
thief, who is pursuing an RC. priest with a,
valuable cross. The three rush about and
converse much in the manner of the beings
in The Man Who Was Thursday. And all
through the yarn are blinding paragraphs
that prod the reader with their audacious
irrelevance. Thus : , „ .
The most incredible thing about miracles is
that they happen. A few clouds in heaven do
come together in the staring shape of one human
eye. A tree does stand up in the landscape
of a doubtful journey in the exact and elabo-
rate shape of a note of interrogation. I have
seen both these things myself within the
last few days. Nelson does die in the in-
stant of victory; and a man named V\ imams
does quite accidentally murder a man named
Williamson ; it seems a sort of infanticide. In
short, there is in life an element of elfin coinci-
dence which people reckoning on the prosaic may
perpetually miss. As it has been well expressed
in the paradox of Poe, wisdom should reckon on
the unforeseen.
This remark seems absurdly superfluous to
this tale of adventure. But Chesterton
usually crowds his narratives with such
merciless reflections. In reading one of his
stories, one has always to wait every ten or
a dozen lines while he plays two-up with
truisms or starts a gavotte of Chestertonian
expressions.
JEAN SIBI.
There is a good shilling’s worth of broad
humor in The Adventures of Smiling Sam ,
oy R. R. Thorne (Worker Office, Sydney).
Sam, with “ a constitution like a lighthouse ”
and a wonderful smile, pranks amusingly,
while his father does those comical queer
things which the bush father is expected to
do in fiction. The father is a distant rela-
tion of Steele Rudd’s Dad, and his prepared
impromptu speech may have been suggested
by Mark Twain; still, Thorne’s work, on the
whole, is fresh and bright, his lively inci-
dents run into one another naturally, and
his style is unaffected. The illustrations, by
Claude Marquet and others, are appropriate,
save that in one a full-grown cow appears to
be no higher than the kitchen table.
The Lost Echo, by James Green (Book-
stall Co.), is not much of a story, but it gives
a good account of the life of the pioneer
cedar-getters on the Richmond Rivei, and
points a sound moral about the ruthless
destruction of timber.
Poems by James Stephens
TO THE FOUR COURTS, PLEASE.
The driver rubbed at his nettly chin
With a huge, loose forefinger, crooked ana black,
And his wobbly, violet lips sucked in,
And puffed out again and hung down slack:
One fang shone through his lop-sided smile.
In his little, pouched eye flickered years of guile.
And the horse, poor beast, it was ribbed and
forked, . ~
And its ears hung down, and its eyes were old,
And its knees were knucklyj, and as we talked
It swung the stiff neck that could scarcely hold
Its big, skinny head up-—then t stepped in.
And the driver climbed to his seat with a grin.
God help the horse and the driver, too,
And the people and beasts who have never a friend,
For the driver easily might have been you,
And the horse be me by a different end.
And nobody knows how their days will cease,
And the poor, when they’re old, have little of
peace.
I THE SHELL.
And then I pressed the shell
Close to my ear
And listened well.
And straightway like a bell
Came low and clear
The slow, sad murmur of far distant seas,
Whipped by an iey breeze
Upon a shore
Wind-swept and desolate.
It was a sunless strand that never bore
The footprints of a man,
Nor felt the weight
Since time began
Of any human quality or stir
Save what the dreary winds and waves incur.
And in the hush of waters was the sound
Of pebbles rolling round,
For ever rolling like a hollow sound.
And bubbling sea-weeds as the waters go
Swish to and fro
Their long, cold tentacles of slimy grey.
There was no day,
Nor ever came a night •
Setting the stars alight
To wonder at the moon :
Was twilight only, and the frightened croon,
Smitten to whimpers, of the dreary wind
And waves that journeyed blind—
And then I loosed my ear—Oh, it was sweet
To hear a cart go jolting down the street.
WINDY CORNER.
Here and there along the street
The lamps are dimly burning,
I And all is silent save the fleet
Footsteps of the winds that meet
To tattle at the turning. ' i,
Sleeping softly snug inside
Lie the people resting, ; \ .
Lip to cheek or side by side,
While I wander where the wide
Breezes go a-questing.
Oh, I can tell and I can know
What the wind rehearses :
“ A poet loved a lady so,
Loved her well, and let her go
While he wrote his verses.
Here he met the maiden fair, (
And here they parted madly, *
Though she’s wed, he still will wear
A shining, twining hoop of hair,
And sing about her sadly.” .
That’s the tale the winds relate
Soon as night is shady.
If it’s true, I’ll simply state
A poet is a fool to rate
His art above the lady.
(From Insurrections, published by Maunsel and Co.,
Dublin.)
WOMEN.
Listen, if but women were
Half as kind as they are fair,
There would be an end to all
Miseries that do appall.
Cloud and wind would fly together
In a dance of sunny weather :
And the happy trees would throw
Gifts to travellers below.
Then the lion, meek and mild,
I With the lamb would, side by side,
Couch him friendly and would be
Innocent of enmity.
Then the Frozen Pole would go
Shaking off his fields of snow
To a kinder clime and dance
Warmly with the girls of France.
These : if women only were
Half as kind as they are fair.
(From The English Review , Sept., 10.)

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Posilippo.
From Mosman’s sandstone look I down
On starlit shores of old Cremorne,
To shining lights of Sydney town,
West to the place where I was born.
Upon the wondrous haven’s tide,
Lights of the ships of all the seas,
Glimmer and wink in ripples wide,
Doubling a hundred argosies.
The wind-blown, sea-pressed sandstone heights,
High held from all the market noise ;
The infinitely-many lights ;
The sense of peace and equipoise.
Posilippo ! A rest from grief !
1 or trade and commerce pass us by ;
Only the tapping of the leaf
To tell us that a breeze is nigh.
Rest! Where the sandstone cliff invites
To lazy dreams by seas empearled ;
Rest ! in the glamor of the nights
And swift forgetting of the world.
But yonder city sweats and roars 1
I hear the calling of the sea !
I must sail out to stranger shores
To grip the prize that waits for me.
N.S.W.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
At Dusk.
At dusk the sullen sunset faded out
The while a homeless wind sang its desire,
And left across the dim path of the west
A trace of sunset fire.
The racing breakers tumbled on the beach,
With hollow boornings filling all the night.
They flashed and disappeared and flashed again
In gleams of ghostly white.
Beneath the low’ring canopy of cloud,
The air was fresh with sudden summer rain ;
Then peeped beyond the flying Wind-torn veil
Faint glowing stars again.
A round, red moon sprang up beyond the great
Wet turmoil of the waters tossing cold.
And smote across the wide fields of the sea
A path of broken gold.
The winds sank low, the noisy tumult died,
To sleepy sighs and murmurs hoarse and deep ;
By rocky coast and shallow beach all earth
Sank softly into sleep.
There showed a wonderous mist of tangled cloud,
O’er which the moon stepped with its silver feet,
And there was peace on all the liquid ways
Where the great waters meet.
VICTORIA.
KATHLEEN WALKER.
Saturday Girls.
Under the flaring arc they go,
A Pageant of changing Girl.
Flirt of petticoat, flutter of lace,
Vision of ankle and smile-set face ;
Jostling, dallying, pushing for place—
Saturday's Pageant of Girl.
Where are the girls of yesteryear
Who used to smile on me?
The laughing lure of Lalage,
And Qhloe’s mockery.
The folly and the careless vow,
The thoughtless yea and nay,
The week-end revel after toil.
The Girls of Saturday.
For some are dead, and some are wed,
And some have flown afar.
And some who live have lived to wish
They, too, had crossed the bar.
Where are the maids of many moods
Who passed with laughing eye,
In twos and threes, with ready tongue,
Who cared not Whence or Why ?
They’re gone—and forth their daughters fare
To* walk their mothers’ way.
The years may die, but ever live
The Girls of Saturday.
And some have strayed and some, betrayed,
Have fled to save their name.
And some have stayed and stayed to stray
Along the Road of Shame.
And so they do their dreary round,
And seek their poor romance
Among the crowds where play about
The little gods of Chance,
Who laugh and sort the couples out
And send them on their way ;
Mary and Maud and Kate and Flo—
The Girls of Saturday.
And some are glad, and some are sad,
Who think of Saturday.
And some with tears and some with years
Have found the Better Way.
Under the flaring arc they go,
A Pageant of changing Girl.
Flirt of petticoat, flutter of lace,
Vision of ankle and smile-set face ;
Jostling, dallying, pushing for place—
Saturday's Pageant of Girl.
VICTORIA.

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountain, N.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season. . .
Medlow Bath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours. ~ .
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office-or your own residence-direct
to the Majestic Hotel. . ,
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds. ■
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Caves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Castlereagh Street.
w. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tint Bulletin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscript ions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin, ” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 10, 1910.
THE BEGINNING OF THE NEW ERA.
The Northern Territory Acceptance Bill has at
last clambered through the Australian Parlia-
ment, so there is hope that this country will now
start doing the Larger Things. To this day some
three-fifths or more of inland Australia is unap-
proachable by railway, is beyond all reasonable
possibility of defence, and isn’t even effectively
occupied within the meaning of the Act. Until
a few years ago that mattered very little. There
was no probable enemy in view till a few years
ugo; and the Pacific Ocean was a mere
backwash, surrounded in great part by
dreaming comic-opera nations with absurd
hats and flagrant paper umbrellas; Australia
had no army or navy, and no visible need of
either; Britain was the unquestioned mistress of
the seas, and in a general way things didn’t
signify. In those days the time to look earnestly
after the neglected and empty and undefended
sections of Australia was next century, or in the
Greek Kalends, or when the cows came home, or
almost any other old date. Unfortunately,
the world has become more strenuous, and
the old, easy-going life is not likely to recur
any time during the next ten centuries. lhe
Federal decision to take over the Territory is one
evidence that Australia begins to realise this fact.
Unfortunately, eight precious years were wasted
before the transfer was seriously attempted, and
one more year has been fooled away by reason of
the insane clamor of certain folk in the eastern
States to have the transcontinental railway
brought at any cost to their own backyards, wi th
a station just at the family ash-barrel and a sid-
ing exactly alongside the cat and the coal-scuttle.
Now it is up to Australia to make the best of
what time remains to it, be the same long or short.
* ■*
To begin with, it is essential to realise that the
first transcontinental railway is not to be a squat-
ters’ line; that its main business is not the “ link-
ing up” of a number of non-paying sheep rail-
ways which end nowhere, so that it may be made
easy for any large, hot pastoralist to move his
jumbucks about in drought time ; and that, even
if south-western Queensland were the garden it
certainly isn’t, the,opening up of south-western
Queensland has nothing to do with the case. 1 he
first transcontinental line should be largely
strategic. It is wanted (1) to carry settlers to the
Northern Territory so that there may be a
strong white population to defend the place;
(2) to open up the Territory so as to enable
it to support a strong white population ; (3)
to provide for the defence of the Territory
by making it possible to send troops there in a
hurry in case of invasion. The Territory can’t be
developed by the building of a railway in Queens-
land any more than Canada can be opened up by
the construction of a tramline in Greece. More-
over, Queensland has its own State Government
to build its railways, while the Territory hasn’t.
And, as a further argument, the Australian
Government is in desperate need of revenue, and
it can get revenue by making available its own
land in the Territory, while a deviated railway
through Queensland and N.S.W. would only
make money for the State Treasuries at Brisbane
and Sydney. As for the question of defence, any
person save a fool or a knave must admit
that the first essential is to connect the Ter-
ritory with the nearest large city, and the
one which is most likely to have troops to
spare when the North requires them. Adelaide
is by a good many miles the’nearest big city to
Port Darwin by land, and Melbourne is not much
farther off. They are also the most remote of the
State capitals from Port Darwin by sea ; in other
words, when the North is attacked by sea, they
can spare troops from the local defences without
immediate danger to themselves, whereas all the
towns north of them will be busy looking to their
own security. To put it in another way, when
troubles are many and soldiers are altogether too
few, the garrisons of Port Darwin and Adelaide
will be practically the same body of men moving
on the shortest possible line of internal communi-
cation.
* * * *
Under the agreement which the Australian
Parliament has just ratified, the straight line
from north to south is, as a matter of obvious fact,
compulsory. Even a bushel of very inferior legal
opinions can’t alter that fact. Yet it is certain
that strenuous efforts will be made even now to
break faith and the law and the agreement; and
to drag the Port Darwin line round in a large,
foolish semi-circle through the eastern States. A
great deal of this effort arises from a curiously
deranged idea that there will only be one railway
from north to south, just as there is only one
Devil and one Day of judgment—that there can
never, under any circumstances, be another ; and
that the State which fails to get there now will
be for ever left. The people who reason in that
fashion have the same inability to realise the
possible future of Australia as had the old-time
potentate who altered Little Oollins-street and
Little Bourke-street, in Melbourne, from wide
thoroughfares to mere lanes, on the ground that
they would never be required for any more
crowded purpose than the admission of the
settler’s cow to his back gate. In the compara-
tively near future Australia will probably require
(1) a" great extension northward of the present
Melbourne-Rockhampton coastal railway; (2) a
line further west through western N.S.W. and
Queensland to some port on the Gulf ; (3) the
great midland line from Adelaide to Port
Darwin, a large part of which is already built;
(4) a north and south railway in some part of
Westralia; (5) a transcontinental railway con-
necting Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide with
Perth (most of this is already built); and (6) an-
other cross-country line from Townsville or there-
abouts to Kimberley or some other convenient
part of Westralia. All these should be cheap and
easy lines to construct, for they would lie mostly
in flat country ; there should be practically nothing
to pay for land resumptions, and the community
should get all the increased land values which
they would create. Hitherto Australia, under
a purely parish pump policy, has been so
busy building up six disconnected State
railway systems, and so much occupied with
its struggles to keep them as disconnected as
possible, that propositions like this seem strange
to its habit of mind. It has been impossible to
avoid having one railway connection between
Queensland and N.S.W., N.S.W. and Victoria,
and Victoria and S.A., in order that people may
get to the Melbourne Cup ; but anything beyond
that has generally been held accursed. Yet when
once the Commonwealth getsintoits strideasa rail-
way-builder on national principles, the idea of six
transcontinental railways will presently begin to
appear simple and obvious. Of course, the
beginning of Federal railway building will also
be the beginning of the end of railway manage-
ment by six separate States ; but that can t be
helped. Nor is it very easy to see why anybody
should desire to help it.
* * * *
Meanwhile, the first transcontinental line (that
to Port Darwin) and the second (the one to
Kalgoorlie and Perth) are calling for immediate
attention, and whatever may happen to the
parish spirit in the future, it is very much alive
in the immediate present. The struggle to sacri-
fice defence to sheep is evidently not ended yet.
Queensland is obsessed with the idea that it has
no future at all, that its railways will never go
any further out, and that if the transcontinental
line—the only one there will ever be, Australia,
according to the parish Jeremiahs, having no
future either —doesn’t come round in a great scram-
bling circle to connect the local railways where
they stand now, the whole State may as well go
down to the mud flats of Pinkenba, and there
solemnly cut its throat in the presence of a J-P-
-and two Commissioners of Affidavits. In the
struggle to bring the-only-railway-that-there-will-
ever-be around so that it will be agreeable to
everybody there will be a powerful effort to put
into it about enough metal for two railways. And
possibly by the time Queensland definitely fails
to drag the Adelaide-Port Darwin into its own
western country history will have already moved
on past the date when that State, under better
auspices, might, in the natural order of things,
have had its own western railway —the thud
transcontinental—well under way. In this con-
nection there is an ancient text which it is well
for the present Labor Government to remember :
“ What shall it profit a man if he shall gain the
whole world and lose his own soul ? ” The political
version of that is naturally: “What shall it
profit a man if he gets all the democratic
legislation that is going, and then lu® 6 ®, ' IIS
country and himself and everything else? ” Even
the best Arbitration Act ever devised is of no
value to a person when the Japanese have buried
him. Up to now the strategic element has never
been considered in Australian railway-building.
It has never been assumed that we would ever
want to send soldiers anywhere, or that we would
ever have any real soldiers to send anywhere.
The adoption of compulsory military training is
an acknowledgment that times have changed,
and it is essential to keep on remembering
how much they have changed. Nowadays
national safety must be considered as well
as sheep, and however handy the eastern
deviation would be as a means for shifting the
jumbuck of Middle Queensland to fresh grass, the
jumbuck is no longer the beginning and end of
all things.
ONE THING AND ANOTHER.
“ Henry Horsecollar writes about war
In a casual sort of fashion I noted the other day that the
Permanent Artillery force in N.S.VV. was ‘2O men short of
its proper complement, while that of Victoria was 1 5
short. It was mentioned that the latter State had re-
cently engaged two recruits to help fill the gap, hut as
one of them failed to turn up and hadn’t been seen since,
it really only secured one recruit, and one recruit isn’t
very much in a whole army. He hardly suggests the
gigantic operations of Alexander the Great, or Darius
the son of Gushtasp, nor even the doings of those raving
Spanish madmen who used to go forth from 12 to
100 strong to attack an empire, and who thought it
a fair thing if there were 25 of them and a dog against
20,000 Indians. They made a few recruits go a long way,
did the old Cortez and Pizarro push, but even they
didn’t enthuse much over a reinforcement of one indivi-
dual. It was further stated that the Victorian Per-
manent Force put up a stupendous effort some time ago
and gathered in 20 recruits, but as 12 of them promptly
resigned the army wasn’t much “forrarder.” The daily
paper which mentioned these facts said nothing about
the luck of N.S. W. in the recruiting line ; but unless it
had enlisted a cat it could hardly have done worse
than its neighbor. There was a further allusion to
the fact that there are now in the Common-
wealth 10,255 senior cadets or eldest armed infants, 334
mounted defensible infants with weapons, and 24,216
junior military infants. In Westralia, it seems, there
are seven schools furnished with racks for holding the
cadets’ toy rifles. A pair of small vessels are on their
way out here to serve as the nucleus of the Australian
fleet, and some more small craft are arriving in cases and
will he put together locally. It is announced that if they
are not put together in N.S.W. another wrong will have
been committed upon that State. The training of a
citizen soldiery is to commence next year or therea-
bouts, and it is believed that very soon some 80,060
men will have learned the rudiments or goose-sttp of
their business. More will follow till, in the slow pro-
cession of years, there will be some hundreds of thou-
sands of men who have put in a certain number of
days training, and acquired a certain amount, quantity
or heap of goose-step. And one little rifle factory is
starting, or nearly starting, or going to start. In addi-
tion to all this, tlie row about the naval base is still in
full blast. It is all very thrilling, and I feel with pride
that my native land is doing something at last. And
yet
* * * *
Then I picked up again the story of the last great war,
which was also the first great war fought at our doors.
It was the more interesting because the winner, even
though he oidy won by the skin of his teeth, was
that same brownish race which, openly and un-
disguisedly, looks upon Australia, Java, the Philip-
pines and the East Indies generally as its natu-
ral heritage, the hegemony of the Pacific as its
natural right, and the leadership of Asia as its natural
destiny. It was a strange sort of war, in which the
bumptious invader had to bring all his forces along by
sea even as he would have to do if the theatre of strife
was North Queensland or the Territory, and the bulk of
the defending army had to be conveyed to the scene by
land over a distance twice or three times that which
intervenes between Melbourne and Cooktown. There
was a scrambling series of naval encounters in which the
white man —ten times better prepared than Australia,
vet ten times too ill-prepared—saw his ironclads dissolve
like salt. He left behind him only a memory of useless
blundering and wonderful heroism, and the inspiring
final command of a doomed Admiral: “ Be brave, men ;
it is our last battle.” But it was the story of the land
fighting which especially interested me, when I recalled
the amazing luck of the Victorian Artillery in capturing
that fresh recruit, and when I looked back on the stun-
ning fact that there were in Westralia —a State contain-
ing- 900,000 square miles of country —seven schools with
racks to contain the cadets’ rifles. The fact that the
Japanese threw away the lives of 20,000 soldiers in one
single act while trying to storm Port Arthur was
a mere episode in the struggle. The battle of
Ch’iu-lien-oheng, though it lasted for some days
and spread over an area about as large as from
Sydney to Penrith, was also little more than an episode.
After that there were casual encounters about half the
size of the battle of Waterloo, followed by' the struggle at
Liao-yang, where for a week or more the biggest force
that had assembled in one place for 1400 years fought
with a wild disregard for consequences that has seldom
been equalled. If the whole male population of the two
Boer States, and every Imperial soldier that went
to South Africa during the Boer war had met on
one battlefield, and fought there for almost as long
a time as was occupied by all their battles put
together, it would have been the duplicate of Liao-
yang. In that one unpleasantness it is alleged that
more ammunition was expended than during the
whole Franco-German war, and it is also alleged that in
one single day, when the argument was at its height,
more ammunition was used than in the whole of the
Russo-Turkish war—all the battles, and the two great
sieges of Plevna and Kars thrown in. Sha-ho, where the
Russians were the assailants, was a comparatively moder-
ate disagreement lasting about nine days. Mukden was
a 14 days’ battle fought to the bitter end by the best part
of a million men, over a shifting line about as long as
from Sydney to Picton, and whether the losses were
50,000 or 100,000 or more on each side no man really
knows to this day. Reading these things over again, I
felt glad—darned glad—that the Victorian Artillery is
endeavoring to keep up its normal strength, whatever
that may be.
Nowadays it seems that the white nation which hap-
pens to live alongside the ambitious brown community of
Japan has to do things on a big scale, and requires to
keep its head. In all these stupendous proceedings—
proceedings which made Wellington and Blucher look
like ants, and the old one-day or half-day style of battle
appear like an infant skirmish in knickerbockers—a really
wonderful feature was the manner in which the defeated
army refused to be flurried. The old tradition, accord-
ing to which the losers naturally break their ranks, and
run for their lives, and scatter all over the landscape,
seems to have died out. Armies which do that sort of
thing are of little use as affairs go in this long-range,
high-explosive Twentieth Century. It is mentioned
casually that a Russian regiment at Mukden lost 1800
men out of 2400, and all its officers save two, yet it only
retreated on receiving the order to do so, and then it
stepped out tidily and without serious loss of discipline.
There were villages where the dead of the two armies lay
in heaps, their hands on each other’s throats, and their
bayonets in each other’s breasts —it was where the
Scrum of the Gods had been a little more intense than
usual for it was there that the Muscovites bad tried to
block Nooi’s great flank movement —and, even in places
like this, discipline didn’t get utterly lost. Out ot the
ruin—out of the smoke and flame of 25 burning
towns—the defeated army extricated itself some-
how, and was still an army. It is admitted
on all sides that superior generalship, arms and discip-
line carried the Japanese through. Yet the Russian
shooting was so good that there was at least one story ot
a Japanese regiment which lost all its officers save a. coi-
poral ; and the generalship which always rescued the
defeated force out of the trap wasn't the feeble kind
which led to the surrenders of Mets and Sedan m left)!
and the discipline w hich enabled a beaten host to retreat
in good order after upheavals like Liao-yang and Sha-no
w’as not to be regarded with utter contempt.
•: * * *
Apparently, war is going to be a high-pressure business
around the Pacific—nothing at all like the sort of small,
splashy conflict that Fitchktt used to describe, -these
two combatants put little nations in the field. *et as
already mentioned, one w'as fighting a few hundred mile-
from his main resources, and the other a few thousand
miles. Presumably, if the war had been outside Tokio
or outside Moscow’, where one party at least could bring
its available forces to bear in earnest, these angry folk
would have really wakened up and done things. ine
prospect looks so serious that I am really darned glad
I can’t help mentioning it again—that the Victorian
artillery got the recruit it was looking for, and I sin-
cerely hope the N.S.W. force did the same. The white
man has got to be wide-awake these days, if his flag and
his empire, and his glory and his religion, and his w "?J e
civilisation are not to go down in the dust. And, while
on this subject, I notice with pleasure that Britain
is also waking to a sense of its military responsi-
bilities. Recently it was decided to test the value
of the Boy Scout as a messenger in war time, so relays
of him, in uniform, ran a long distance across country
carrying an important despatch. Other Boy Scouts,
representing the enemy, lay for the messengers. When a
Scout, suspected of having the document about him, was
captured, the enemy wasn’t allowed to shoot or imprison
him, and itw'as only allow ed a certain number of minutes
in which to search him. The despatch got to its destina-
tion hidden in a red herring, and it is now felt that there
are no flies—or at least no vultures—on the Boy Scout,
regarded as an appreciable force in war time. Yet if a
Russian in uniform had tried to run at full speed with a
red herring in his pocket over the disturbed area in Man-
churia, he might have made very little difference in the
result. Things were done on a large scale there, and a
Boy Scout and a red herring seem very small. On the
other hand, if Goliath had been sent with a whale under
his arm and the Mitchell Library concealed inside the
w hale—well, he might have been more m keeping with
the circumstances, but he would have been much too
conspicuous.
SOME N.S.W. LAW ABUSES WHICH NO
ROYAL COMMISSION IS GOING
TO SCOTCH.
W. A. Holman, N.S.W.’s new Attorney-General,
remarked, last week, that “tl#poorer sections of
the community have, at the present time, very
great difficulty in either learning their rights
under the civil law or of enforcing them ” ; also,
he said, “ the vast number of cases never go into
court because the parties prefer to suffer in silence
rather than incur the expenses of a trial.”
Holman’s idea of coping with the trouble
is to appoint the usual Royal Commission.
Ninety per cent, of Royal Commissions are notori-
ously time- and money-wasting superfluities ; and
a body of the sort that sat down to worry out
measures to make law more accessible in N.S.W.
would be wasting its time in a particularly futile
fashion. The Law’s delays and expensiveness m
the Ma State are due, not to the processes that
obtain, but rather to the archaic ways to which
the Courts cling, and to the outrageous license
that is permitted to counsel. In certain aspects
the Law’s habits constitute a standing joke for
those members of the public who happen, for the
moment, to be unaffected by them. In almost any
given breach-of-promise, divorce, libel, slander or
other civil suit, it is easy for the man on the tram
to make a fairly accurate estimate of the mone-
tary resources of the parties concerned by noting
the number of hours, days or weeks that their
court trouble lasts. In spinning out these suits
to the limit of the litigants’ financial tether, coun-
sel enjoy the hearty co-operation of the attorneys
who instruct them, of the gentlemen who appear
for the other side, and even, in a sense, of the
Judge. As long as the ready cash is forthcoming
for Costs, out-of-pocket expenses, refreshers
and so forth, the legal warriors may be con-
fidently relied on to continue producing more or
less immaterial witnesses, examining, cross-
examining and re-examining them, chaffing them,
“objecting” affably, or with an impressive show
of passion, to utterly irrelevant or unimportant
questions, and in general playing the legal game
for all it is worth to their bank accounts. Nor do
the bulk of the Judges ever make more than a
half-hearted attempt to check the scandalous
business. This the populace observes, and it is
shy of the law in consequence.
The cumbrous and absurd rules of evidence
likewise do a vast deal in the way of scaring
people off the pursuit of legal redress. In
1901 the Commercial Causes Act was put on the
N.S.W. Statute Book with the object of miti-
gating this evil. It empowers a Judge to confer
with the parties if they so desire—or with the
latter’s legal representatives —in certain classes
of cases, for the purpose of getting as quickly as
possibly at the pith of the matter. To this end the
Judge may call on each side to make formal ad-
mission of such facts as are not in dispute. Thus,
assuming the case was one in which a claim was
made in connection with cargo alleged to have
been damaged at sea, counsel would, under the
old system, have had to formally “prove” the
bill of lading, the shipment of the goods, the
signatures of the parties, possibly even the
signatures of persons resident abroad, and
a host of other matters that no one
dreamed of questioning. The Commercial Causes
Act enables the Judge to sweep away the whole
of this absurd top-hamper and get down, either
alone or with a jury, to a consideration of the one
basic point at issue. In England a similar Act
has been made frequent use of during the past
dozen years by such great commercial Judges as
Walton, Matthew and Collins, to the infinite
cheapening of litigation. But in N. S.
litigants rarely avail thmselves of the Act. In
the past decade there have been no more than two
or three cases under it. A likely reason is that
the vast majority of business men are ignorant of
its existence, and attorneys are, for obvious
reasons, none too anxious to enlighten them. As
a leading solicitor put it to the writer : “ Legis-
lation on those lines is right enough for litigants;
but where do we come in ? ”
* * * *
Before it becomes safe for men of moderate
means to appeal to the Civil Law of N.S.W., the
Judges will have to deal in a square-jawed manner
with nrocrastinating counsel. The habit of hold-
ing up cases by the introduction of jokes, anec-
dotes, chaff, electioneering and parliamentary
gags, and personal reminiscences should have
been jumped on long ago. The outrageous
old convention that barristers who take a
brief must be paid, even if they omit to turn up
in court, should be passed the death-stroke.
Indeed, if the equities were considered, there


would he some legal redress provided against
barristers who put their clients in a hole by
engaging infereutially to be on hand when re-
quired, and then staying away because something
better offers. Counsel’s custom of interminably
arguing questions of evidence—a practice whereby
causes are immeasurably spun out, with profit to
none but the arguers—clamors for the axe.
The Judges alone can scotch this evil. Nine-
tenths of the evidence whose admissibility is so
strenuously advocated or opposed by counsel,
doesn’t matter a straw one way or the other, and
everyone in court, with the possible exception of
the victim who is paying through the nose for the
farcical uproar, knows it. In palpable cases of
this sort the Judge should step in, stifle the back-
talk of the legal persons, and either allow' or dis-
allow the futile testimony out of hand. Leading
questions ought to be more generally permitted
in the examination-in-chief of witnesses. Many
thousands of hours —at a good round sum in
guineas per hour—are lost each year, owing to
counsels’ intensely gradual steering of dunder-
headed witnesses up to points w'here business
really begins, by a system of interrogation which
is as unnecessary as it is elaborate and puzzling.
There are two big legal reforms which N.S.W.
needs so badly that no Royal Commission is re-
quired to report on them. One is the institution
of a Court of Criminal Appeal; the other the
granting to dissatisfied litigants of the right to go
direct to the High Court, without the expensive
gamble of a previous struggle before N.S.W.’s
Full Court. For the rest, the terrifying expense
and so forth that disfigure the Courts is less a
question of measures or processes than of methods
and men. Strong Judges are needed more than
new enactments; and counsel with an ambition
to do the work they are lavishly paid for, on other
than what anti-Laborites know as “ the Govern-
ment stroke” principle.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Isle of Jim-Jams.
Nkws of a case of proposed child-abandonment
comes from Tasmania. The author of it is Ewing,
M. H. A., one time a Tory Senator. Ewing has an
idea that Unification is coming, so he proposes that
Tasmania, with a population of less than 200,000,
should borrow another £1,500,000 in one big act
(which would be the same as if N.S.W. suddenly
borrowed about £12,000,000), and spend the money
on roads and bridges, and then, in his own words,
“we can hand the baby to the Unificationists.”
If the Commonwealth happens to be taking
over the State debts just about the time the
baby is ready, it will be all right; .if it isn’t
doing anything of the sort, Ewing’s idea,
possibly, is that his little State should serve
notice on the Commonwealth that it isn’t going
to meet its interest bill any longer, and ask the
Commonwealth in a cold, hard tone of voice what
it proposes doing about the matter. The theory
is startling, yet it has many good points
about it. It is fitting that it should be
a Tory who proposed such a thing, and the
fact ought to be remembered. If there is to be
any repudiation in Australasia it would be espe-
cially advisable that the most Tory of all the States
should do the deed. And so long as it was a Tory
State and a Tory Government that carried the dis-
grace of it, there wouldn’t be unmixed regret at
the deed being done. It would make the trans-
fer of the State debts to the Commonwealth
an immediate necessity. It would force the
Commonwealth to take control of future borrow-
ings lest some Tory should break out again in the
same place. And it would give the Unifica-
tion and Reconstruction movement a great
impetus. So far, Premier Lewis hasn’t
decided to rush things to the extent that
Ewing suggests, but he does propose to borrow
and spend £440,000 this year, which would be
about equal to £4,000,000 in N.S.W. For some years
prior to 1907 the Apple Isle spent on an average
£150,000 of Cohen’s money. Then thefigureleaped
to £350,000 ; and now it goes up £90,000 more.
Tassy’s borrowing policy, too, is a weirdly futile
business. No other State has so little in the way
of reproductive works to show for its loans, or
has used so much of its borrowed money for small
casual jobs and repairs—for the soling and heeling
of the public pants and the putting of a new
roof on the national boots. These are some recent
figures:—
Unproductive Unproductive
Debt. Debt per Head.
N. S. Wales .. ..£7,894,899 .. £4 19 0
Victoria .. .. 8,769,034 .. 219 0
Queensland .. .. 7,087,514 .. 12 17 0
S. Australia .. .. 5,404.372 ..' 13 5 0
W. Australia,. .. 3,320,081 .. 12 8 0
Tasmania .. .. 4,535,480 .. 24 8 0
A State with such a shocking record of Tory
finance might almost as well do what Ewing pro-
poses and put itself out of its misery. Thus it
would show, the absolutely essential character of
the Unification and Reconstruction policy. The
Commonwealth would take it over and pole-axe
its dreadful Upper House, either with or without
chloroform. The Isle would get a new and per-
fectly democratic Legislature of one House. Its
finances would be put straight, and its big landlords
would be chased down a steep place into political
oblivion. Its old Dodery legislators, out of poli-
tics for good, would sit gibbering in their clubs
about the good times that were gone and the
horrors that were coming. And a colossal figure
to represent Insolvency could be erected at
the entrance to the Derwent, even as the statue
of Liberty adorns the front gate of the United
States—a figure in a frock coat and helltopper,
that being the usual garb of Toryism, with a snake
sticking out of its pocket to suggest financial
intoxication, and a burning IO U in its hand to
indicate the light of a better day to come. This
suggestion is open to amendment, but as a rough
outline the writer respectfully submits that there
are features about it.

“The Times” on the Cornstalk Congestion
Policy.
The fame of the N.S.W. railway system has
spread even to far-off England, and the London
Times has heard of it, and decorated the subject
with a map and a leading article. Some of the.
paper’s observations are interesting—these, for
instance :
The railway problem necessarily affects the Common-
wealth as a whole, so long as Australia remains without
a single through trunk line from north to south, or from
east to west. It also affects every separate State in
greater or less degree ; but there is no single State where
the railway question is so immediate and urgent as in
New South Wales. Mr. Wade, the Premier of New South
Wales, is about to go to the polls upon a programme in
which railway reform and construction occupy a fore-
most place. He proposes gradually to expend an enor-
mous sum for these purposes. He contemplates the
duplication of main lines, the provision of further facili-
ties at Sydney, the creation of cross-connections, and—a
good deal more vaguely and tentatively—the opening up
of neglected ports along the coast of his State. ...
It is often said that the Australian railway system has
not been constructed upon any coherent and well-con-
sidered plan. That is certainly not true of the railways
of New South Wales. A glance at the useful map which
accompanies our correspondent’s letter will show that
the New South Wales railways have been made with one
fatally clear and deliberate purpose, the expansion of
Sydney to the largest possible proportions. Along the
whole coast of the State the railway emerges on the sea
at only one other point. Sydney Harbor has been at
once the pride and the bane of New South Wales. To
serve the interests of the capital and its harbor, the wel-
fare of the interior of the State has been persistently
sacrificed. All possible traffic has been diverted to
Sydney, while such useful harbors as Twofold Bay,
Jervis Bay, Port Stephens and Coff’s Harbor have
been wilfully neglected. It is not as though Syd-
ney Harbor were well placed for the handling of
the seaborne trade of the State. Magnificent and almost
incomparable though its faculties are, it remains an inlet
in the midst of mountains. The gradients approaching
it involve costly haulage, and, as our map indicates, a
long' distance has to be traversed before the fertile areas
of New South Wales are reached. .. . The undue worship
of the fetich of Sydney has contributed to produce de-
plorable results. ... A great deal of stress seems to be
laid upon the undoubtedly urgent necessity for the dupli-
cation of existing main lines, and there is no hesitation
about the creation of further facilities in and near Syd-
ney ; but the construction of cross-connexions is not
pressed with quite the same enthusiasm, and the disre-
garded out-ports, which might be easily linked up with
fei tile agricultural districts, appear to be an extremelv
secondary consideration. Sydney, in short, still dominates
the situation, and is very nearly as great an incubus as
ever. No one who examines the map, and notes the gaps,
duly marked, which have been deliberatelv left to draw
trade to Sydney, can doubt that New South Wales will
never rise to the fulness of its destinies until it is able lo
discard even State preoccupations in the matter of rail-
"; a -7. constructio »- Those ominous gaps are the most
vivid argument in favor of the Federal principle that we
have 4ver seen ; and they would have been stronglv rein-
forced, could we have illustrated the lamentable breaks
of gauge on the northern and southern boundaries of
New South Wales. There could be no more instructive
contrast than to compare the map of Argentina, grid-
ironed with well-devised railways, with the map we
publish to-day.
A New Phase of the Centralisation Lunacy.
Thk “Liberal ’’craze for dragging everything to
Sydney applies, seemingly, even to disease.
Nine months ago, a Sydney X-rays specialist sug-
gested to a member of the Wade Government
the ad visability of starting a State Radium Bank
in N.S. W. Health-officer Ashburton Thompson
endorsed the scheme. But the Wade Ministry
objected to it as “Socialistic”; so Ashburton
Thompson’s favorable report was shelved, and the
subject dropped. Early last June the Telegraph
got wind of the idea, reckoned there was a “scoop ”
in it, and set out in chase of subscriptions.
Ihe public, as is its habit tnese days, regarded
the Telegraph’s request for cash with a chill and
unresponsive eye. Things looked black for the
Radium Fund, when the ambition to do some-
thing for the poor and miserable invaded the
kindly heart of Miss Nellie Stewart. She
organised an amazingly successful matinee in aid
of the cause. Moneyed people got interested;
the CMsh rolled in. Iwo thousMnd three hundred
sterling was collected; the Wade Government
was prevailed on to promise a £ for £ subsidy, and
the total proceeds were handed over to the Sydney
Hospital. A medical man is proceeding to Europe
to buy £4600 worth of radium, and within a few
months he will be back with the goods. Now, a
thing the Vast majority of those who subscribed
the initial £2300 worth of radium-money didn’t
understand, and which the vast majority of
N.S.W. taxpayers who are to find the second
£2300 will most certainly resent, is that, under
the present arrangement, every cancer patient
who wants radium treatment, whether he lives at
distant Barringun or adjacent Balmain, will be
forced to go to the Sydney Hospital to get it.
Consider the case of a cancer-sinitten shearer,
cocky-farmer or other indigent citizen whose home
is in the neighborhood of Bourke or Young.
He must journey, at his own expense, to Sydney.
When he arrives he will have to find the money
for his board and lodging and so forth—the cancer
cases in which radium is of use being practically,
never the sort that entitle the sufferer to become
a hospital in-patient. For many weeks he will
need to visit the hospital daily, or at short, fixed
intervals, and undergo a treatment lasting from
one to three hours. That will debar him from
getting or holding a job while he is in Sydney.
And, even after he is discharged, his troubles will
not be ended. According, to Louis Wickham,
the world’s greatest authority on radium (British
Medical Journal, 18/12/’O9)
We can only assure the. patient of a cure on condition
that (even after the initial treatment) he binds himself to
come once a month for a long period to see the doctor
and enable the latter to catch the first trace of any
relapse. •
That is to say, for 18 months or more the afflicted
Wayback will ka"e to be continually dodging
between his distant home and Sydney. The
odds are that few of him will attempt the des-
perate task, and that thousands of cancer-subjects,
whose cases might have been ameliorated or
cured by radium, will never avail them-
selves of it. The Wade Government, had it con-
sidered the interests of N.S.W. instead of the con-
gestion of Sydney, was offered two alternatives.
It might have invested five or ten thousand pounds
in a Radium Bank, which if run on the lines of
the Paris Banque du Radium—where radium is
hired out to hospitals and private practitioners at
a fixed rate—might have been as self-supporting
as the State railways or tramways. Or, better
still, it might have gone the entire humanitarian
animal, and used the £lO,OOO it recently allocated
for the construction of an absurd new ballroom at
the State Governor’s residence, to purchase a
radium supply and loan it out to hospitals, free,
as required, for the benefit of the genuine poor all
over the State. The position at present is that
the radium for which N.S.W. has found the
money will be of use, (1) to Sydney and suburban
cancer patients, (2) to the few up-country sufferers
who can afford a Jong series of trips to Sydney, (3)
to those Sydney residents who are financially
benefited by the incursion of the afflicted ones.
And it will be of use to no one else.

Cremation.
One of the few commendable acts of N.S.W. ’s
ex-Premier Wade was to promise a grant for the
construction of a crematorium—not a place in
which to burn the papers relating to the trial of
Petek Bowling, but an appliance for the decent
and sanitary obliteration of the dead. It is to be
hoped that his successor will see his way to honor
Wade’s p.n. Cremation is not only a system
which calls for encouragement; it is one which
ought to be made compulsory. Away far back in
pre-Christian times two of the features of decent
and enlightened pagan society were the crema-
torium and the bath. The bes't kind of heathen
believed in washing carefully while he was alive
and in being carefully burned when he had
no longer any use for himself. Then the new
religion came along, and its excited votaries made
hash of the clean old ways. They preached that
the body was a vile, ordinary, mi-account thing
compared with the soul—that it was something of
absolutely no importance—and to emphasise this
view they practically left off washing it. They left
off so utterly that the thousands and thousands of
public baths, which decorated some of the old
pagan cities, vanished, in time, almost utterly. It
is an unromantic fact, but Meucia in “ The Sign
of the Cross,” was probably an exceedingly insani-
tary girl, and so were the other members of her
sect; in fact, the lion in the Colisseum often had
to hold his nose with one paw while killing his
Christian with the other. But these same
Christians had a fixed idea that the body,
which was of no account now, would become of
great value later on. It was to appear again in im-
perishable form and complete repair; therefore,
instead of burning it, they laid it carefully in the
earth to be ready for future use. With strange
inconsistency they seemed to believe that, although
the Almighty could raise it again after it had
become a mere handful of dust by reason of slow
decay, He couldn’t raise it again if it became a
similar handful of dust through exposure for a few
minutes to the action of fire. So cremation went
out of fashion, and the dead were put not very far
underground, to pollute the water supply and pre-
serve the germs of disease. In the really strenu-
ous days of early Christianity these things didn’t
matter ; it was better to be dead than alive;
so it didn’t signify if the water was polluted.
Besides, the body, while living, was a thing to be
contemned and despitefully treated, and the pour-
ing of impure fluids into it was a mere episode
amid its troubled experiences. But after all,
burial failed of its chief purpose. Among the
original believers the end of the world was ex-
pected any day; in fact, it was a common impres-
sion that the first generation of Christians would
live to see it. It was calculated that, as there
was so short a time to wait, the dead would still
be found at the original address when the trumpet
blew. But centuries rolled on. Graveyards
filled and were abandoned and closed, “ and
then, because it was impossible to re-
serve indefinite areas of thickly peopled
lands for the tenancy of the very ancient dead,
they were often ploughed up and the bones and
4.11 st were effectually dispersed. Thus the saints
ceased to be find able at the old address. Then,
as the old, strenuous faith grew cold, the human
race re-discovered the almost lost art of washing,
and in a new passion for sanitation it began to
look askance at the cemetery. The,chief reason
for that institution vanished when the end of the
world was indefinitely postponed, and now things
are moving slowly towards its abolition. It is a
pity they are not moving much more rapidly ;
but the mournful burial place has become a habit
of at least 1875 years standing, and habits of that
age are not easily shaken off.

Bull's Army.
“ The Old Soldier ”; A cable message men-
tioned last Friday that Lieutenant Alan Sutor,
R- A-> had been removed from the army, the King
having “no further occasion for his services.”
His offence was that lie harshly criticised the
British army. How badly the army needed criti-
cism is indicated by French and German expert
reports that have been published of late. Thus a
German staff-officer with a European reputation:
The British army does not compare "with the French
German, Austrian or Japanese armies. By its present
training the British army is not fit for a conflict with a
continental army.
The raw material for an army is eminently good, and
would yield to no other in the world. But the com-
mandeis have lio idea'of handling troops in masses, and
the generals are unsound in their tactics.
Handling troops in masses” is a mechanical
business which must be learned by practice.
No British General ever sees—even at Aider-
shot, or an Indian camp* of exercise—
what a French or German soldier would call
“troops in masses.” The S’African Boer-chase
taught the British leaders nothing that is going
to be of service to them in a European war. The
German is not alone in his depreciation of the
British troops. The French General, Langlois
who has been inspecting Haldane’s territorial
army, has published a book, “The English Army
in European Conflict.” He says : J
England requires : (1) An army responsible for the pro-
tection of the colonies, to furnish them with garrisons
and supply them with men and material; (2) territorial
army, a force which would not be called on to leave the
country ; (3) national army, prepared to take the field in
Europe. England has shirked the construction of the
third army, which would involve the necessity of a con
siderable increase of the army estimates. Thus it is to
day that the regular army of the United Kingdom is
really the colonial army, and is forced to undertake the
double duty not only of protecting the colonies, but of
supporting the allies of England on the Continent in case
of need.
General Langlois was specially borrowed by the
War Office toinspectand report on Haldane’s Ter-
ritorials. He was feted and flattered, yet in the
end, he had to say that, without the support of regu-
lar troops, the Territorials would go down before a
iiiuropean invader. He says much the same to
his own countrymen in his book, and he sums up
the whole by giving his opinion, and also appar-
ently the opinion of the French Army Staff, on
the value of the Entente to France :
(1) The Entente between England and France is usefu.l
to the latter provided that the English expeditionary
force can be landed on the Continent on the 15th day at
the latest. Unless England is prepared to do this she
will lie too late unless the decisive battle should be pro-
longed beyond the expectations of the French strategists.
(A the Entente is even more useful to England than
to trance; for it protects England from any serious
danger on the side of Germany.
It is hard to say whether the French or German
opinion is the. less flattering to the British army.
E/ither way it makes cheerless reading for Aus-
tialians, have pledged themselves in some
' ague way to model their land-defence organisa-
tion on British principles.

P.M.” defines Labor-leader Prendergast’s
attitude on the Sunday-shows question :
O. K. s ’ attack (B. 27/10/’10) on Labor-leader Pren-
dkrgast on the Sunday Entertainments question shows a
bat-ej ed view of the surrounding circumstances. When
PitEN DERG AST was asked to present a petition of 10,500
signatures against Murray’s Bill, he immediately com-
Plied and he never said that “any interference with
tne Gay of Rest ’ ought to be deprecated by the working-
man. What he advocates is one and a-half days’ rest a
week, universal as far as practicable. What he protests
against is that some of those who have secured
this on Saturday afternoon and Sunday are will-
ing' to see the rule broken in order that they
may be provided with additional amusement, involv-
mg additional Sunday labor. “0.K.” talks foolishly
when he applies the “ logical conclusion ” arguments.
Ihe matter of Sunday labor is one for reasonable adjust-
ment, so that it will be kept at a rational minimum. He
also argues vainly w'hen he talks about public conveni-
ence. According to that argument, everything con-
venient to the people on a week-day would have to be
kept going on a Sunday, and so the boon would be lost
to all. In the particular case of these Sunday entertain-
ments, which are run entirely for the profit of certain
theatrical proprietors, those who have inquired know
that the good employers (from a Trades Union point of
view) do not open. The hands employed are mostly
against Sunday entertainments. They want their
leisure the same as ever.vliod3’ else, and are content with
BIX ‘ a >s work. Prkndkrgast knows this, and “0.K.”
should have been in the way of finding it out before writing
so cocksurely. Theatre legislation in Victoria is de-
plorable, inasmuch as it permits a man without a license
toi his house to open on Sunday, while it compels a
licensed house to keep closed. There is no compulsion to
license either. Admittedly, the churches are supporting
l lie Murray Bill all the way. But that is no sufficient
reason why the Labor party should not also support it as
far as it fits in with the party’s aspirations. Finally,
1 RENDERGAST is not foolish enough to support any legis-
lation which would place the permission to hold Sunday
public meetings—political or otherwise—in the hands o‘f
possible opponents ; nor is lie in any sense opposed io any
form of mutual entertainment. What he is absolutely
opposed to is the private-profit-making Sunday enter-
tainment.
A Melbourne man moans his grievance against
the committee of Henley-on-Yarra. The com-
plainant’s sad story is that on the afternoon of
“ Henley ” he paid Bs. for the admission of him-
self and family to the illegally-reserved space on
the south bank of the Yarra, with the intention of
leaving his family to picnic there till the evening,
when the mud-barge houseboats are illuminated
and a few fireworks are provided by the manage-
ment. But after the regatta in the afternoon
complainant’s family and all other people were
ejected from the reserve, and the poor man had
to pay another ss. to pass his family back to the
place whence it came. Legally, it seems, he was
an outraged citizen in being wired-off by an asso-
ciation of johnnies from any portion of the river
bank, the outrage being; aggravated by the fact
that some decorative bits of “public gardens 99
were included in the Henley reserve this year.
Socially, however, he and hundreds of others
deserved all the extortion they got. They didn’t
pay for a view of the regatta in the afternoon, or
for the best possible sight of the illuminations in
the evening. These advantages are obtainable
by the crowds who have free access to the
opposite bank of the Yarra. Admittedly, the
City Council has no legal right to grant the privi-
lege of enclosing either bank for one day, or for
one hour, to a mysterious party of Toorakians
who run the Henley show. Nominally the coun-
cil does it in the interests of boat-racing 1 , and on
the understanding that the profits are to be
expended by the council in beautifying the public
ground thereabouts. No luminous statement of
accounts is, however, ever issued. The whole
business is an extraordinary bluff, wrapped in
mystery. But it is a bluff which owes all its
success to social snobbery. The people who pay
for admission to the illegal reserve go there in the
afternoon to behold one another in their best
clothes, and in the evening thev go there to be
preserved from contact with the non-paying pub-
lic. Seeing, therefore, that the imposition is
suffered only by such as deliberately seek it, with
a full knowledge of the fact that they have
nothing but “social selectness ” to gain by choos-
ing the reserved side of the river, they can’t
fairly complain of being turned cut whenever the
management; feels so disposed, seeing that the
oftener the reserve is cleared, and the higher the
tar.ff is made, the more they will get of the snob-
bish “selectness” that they pay for.

“ 0. ” WRITES
When a person or paper or party is a very poor liar it
is almost as well to give up lying and tell the truth, or
else drop the subject. The N.S. W. “ Liberal ” partv and
its press representatives lied shamelessly and horribly
during the recent electoral campaign ; but they didn’t do
it plausibly It was crude, clumsy work, like 'the statue
which an aboriginal hacks out with a stone axe. Here
for instance, is a specimen of the naval or marine or salt!
horse lie as told in Sydney Telegraph ;
If the Labor party should get into power as a result
of the present election in New South Wales, Victorians
are confident that they will be able to exercise sufficient
pressure upon the Federal Government to squeeze it into
making Hobson’s Bay the headquarters of the fleet In
uv ? aBe > the station in Sydney could, of course be
abolished. A warship might occasionally be seen in Port
Jackson, probably during Show time, for a couple of
days, but all the advantages which Sydney now derives
from the Imperial squadron, with the employment that
it furnishes m supplies, in docking and in other wavs
would naturally pass to Melbourne.” J ’
The State Government, whether Labor or “Liberal ”
has no control at all over naval matters ; so the proposi-
tion that the continuance of Wade in office would keep
the Imperial squadron in Sydney harbor, while the up-
rising of McGowen would cause that squadron to be lost
was a pretty mean way of cadging for votes. The intima!
tiori that Victorians are confident”is an obvious lie as
the Telegraph couldn’t possibly have time to go round
and ask all Victorians whether they were confident or
not. And the statement that the advantages of “ the
Imperial squadron ” will “ naturally pass to Melbourne "
is fairly outrageous in view of the fact that the
same paper has announced that the local head-
quarters of that same squadron are to be at Auckland
Even a dangerously insane paper shouldn’t try to pass
on the public the yarn that a composite sea force niade
up of Maoriland ships and a few British vessels from the
Chinese station, is liable to be posted at Melbourne be
cause of the success of the N.S.W. State Labor party It
is more likely to be sent to Timbuctoo because of the
great rise in the price of slop clothes on the Goodwin
In Sweeping Curves.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For some curious reason it is considered necessary in
Tory quarters to bring the very essential transcontinental
railway round by a long eastern circumbendibus.—
Bulletin (ZO/lOI’ 10).
The Tory person loves to dream
Of corkscrew railways curling round,
Where trains forlorn may gently steam
In circles over barren ground.
He loves to picture in his mind
A Puffing Billy, crawling slow,
Some outback bales of wool to find
By pads where camels come and go.
He wants to see the North express
Start out from Melbourne once a week,
And trail through every wilderness
’Twixt Esperanoe and Cooper’s Creek ;
And, taking Perth upon the way,
With Parramatta and Geelong,
Come round by Kimberley and Hay
To Alice Springs and Gerringong.
Passing Cape Otway on the right,
’Twould reach Port Darwin by-and-bye,
Keeping the South Coast well in sight,
And Brisbane always in its eye ;
And not forgetting Sydney town,
Nor Adelaide, nor Warrnambool,
’Twould run by grey Gulf waters down,
And up by Kosciusko cool.
Thus, when the footprints of the foe
Our northern valleys had debased,
Eor eighteen sudden weeks or so
Our troops would wander round in haste ;
And, waiting for our counter-stroke,
Each Pagan face would wreathe in smiles
To know our wrath and battle-smoke
Had gone on tour 12,000 miles.
N.S.W.
PAT O'MAORI.

According to law, every Federal public servant
aged 21 or over, and with three years’ service, is
entitled to a minimum salary of £llO a year. It is
now proposed to grant the £llO minimum whether
the recipient has three years’ service or not,
which proposal may be reasonable enough
if the thing was done by an amendment
of the law—and a Bill of one clause would cover
all the necessary amendment. But the active—
not to say gymnastic—mind of Attorney-
General Hughes scorns the plain and simple
method. He proposes to leave the law as
it stands—for the present, anyway—and skip
airily round it by calling the extra payment
•‘bonus.” And by this mental agility Hughes
establishes a precedent by which every future
Government may override the decisions of the
Public Service Commissioner and proceed to “ be
good to its friends” by calling the thing “bonus.”
In N.S.W. State politics Attorney-General Hol-
man, who is in some ways the mental duplicate of
Hughes, has also been distinguishing himself. Two
months ago a Political Labor League secretary was
convicted of inciting a young lady to forge two
signatures to applications for enrolment as voters.
He didn’t even do his own forgery, but pushed
someone else into doing it. The Law said it was
an exceedingly bad case—and fined him £lO, with
355. Costs. Attorney-General Holman has now
reduced the penalty to 2s. fine and 355. Costs,
and after that the less Holman, at all events, has
to say about Wade’s action in the McKye case
the better. Even at the worst, Wade only cut
down McKye’s fine by one-half. These are
the sort of things that supply ammunition
to the enemy. The kind of member who
is most likely to prove the evil genius of the
Labor party is the voluble, fairly capable, shifty-
minded lawyer, with his clean shave and his fatal
ingenuity. That remark has been made before,
but it is plainly time to make it again.
