The S. M. Herald projected this dead cat at the
Wade Government as soon as it was defunct: —
Should the McGowen Government realise its responsi-
bility, the Minister in charge of the Lands Department
has plenty of scope for valuable work. If he can place
the Department in the position it is rightly entitled to
occupy, he will earn the gratitude of the producers of
the State. There is room for improvement, a necessity
for getting out of the rut, and breaking irksome bonds
of red tape, and, above all, extending the work of the
Department. . . . There has been no explanation of the
reason for fixing salaries so low as, for instance, £2OO per
year for a potato expert, an educated, scientific and prac-
tical man, able to instruct growers. It is certain that
the Department, to do good work, must have the aid of
first-class men, and to secure them must pay first-class
salaries.
Put otherwise, the Wade Government, so far as
its Lands administration was concerned,
(1) Failed to “ earn the gratitude of producers.”
(2) Was bogged in “ a rut.”
(3) Was leg-ironed by red tape.
(4) Sweated its employees.
It is all true, doubtless, but it is amazingly dif-
ferent from what the same paper was saying a
month ago.
♦
At last George Reid has attended something
besides a banquet. It was a meeting held in
London to consider the question of adopting a
standard trade mark for use on all goods manu-
factured in the British Empire. So far as can be
made out from the meagre information to hand (if
the item were about cricket it would have been
twice as large), the Empire Trade Mark is to be
somewhat similar in effect to the Commonwealth
Workers’ Mark, which was included in the Com-
monwealth Trade Mark Act of 1905, but was
hurriedly thrown out, at the instance of the
breweries, as being ultra, vires. This Empire
Trade Mark is to be governed by the British
Board of Trade; and, according to present infor-
mation, the Board will issue certificates to the
firms that employ white labor only. The pur-
chaser of any goods will know from the mark on
the wrapper that the article is not the result of
foreign labor, and was manufactured within the
British Empire. It won’t specify the labor con-
ditions under which the goods were produced, but
it will be something to know that it was white
labor, anyhow.
“W.C.M.”: The following item is starred in
Dalgety’s Review
Mr. William Officer, of Zara, Deniliquin (N.S.W.), has
sold the following stud sheep to South African buyers
during the present season :
To the Orange Kiver Colony: 18 stud rams, from
lOgns. to 90gns.; 116 stud ewes, from 3gns. to 20gns.
To the Transvaal Government: 1 stud ram.
To various buyers in the Cape and Orange River
colonies, Natal and the Transvaal: 54 stud rams, to
50gns.; 36 stud ewes, from sgns. to 75gns.
Total number of sheep sold, 325.
These extensive sales speak volumes for the reputation
of the Zara sheep in South Africa.
And they also speak volumes for the monumental
stupidity of Australian sheepbreeders. When
the question of importing stud angora goats and
stud ostriches from S’Africa was mooted,
S’African Governments temporised for a few
months, and used the time to rush through a
Bill forbidding, under heavy penalties, the expor-
tation of angora goats or of cock ostriches or
even of the eggs of the ostrich. S’Africa wanted
no competitor in the angora industry, and as little
as possible in the ostrich industry ; but the fool
Australian cheerfully sends away the best of his
sheep or horses or any other old thing that he may
possess, and thus adds another to the competitors
in the already-crowded markets of the world. It
is no use talking to the sheepbreeder ; like breeds
like to a certain extent, and the sheep is about
the silliest and at the same time most obstinate
animal on earth. But the Commonwealth
Government should step in and absolutely forbid
the exportation of the merino sheep, not in the
interests of the fool woolgrower, but in the in-
terests of the Australian sheep industry. The
habit of being a jolly good sort of a chap all
round and hang the expense is just as ruinous to
nations as it is to individuals.

“ Rooinek,” about some other people’s railways:
South Africa is rapidly acquiring the most up-to-date
Government railway service on earth. Its 7000 odd
miles of line are now under one management. All the
long-distance trains are corridors, and you can ramble
from the engine to the guard’s van. They are lit through-
out by electricity, and the lavatory accommodation is
very good. On the Cape-Jo'burg line coffee is served to the
sleepers first thing in the morning; then you can turn out,
have a shower-bath, dress and stroll along to the dining-
car for an excellent meal. There is a bar on the dining-
car, but you don’t necessarily have to go to it fora drink.
Afternoon tea is brought to your compartment without
your asking. From Jo’burg to Pretoria is only 45 miles,
but the 8 a.m., 1 p.m. and 6 p.m. trains have dining cars,
and serve tiptop meals on board. This seems like
carrying luxury too far ; but these trains are used daily
by hundreds of very busy men. The Cape-Jo’burg
trains have circulating libraries on board—a boon
and a blessing. The speed has improved tremendously.
You can run the 1011 miles from Jo’burg to Capetown in
35 hours, an average speed, including stoppages, of about
30 miles, notwithstanding that the travelling has to be
dead slow in parts, especially through the moun-
tains. In the Free State the trains purralongat between
50 and 60 miles an hour, and your liquor doesn’t spill out
of a brimming glass either. All this is done on a
3ft. 6in. gauge, and convinces me that that gauge
is good enough for any country. To change from
sundry broad gauges to one uniform narrow gauge is a
comparatively simple and inexpensive matter ; but to
change from narrow to broad is a Sheol of a job. No doubt
the inevitable fool will rise up and say that my remarks are
all rot —he knows, because he was in S. Africa farm-
burning some years ago ; so I get in first by telling him
that he is a back number already so far as S'Africa is con-
cerned.

“Salvation Nell,” the new show at Syd-
ney Royal, has evoked shocked protests from
the local daily press and other quarters
where the militant right-thinker abides. And
yet the piece is no more calculated to subvert
anyone’s morals than a visit to a police court. On
the contrary, it is a sort of dramatic tract, with
a mission to show the reformative power of re-
ligion. The name of the Creator is put to
free and frequent use, an inevitable feature
of a work which lays itself out to give
a faithful picture of Salvation Army methods.
The characters are outspoken ; but they say
nothing that could bring the blush of shame
to the cheek of one in the habit of studying,
say, the Old Testament. The finish, if trite
and unconvincing, is, at any rate, highly edifying.
Something—the purifying influence of a woman
who has been “saved,” the love of a child, the
Christian faith—comes between the chief evildoer
and his sinful habits; a smile of almost unearthly
holiness distorts his visage; he becomes as a
brand plucked from the burning. What is there
in this to shock the most puritanical ? The
answer is that the average citizen has but one or
two phrases to express many emotions, and that
he tends to confuse a shocked aesthetic sense with
outraged righteousness.
“ THE SPIDER AND THE FLY,” AT
SYDNEY PALACE.
The hair-raiser as seen from the back stalls.
Certainly, “ Salvation Nell ” is shocking—
as shocking as a couple of hours in a well-filled
morgue. All through it people are flying like
animals at each other’s throats, getting bestially
drunk, pummelling each other, flinging each other
into the street, howling curses, and, generally,
exposing human nature in its most elementary
aspects. There was a time when this sort of
thing would have appeared natural and seemly to
a theatre crowd. The patrons of the 16th century
cockpit used to revel in it. The burgher of medi-
eval times had no use for fine shades of feeling or
sentiment; he liked to see portrayed on the stage
the passions with which he was familiar in every-
day life; and he liked them served up hot and
strong. The modern playgoer has advanced
several steps beyond that. He recognises that the
primary passions still exist, but he doesn’t yearn
to see them depicted. What absorbs him are the
manifold shifts and concealments which, under a
complex civilisation, arise from those passions.
An isolated outbreak of brute-like rage—the fur-
niture-smashing frenzy of Maldonadoin “Iris,” for
example—the 20th century theatre enthusiast can
stand. “ Salvation Nell ”is over the odds in the
matter of crude unrestraint. It is one long pageant
of primal emotions, and the sophisticated modern
drifts away from it disgusted and resentful in
consequence.
* * * *
The depressing illusion of the piece is
driven home by some superb acting. Jim Platt,
as handled by William Desmond, is an outcast,
whose fidelity to type is unquestionable. Des-
mond even keeps Jim natural and almost likely
in the banal scene where, as the result of a few
crusted old Salv’army gags, that appalling
scoundrel is suddenly stampeded into the path
of virtue. The conversion of Platt is where
an otherwise sincere play slumps violently.
Miss Katherine Grey provides a fine study of the
sinner who “finds salvation,” and yet is in
danger of relapsing into her old ways at the call of
love. Her presence and her odd lack of sex-
appeal handicap her in a part which was
probably written round a full-blooded Bowery
lass. Miss Elsie Wilson’s startlingly realistic
portrait of a New York demi-mondaine is
the best thing she has done. Sydney Stirling’s
Salv’army Major is a beautiful but figure.
He suggests a cavalry officer more than he does
a mere Yankee Gospel-banger. It is hard to
credit that even a one-time “ Fifth Avenue aristo-
crat” would, after a decade in the slums, cling to
the unimpeachable patent-leather boots which the
Major affects.
** * *
“Mr. Preedy” is still at the Criterion
extending a hospitable hand to the very large be-
lated peeress and holding counsel with the peer
whose legs suggest a pair of compasses, but his
days are numbered. They add up to exactly two
—this one and to-morrow. On Saturday night
the old glad story of “What Happened
to Jones” will begin to fill in the last 12
nights of the season, and unless Johnson’s railway
and tramway system breaks down beyond all re-
pair, Sydney intends to be present. Its joy at
the prospect is tempered by a certain amount of
grief at the loss of Preedy, for whom it begins
to have a genuine affection. Three characters
in the departing drama who have left a steadily-
increasing footprint on the sands of applause are
Cornell’s Reginald Saunders, who is a dentist and
a springy sort of bounder ; the Hon. Jennerway,
done by Harold Parkes, who is a heavenly idiot;
and Hugh Buckler’s Earl of Rushmere. The
peerage comes off badly in modern comedy
—possibly because most of our modern comedy
comes from places where the peerage is
under personal observation. It may joe roughly
divided into nine classes : (1) Jumpy, idiotic and
without visible beans of support; (2) jumpy and
idiotic but solvent; (3) foolish and gone in the
legs; (4) drunk, with any of the above complica-
tions added ; (5) sleepy, fat, unwise and always
losing its eyeglass; (6) exceedingly swift on the
feet through long practice at ieading the fast
life and pursuing the fast girl (all the other kinds
lead the fast life also, but this species makes it his
one speciality); (7) elderly, mean, fat, ill-dressed
and grumpy; (8) just elderly and mean; (9) the
shrill sketchy boy peer who is preyed upon by the
bookmaker. These are the British aristocrats of
comedy. When there is added the peer of melo-
drama, who is a hardened girl-drowner and mfant-
smotherer, the Upper House appears as a very bad
lot.

Once more the common round or weekly
task devolves on this paper of mentioning that
“Our Miss Gibbs” continues to be firmly
anchored by the golden strands of popular
affection to Her Majesty’s, Sydney. So securely
is the lady chained down that there seems no
likelihood of her shifting her moorings before
Christmas.

The bald-headed warder who kills the
villain with a blank cartridge in “The Spider
and the Fly,” which is being produced this week
at the Sydney Palace, is to be forcibly re-
moved to-morrow night. No explanation as
to the why or whaffor is forthcoming
from the Hamilton-Maxwell management.
The Bulletin has come to the decision, however,
that it is the blank cartridge that is at fault. If
ball cartridge were used, and one stage villain
shot per night, this publication makes bold to say
that even a warder 99| years of age, bald, blind,
splay-footed, spavined and hamstrung, would fill
the theatre every time he was billed to pull the
trigger. Unfortunately, this is an age when
realism must give way to economy, and the
supply of villain is limited by the demands of
politics. The only villain under engagement to
the Hamilton-Maxwell Co. is therefore being
saved up for “The Revenge,” which will be
staged on Saturday night by arrangement with
Bland’olt.
** * *
Dan, the Drunken Dog, and the rest of
Barnold’s wonderfully well-trained animal troupe
are drawing people with money in their garments
from all points of the compass to Sydney Tivoli
box-office. The hilarious turn advertises itself, and
the official notification that Dan is still drunk is a
superfluous noise like the Sunday church bell and
the barrel organ. The burlesque juggling act of
Howard and Foy is the most notable addition to
the bill this week. The Dancing Gilberts, Woods
and Fraser, Olga Grey and Brown and Robinson
remain as they were; and the beer-garden first
part is flourishing, apparently with the enthusi-
astic approval of Judkins.
** * *
This week West’s Pictures at Sydney
Glaciarium include a tinted “ Geisha.” It is, of
course, a Pathe film, and one of the firm’s master-
pieces. Writer does not remember seeing one in
which the color was laid on with a more dexter-
ous touch. It is the story of a love episode in old
Japan, in the days when the Samurai went
around armed with long swords and fought cheer-
fully over any ugly old subject or pretty young
geisha. The scenery is almost too elaborate in its
honey sweetness; but the general effect is^ de-
lightful, charming and hang-to-morrow. The
Pathe Gazette includes some fine cinematographic
snapshots of Chavez on his wild flight through
the Simplon Pass. Unfortunately, the flight
through the hideous pass is not shown, but the
beginning and the end are ; and the sight of that
frail monoplane gliding through the grey atmo-
sphere over the crags, pines and pinnacles of the
Alps is very fine. The revolution in Portugal is
also among the fearful deeds now being done at
the Glaciarium.

At Sydney Lyceum Spencer’s Theatre-
scope is showing the usual varied programme, of
which the best items are the “Sorrows of the
Unfaithful,” an American biograph film of in-
tensely dramatic quality, (some people like
it that way), “The Little Hero of H°l*
land ’’—the old, old story of the small boy
who stopped the leak in the dyke and nearly
died of sneezing as a result —is a fine film, and
presents little Peter in a new and rather interest-
ing light. Palermo makes a fine scenic series;
but, somehow, one always imagines that these
Dago cities are better on the screen than
in reality. To be absolutely realistic it
would be necessary for Spencer to lay on a
supply of sulphuretted hydrogen, or break
a few stale eggs. All this is missed by see-
ing the places on the film. King Manoel chases-
Gabriel of the White Lilies across the screen at
the Lyceum also ; and an excellent satire is poked,
between the ribs of the Wowser in Sunday
Rest.”

as Bounsall.
At the King’s (Melb.) a diversion from
the Andersonian course of things is provided by
the appearance of the Maggie Moore-H. R.
Roberts co. in “Shadows of a Great City.”
Speaking from dim recollections of how it looked
at the Royal a quarter of a century ago, one
would say that “The Shadows” is not
quite the drama it used to be; but, any-
way, it was even then the story of how
a bell-toppered villain had base designs on
a million dollars. A dejected lady in the first act
is lawful heiress to the dollars, and when she dies
her daughter ought to get them. Eventually she
does get them—ls years after the decease of her
sainted mother. In that long interval all the
adult people concerned in the matter have re-
mained much about the same as they were at first,
whilst the infant heiress who was saved from a
watery grave has become steadily enamored of
her preserver. At the end of the 15 years
which made so little difference to her friends
and enemies, Helen Standish is a sweet, pluni-
some girl, in a nice, cool, yachting costume, and
spends her days in rowing a small boat around the
rock wherefrom the villain’s chief myrmidon tried
to drown her long ago. The million dollars has
been lying dormant all this while, but one day the
myrmidon aforesaid drops casually into the
happy home of the unsuspecting heiress &na
identifies the innocent hero who formerly escaped
from prison, by discovering his convict garb in a
box conveniently placed in the middle of the
domicile. Then the plot is resumed, and some
more murderous attempts are frustrated ere the
dollars are sheeted home to their rightful owner.
* ■» * *
“ Shadows of a Great City ’ is neatly
staged and acted. Maggie Moore, surmounted
by a gaudy head of hair, appears as the motherly
Irishwoman of the drama, and when left alone
for five minutes she sings about Tip of
Tipperary. Roberts is the soft-spoken hero
who looks beauteous even in his prison suit.
Montgomery wears the black belltopper of a
blameful life. E. B. Russell asserts a sham nose
and runs a pawnshop in the interests of crime ;
and John Eorde is a rather sombre criminal
instead of a sprightly malefactor. Charles Stan-
ford is among the other seasoned performers who
figure as starters for this event. And although
there is room for more force and experience in the
acting of the long lost heiress (Ethel Bashford),
her personal appearance leaves nothing to be
desired.

This is the eighth week of “ The Whip ”
at Her Majesty’s (Melb.), and the management
announces with much pleasure that it has “ ex-
ceeded the record ” of any previous drama staged
in Australia. It will continue to exceed the
record until further notice. '

The most notable performer in “The
Whip the auburn small boy who adds a
new terror to the Chamber of Horrors—is mow
doing a “ turn ”at the Princess’s likewise. Bast
Saturday a crude travesty of the railway collision
scene in the J.C.W. hippodrama was dumped
among the other banalities of “ A Knight for a
Day.” The climax of this alleged provocation
to mirth was the voice of the small boy
crying, “ I want to see Charlie Peace ! Which
suggests the advisability of establishing a system
of exchange and barter between the drama at
Her Majesty’s and the cheerful hotchpotch at the
Princess’s. For instance, the ballet from the
variety show might go and shake up the last
mights of “The Whip,” whilst the hysterical
parson was borrowed from Her Majesty s to
solemnise the nuptials of Bert Gilbert and the
lady who helps him to be funny.

A revival of “The Arcadians ” is impending
at Melbourne Royal. ‘ ‘ Tom Jones ” will be cursed
and blessed by the squire of the lobster counten-
ance for the last time on Friday, and next night
the guileless spinster from Arcadia will preach
the Simple Life to the monsters. This time Miss
Winifred O’Connor will be Sombra, and the new
dancer—Cristie Mulniah—is to make her first
appearance. Also, Cromwell will be reincar-
nated as before, but the end of Cromwell’s long
engagement is in sight, and his complimentary
benefit is dated December 1.
*# * *
Wirths’ Circus, which talks of going away
in a week or two, is inviting everybody who loves
a hippopotamus to come and see Lizzie bathe in
the waters of the deserted chute ere she retires
for the night. There are many things beside
Lizzie at the Circus, but these are as naught in
the advt. compared with the lumbering quadruped
that oozes gore from her hide.

Judge Kelly is leaving the Opera House
to-night (Thursday), and another star item is
to shine in his stead. Otherwise the Opera House
bill will remain unchanged, the Quealys being the
latest addition.
** * *
Now that the Horse Carnival is disposed
of, Melbourne is expected to lapse into a musical
mood. A popular concert will transpire at the
Athenaeum Hall on Saturday night, and in the
meantime the Town Hall will be busy. To-night
(Thursday) the usual organ recital has two singers
and a trombone soloist on the programme; to-
morrow Walter Kirby will warble in a farewell
strain to the most fashionable audience Melbourne
can raise for the occasion ; and on Saturday after-
noon the Y.P.O. will give a symphony concert —
the sixth and last of the series. W. A. Laver is
to conduct the orchestra.
** * *
The Melbourne Cup, as pictured at the
Melbourne Glaciarium, looks a duller sort of
function than it is, since the multitudinous tints
of frocks, faces, earth and sky are reduced to a
.monotone. The blessings of the sentimental,
however, will fall on “Discipline and a Gener-
ous Act,” a tearful story of filial love; and on
“The Usurer,” a fanciful tale of a grasping Jew.
The loudest laughter follows “An Enemy of the
Dust,” which shows a man getting himself fear-
fully and weirdly soiled. Eventually he becomes
so encumbered with dirt that he could cut up his
clothes for closer settlement.
.** * *
People will not be impressed by the last
British Army manoeuvres as unfolded at Mel-
bourne Olympia on Saturday. To the layman
the picture is technical and almost tedious, for it
affords no glimpses of imitation Jenas or mimic
Waterloos. The tale of “Wilful Peggy” is, how-
ever, excellent for its cute plot, comely women
and natural gaiety ; while “The Glove” is thick
with a realism that excuses its ancient story. As
for the innocent yarn of “ The Cowboy’s Strata-
gem,” this paper is doubtful whether to love it for
its love or loathe it for its length.

In a quiet week, the Appendreena Society
of Adelaide has given a clever and well-balanced
rendering of “Mrs. Gorringe’s Necklace.”
Branscombe has started a season of “Dandies”
on the terraces below the Jubilee Exhibition.
They are much the same thing as the Pierrots
and Follies who disport themselves on the beaches.
Summer, however, hangs fire in Adelaide this
year, and out-of-doors shows are suffering. The
Royal has re-opened with “ The Winning Ticket,”
which is rather a tight fit for the small stage when
the Cup is run. The play is pleasantly free from
bushrangers and even murders, and shows the
city side of Australian life right up to date. The
Melbourne Cup is at the Empire, too, per
picture. This house has also revived the
gentle convict who repents to organ accom-
paniment, with a real vocalist singing a real
hymn. West’s Pictures are starring a hasty
glimpse of the revolution in Portugal. The
Tivoli is rather various these times. Powell and
Miss Vesey have left for England, much re-
gretted, but the Meymotts adequately replace
them, while Hughes and Prior run a bit of every-
thing into their brisk turn. Larry Lewis is
popular, but might be more so if he had fewer
mannerisms—or less of one mannerism. Alex-
ander Prince is an Englishman who squeezes
wonders out of the concertina. He can make it
resemble anything from the organ to the bagpipes,
but—as generally happens in Adelaide—he gets
quite as much enthusiasm for the masterly play-
ing of a simple air as for his whole bag of showier
tricks.
HUGH WARD, THE NEW MEMBER OF
THE J. C. WILLIAMSON FIRM.
The thirteenth Federal picture-show in
Adelaide has not sold any pictures to the S.A.
Gallery yet; but the trustees are to come round
later. There is nothing actually crying out to be
bought. Many of the inter-State men already re-
presented in the Australian Room at Adelaide
have stayed away this year. From Sydney,
Albert Hanson sends a “ Bush Smithy ” and some
smaller things. Norman Carter dominates the
big room with his “Low-toned Harmony.” Dattilo
Rubbo has some of his characteristic heads; Nicho-
las Shiels, an ambitious but curiously hard domestic
scene, and A. E. Rosebray some clever native
beasts. Of the Victorians, Bernard Haft scores
with “ Under the Colosseum,” a semi-nude figure
on the sands of the arena; it makes technical dif-
ficulties only to triumph over them. Ford Pater-
son sends ordinary landscapes. E. W. Christmas,
who is a cosmopolitan, has trucked in a great
Maoriland snow-topped valley, his diploma work
of last year for the Royal British Artists’ Society
in London. The S.A. men have been (and
some still are) in London. H. S. Power
has got away from his animal studies
with a strong picture of the Thames and
the Houses of Parliament alongside ; and Gustave
Barnes contrasts an Australian summer sketch with
“A Suffolk Pasture,” all pale-green and mist.
James and Will Ashton (father and son) need not
sign their seascapes, their respective styles being
beyond mistake. George Webb has deserted
portraits for landscape. The Hambidge sisters
contribute the usual highly-finished figures, and
Rose Macpherson some careful still-life studies
which compete with Bernard Hall’s studio-
sketches. Hans Heysen and Hayley Lever are
missing—and badly missed.
The Sydney morning papers published
three columns of the heroic guff of Wade and
Oarruthers at the Town Hall banquet of last
Monday. And in all the speeches there wasn’t a
hint of how the Wade Ministry proposed to find
the money for these amazing, not to say impos-
sible, railway and tramway pensions.
Tait’s retirement from the Victorian Rail-
ways is creating a vacancy for a third Commis-
sioner, which gossip is endeavoring to fill. So far
it especially favors the chances of L. McClelland
and E. B. Jones. McClelland is Secretary of the
Railways, while Jones is General Passenger and
Freights Agent, and, moreover, is alleged to
possess a grip of the fashionable business of elec-
trification.

Discussing the second ballot result in
Hastings and Macleay, Sydney Telegraph pro-
pounded the following :
If Mr. Morton decides to throw over the larger section
of those who elected him and join the ranks of Mr.
McGowen’s followers, there is nothin? to prevent him ;
or if he eleets to ignore the smaller section and support
the Opposition, he is free to take that course.
Had any of the 3300 who voted for Morton ap-
proved of “Liberalism,” the opportunity of
proving the fact lay ready to hand. Davidson,
and his friends and canvassers, the Sydney press,
and all sorts of other strident folks, were yelling
for support. The paper that is capable of sug-
gesting that the Hastings electors who deliber-
ately voted against “Liberalism” were for the
most part “Liberals,” is capable of arguing that
when Judkins asks for milk he expects beer.
The Gr.P.O. at Sydney isn’t an old build-
ing, and it has already been enlarged sideways
and enlarged upwards, and one department has
been turned out and sent to house itself elsewhere,
in order to make more room for the others. Yet the
complaint of hopeless overcrowding grows worse
every day. The G.P.0., in fact, is like the in-
sufficient Owe’Sullivan railway station, and the
narrow streets of the city, and the wretched
Federal capital site—the result of utter inability
to look ahead and see the greatness that is coming.
A little while ago the disgusted municipal fathers
tried to sell theirwhiteelephant, the Queen Victoria
Markets, to the Government as a new post-office
site, and offered it amazingly cheap as elephants
go; but, again, nobody looked far enough ahead,
and the offer was rejected. The place seemed
too big. Probably, some years hence, it will be
bought at 500 per cent, advance just when it is
about,to become too small.
“The Snake”: The woodener which
N.S.W. has given to the wowsers ought to
hearten up Maoriland a bit. That unfortunate
country has for years past been bridled and
curbed by snufflebusters, until a moral gloom has
settled on the inhabitants like a wet Sunday on
Dunedin. The worst part of it is, there is hardly
a paper of any influence that will take the kill-
joys in hand, and speak advisedly to them regard-
ing the necessity for obtaining work. On the
contrary, every encouragement is given them by
free advts. to continue in their mournful tracks.
Let me quote the Wellington Dominion as an ex-
ample. On a rece'nt Wednesday, the issue con-
tained just on six columns of Snuffle news, in-
cluding articles, reports and paragraphs concern-
ing :
Suggestions “ to win the town for God.”
Anglican Canon’s message to the weary and heavy-
laden.
Street processions of clergy and choir.
Presentations to Sunday scholars.
Deputation to Premier to promote the cause of total
abstinence.
Mid-day meetings for men.
Mission services, in-door and out-door.
Missioners’ descent upon railway workshops and
woollen mill in pursuit of Satar,.
Objectionable forms of worship in China, India and
Africa.
Need of pure language in public baths.
Sunday swimming restrictions.
Pen-picture of Joe Harris, sometime miner, now mis-
sioner and social worker, addressing wharf-lumpers on
Sin.
These are only a few items in one issue of one
paper out of hundreds; but it gives a reasonable
idea of the grip the wowser’s ice-cold hand has on
the national heart.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Hugh J. Ward, the new member of the J. C. William-
son firm, will retire from the rush of the road on June 19
next, after a farewell Australian and Maoriland tour.
His first trip to this country was with Harry Connor's
“Trip to Chinatown” co., where his dancing- brought
him into immediate prominence. An engagement
with the Royal Comic Opera Co. followed ; and it was
then he started to dream of the time when his name,
instead of being on J. C. Williamson’s salary list,
would appear on the bottom of the firm’s cheque.
After fulfilling his engagement with the Royal Comics,
Ward went on to London, where he made another hit
with a man-ape or ape-man dance in Drury Lane pantp-
mime. After that he joined George Willoughby, played
comedy through Australia, returned to London, toured
America with his own co., and has been roving over the
planet ever since. He has been, and is, one of the best
employers on the road, and one of the most conscientious
showmen in his dealing with the public ; it is a fair thing
to say that he is on the good books of every actor or actress
who has been associated with him. His business
abilities need no farther guarantee than that given by
the invitation of J. C. Williamson to come into partner-
ship ; and his personal popularity is vouched by the unani-
mous hooray of Australasian pressmen. Ward will have
charge at the Sydney end of the Williamson operations.
Though the changes in the J. C. Williamson firm, men-
tioned last week, are pending, J.C.W. himself isn’t, of
course, retiring from the business ; and it seemed quite
unnecessary to say that much at the time. All that is
expected is that he will ease off a trifle. No hand, cer-
tainly, has yet been found strong enough to restrain him ;
but there are hopes that he will some day be persuaded
that he can’t be such a furious, incessant worker for ever.
“Henry Horsecollar”: I regard with bitter contempt
the oration of Salvation Nell in the play of that
name, now being performed in Sydney. I look upon it
as the limit. The heroine has spent her soul and her
weary days and her boundless affection in trying to drag
a certain slouchy brand from the burning. He hasn’t
dragged worth a copper, though he has slouched a great
deal. He has only soaked himself with rum so that he may
burn better. He is an evidence that figs don’t grow on
thistles or oranges flourish in Greenland, and that no one
ever saw a monkey sitting on the roof of the forest reading
the masterpieces of Greek prose. No ordinary methods
are likely to apply in his case. Yet the Salvation heroine,
standing on an inverted egg-cup in the street, speaks a
few sentences of the old, weak, foolish, conventional big-
drum language, and, behold, the incurable one is saved.
Now, that kind of sinner requires strenuous treatment.
This is the sort of oration that I should try on him :
“ Dear brethren andsistern, also lectern, slattern, bittern,
tavern and cithern. If you don’t repent you will be biled
forever. You will bile inwardly. When you open your
mouth to breathe, a cloud of steam will come out like
washing day. Flame will spread out from your ears for
a distance of two yards. Your head will be a voleano.
You will dwell for ever in a pitch-black cell. There will
be no meal times—only flames. Your bedstead will be
flames. You will have a red-hot mosquito-curtain
to keep out the vampires, and they won’t be kept out.
When you hear a passing footstep in the dark once every
billion years you will call out and say : ‘ What time is it?’
and a hoarse voice will answer: ‘Eternity.’ My friends,
among the Norse heathen there was a legend about the
Queen of Hell. It ran something like this:—
‘ Famine was her knife,
Hunger the table she laid,
Delay was her serving man
And Slowness her serving maid ;
Precipice was her threshold,
Care was her thorny bed,
And the tapestry of her chamber
Was Anguish burning red.’
And that, my friends, was nothing to what is going
to happen to you. The best you can hope for is to spend
eternity cleaning the boots of that heathen serving-maid,
and these boots will be three miles long, and you will
clean them with a red-hot brush and bilin' blacking.
And while you are cleaning these boots a moment will be
as 1000 years, and it will take a million years to each
boot.” That is the way I would attack the dreadfulsinner
in “Salvation Nell.” He is a bad lot, and requires to be
spoken to firmly.
A new voice, a mezzo-soprano, which has been classed
high by those whose qualifications justify a respectful
attitude towards their opinion, is to be heard this week
in Sydney. The owner is Miss Ida Moran, a young Maori-
lander, who arrived in Sydney three or four years ag-o.
Shortly afterwards she toured the North Coast of N.S.W.
as the "elocutionist in a concert co. with creditable success.
But reciting is a waste of good voice if the singing quality
is there, and Miss Moran has it on good authority that it
is there. Steffani has recently been polishing it, and to-
night at the Y.M.C.A. Hall it will be offered for criticism
ait an invitation concert. Another feature of the pro-
gramme will be the playing of Alfred Hill’s “Maori
Sonata.”
West’s Pictures put up a cinematograph record in con-
nection with the Melbourne Cup. Immediately after the
race the undeveloped film was carried from Flemington
by special train, brought on to Sydney by express, put
on board the Maheno leaving for Auckland (M.L.) the
same morning, transferred to train there, and wheeled
over the main trunk line to Wellington, where it arrived
on Tuesday morning, and was shown on the screen at the
firm’s theatre the same night.
Emil Greder, of the line, tragic presence, soulful eyes
and rich baritone voice, who came to Australia with Mus-
grove’s German Opera co.,and remained for some time
teaching, was at latest accounts in N’York singing to 10
cent audiences at moving-picture shows. Six years back
he was singing all the leading baritone grand opera r6les
at the Metropolitan Opera House. That was before he
made Australia’s acquaintance and took away a bunch of
Australian .singers, his pupils, to N’York. Four columns
of explanation in an American paper fail to make clear
the reason of the baritone’s slide into 10 centdom. His
voice is said to be as good as it was when he was Wolfram,
Don Juan and others.
The everlasting war between theatres and music halls
in England has been carried a step further by the en-
gagement of Sara Bernhardt for a half-hour’s turn at
London Coliseum. She takes along her co. and plays the
second act of “ L’Aiglon.”
Once off the regular track of show towns even such
names as J. C. Williamson and Path 6 Frferes cut no ice,
so to speak. T’other day M. P. Adams dropped into
Amphitheatre (Vic.), a one-horse hamlet, with Path 6
Fr&res “ Australia at Work ” enterprise. Before the
pianist could obtain the key of the piano the caretaker
requested the rent, and also informed the management
that, having given its performance, the co. would not be
permitted to camp in the hall—no, not even for one
night!
The haughty and beautiful mummer of Australia pro-
poses to drown his pride and go in for unionism. The
following are the chief objects he sets before himself :
(1) Legislation to control the wholesale importation of
theatrical companies from England and elsewhere; (2)
steps to prevent the introduction of Sunday shows, which
mean that the actor would be obliged to work seven days
a week for six days’pay ; (3) a minimum wage ; (4) Better
conditions as to loss of time, payment for matinees
and other matters. When 100 mimes have thus banded
together, they will be registered under the Trades Union
Act, and set out in chase of a living wage and a fair thing
generally.
“Stargazer”: Walter C. Kelly, from America, now
pitching yarns for Rickards, demonstrates the superiority
of talent over traps. He flies so light in the matter of
professional baggage that, after picking up a stick and
borrowing a penny exercise book from the nearest school
kid, he could set up his turn on a stump. He could even do
without the book and the stick and the stump. Walter
has just the easiest gift imaginable. He talks his charac-
ters, throwing in a touch of ventriloquism and trifles of
facial adjustment, and the people live. His stuff is all
good, it is touched with our common human cussedness,
and it goes with a music-hall audience as well as the most
uproarious kind of knock-about extravaganza. There’s
literary talent behind W. C. Kelly—whose?

Arnbrose Kyte, who gave £lOOO to the Burke and Wills
expedition, and whose Lane is threatened with the new
name of Payne Place, owned the Theatre Royal in Barry
Sullivan s time. They disagreed over a lady. Later on,
George Fawcett “ took off ” Sullivan in a burlesque called
the Chamber of Horrors, something like the Waxworks

“P. Prink”: George Musgrove reckons that Austral-
asians don’t realise what a good grand-opera orchestra
is. He told his old partner, Williamson, a while ago
that he wouldn’t touch a proposed grand-opera scheme
without an orchestra, and that would cokt too
much money. “We haven’t got the people,” said
Musgrove. “ Most of the few players in Aus-
tralia who would be good enough to play in a
grand opera orchestra in Europe are teaching, and it
would not pay them to sacrifice their connections.”
Talking of the Royal Opera in Berlin, Musgrove says that
the orchestra is composed of 120 finished instrumentalists,
80 of whom play while 40 rest. These musicians are paid
£2 or £2 10s. a week, but money is not so much the con-
sideration—it is the honor. And they are entitled to a
State pension after 10 years—as is everybody else in the
theatre, from the footlights to the stage door. “When
the orchestra is at work,” said Musgrove enthusiastically,
“ it is like one man playing—perfect to the finest point.
Opera is not an amusement in Berlin ; it is a serious busi-
ness—the embodiment of high musical art. In Australia
one can only give a suggestion of the real thing—joinery,
not cabinet-making.’’

Bernhardt shortly essays the rdle of Mephistopheles in
a Gallic version of “ Faust,” written by the obliging
Edmond Rostand. “My conception of the part,” said
the lady recently, “ will, of course, be somewhat differ-
ent from that of a German artiste. Art, it has been said,
is life seen through a temperament. lam essentially
Latin in temperament, and therefore this, no doubt, will
appear in my interpretation of the rdle. To put it
broadly, I imagine Mephistopheles as a fallen angel, with
something of his former self still left in him. It is this
trait of his psychology which I shall attempt to repro-
duce on the stage.” Bernhardt, who is securing an
eminent array of talent for this production, has caught
the distinguished Le Bargy, of the Comedie to
enact the part of the trusting Faust.

“Scene-shifter”: Lewis Waller, booked for Australia
in 1912, will find that Ju Knight has made us familiar
with some of his crack pieces—including “Beaucaire” and
“ Robin Hood.” “ Beaucaire ”he put on as a stopgap ;it
ran 400 nights. Waller first “ went on ”at Toole’s theatre,
under Toole’s management, in the early ’Bo’s, as a
youthful Orlando, and in similar roles. He*gained much
useful experience round “the provinces” with Modjeska.
He first attracted Big Smoke attention in 1887 in
Kate Vaughan’s production of “Masks and Faces."
Susie Vaughan, now one of us, was the Kitty Clive of
that show. At the Gaiety, Waller played with Mrs.
Brown-Potter and Kyrle Bellew ; then followed jobs with
Wilson Barrett, the Kendals and Tree; and from 1899 on
he has mostly been making his own jobs. He has accu-
mulated an extensive Shakespook repertoire, which he
may unpack in Australia. His wife, who tours under
hubby’s name, and generally with her own co., created a
sensation in 1901 as Zaza, a piece associated here with
Nellie Stewart.
THE BETTER PART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No difficulty should be experienced by the J. C.
Williamson management in securing: “supers” for
“Salvation Nell.” Real beer is handed out in the first
act.
One r6le there is for which the mime
Asks little salary—
At which he labors overtime
With keen alacrity ;
It is, in plain brief terms, a part
Designed to warm a mummer’s heart.
No wearisome rehearsals are
Gone through to get it right;
And yet he acts it like a star
On the eventful night.
(It’s not, I might remark, his will
There’s no preliminary drill.)
The artist who sustains this role
Dreads not the critic’s jeer.
No nervousness disturbs his soul;
His heart is free from fear.
He knows he cannot help but play
In lifelike fashion, come what may.
Hard is the row the actor hoes ;
Few boons or joys are his ;
Still, perfect happiness he knows,
Not to say frantic bliss,
When the glad words impact his ear :
“ You’ll play a bloke that’s mad on beer.”
N.S.W.
H. S.
RIME OF A JOYSTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nowadays, the Melbourne Temperance Hall is used for
vaudeville “ pops.”—“ Poverty Point” fact.
A crayfish in each hand he held ;
With prawns his pockets bulged and brimmed,
And as he rolled along he yelled
With laughter. Life and joy he hymned,
And he told the world in a loud, wild bawl,
“ See, I’ve bin up at th’ Temp’rinsh ’All 1 ”
He sang gay songs of a breezy kind ;
His rollicking voice was rich with beer;
And beer and crays and prawns combined
To spur him on to hooray and cheer,
And bellow a hint to the Law, “ Sorright—
Bin up to—hup !—Temp’rinsh ’All, to-night!”
He swept along on a long-beer jag,
And as he mafficked he did not cease
To shout “ ’Ooray! ” —with a cray-tail flag
Uplifted to wave at the shocked police.
“ ’Ooray ! ” he hollered, a fount of sound,
“For th’ Temp’rinsh ’All an’ th’pubsh all round ! ”
The night was loud with his joyful voice
As he drank his beer and he blewed his “ dough,”
As he bellowed and bid the world rejoice,
And nibbled his prawns and his cray-claws... no,
You’d never have guessed from his style, at all,
That he’d spent the night at the Temperance Hall!
VICTORIA.

REDUCED FOR CASH.

swindled me over that horse deal.
You said he could trot a mile in two minutes , and the best I can make him do is a

AUCTIONEER: “ Vell the other two minutes is discount."
The second reading of the Bill to prohibit
certain classes of Sunday entertainment has ill all
solemnity been carried by the Victorian Assembly.
Premier Weary announced in a pained tone that
the breaking of the day of rest was rapidly becom-
ing a scandal. Britishers, Bernard Shaw tells us,
think they are moral when they are only uncom-
fortable, and opine, therefore, that a day of roast
beef and bad sermons is a day of rest. The
average sensual citizen eats enormously on Sun-
day because he has nothing else to do, drowses
with relaxed buttons during the warm afternoon,
turns and snores all night in his sleep, and goes to
graftonMondav morning biliousand bad-tempered.
This is the British ideal of a well-spent Sabbath,
an ideal adopted with enthusiasm by a large
number of otherwise normal Australians, A
day of rest means simply a day of
cracked church bells and Salvation Army cor-
nets, masterful inaction, lassitude and indigestion.
As to the economic or industrial side, Murray’s
Bill is, of course, horrible hypocrisy. In France,
where they don’t have this dead Sabbath, a
weekly rest has been established. There even
domestic servants have 24 hours’ complete rest in
every seven days. But when a deputation
recently waited on the good Victorian Premier to
ask for one day off in every seven for all workers,
the Wowsers’ Friend (who thinks the way Sunday
is broken is a scandal) sent them away empty.

“ Gent One ” has long been a synonym for
rapacity, low cunning and double dealing ; in a
thousand melodramas and on a thousand music-hall
stages the solicitor has been held up to execration
as a sly, unscrupulous hound; and literature
teems with stories of the unworthy ways of the
“ pettifogging attorney.” But, perhaps, the
shrewdest stab a maligned profession has ever
endured was inflicted on it last week, when the
land agents foregathered in Sydney and decided
unanimously not to allow solicitors into their ranks.
Banned by the land agency profession ! It is
enough to make a sensitive solicitor, if such there
lie, take to polities. There are exceptions, no
doubt; but, as a general thing, land, house and
estate agents are about as hot propositions as
you will find outside Parramatta Gaol, where the
second and third-timers are. Their job is the
last resort of (1) rejected Parliamentarians, (2)
solicitors who have been struck off the rolls. (3)
unfrocked clerics, (4) Wowsers who have fallen
beneath the spell of Beer and been found out, (5)
ex-gaol-birds possessed of “a manner and ad-
dress.” Of course, there are respectable land
agents. Anyhow, it was scarcely for land agents
to propel the first brick —not even at the Ma
State’s solicitors.

How they brought the good news to Mel-
bourne :
The first word about the Eureka Stockade appears to
have been taken to the Geelong Advkrtiskr by one
McElwaine. Another messenger went direct to Mel-
bourne and told Attorney-General Stawell, who was at
St. James’s Church, the battle having been fought at
dawn on Sunday, December 3. Thus far history. The
writer of an impracticable yarn about Eureka, which I
bought the other day, adds some trimmings to the story.
He-says the Chief Commissioner picked out Gore, a smart
officer, and sent him off full tear on a horse named
Inkerman, out of Cobb’s coach. Inkerman pounded on
to the first Cobb change—Puddy’s wayside inn. “ Here,”
says Gore, “give me the best of Cobb’s horses and take
this one”; and the best was a roan mare (Jeanette), a
racer. Jeanette carried Gore over a bad patch of road from
Gordon’s towards Ballan. He thought she was down once.
At Ballan he didn’t try Cobb, knowing that his horses de-
teriorated getting away from Ballarat and Melbourne
(always the bloods for the start and the finish). But
he had the luck to meet Fisken, with a splen-
did fresh horse, and swapped. The fifth horse was
got at Melton; the sixth took him to Melbourne.
Gore made straight for St. James’s, with a conviction
that Stawell would be there. The congregation was sing-
ing the Te Deum. Says Gore to the verger: “Please
call Mr. Stawell out. News of the most urgent im-
portance.” Out comes Stawell, bareheaded, to get the
first news of Eureka.
A bakers’ deputation which waited on
N.S.W. Premier McGowen the other day, told
him that fresh bread, hot from the oven, was a
discredited old image with clay feet. The depu-
tation wanted McGowen to pass a law prohibiting
night work in bakeries, the idea being that a
Wages Board can’t successfully do the trick
because of the number of small bread-mills which
don’t employ any baking-labor except the boss’s.
Pre&ty well everyone admits that it is better
for men to work by day than by night—
better for their health and better socially—
but in this matter the fresh-bread fetich has got
the public by the throat, and even the sym-
pathetic McGowen wants to be assured that there
will be no public inconvenience before he goes
ahead. The best assurance available, so far, is
that 11 years ago Liverpool abolished night work
in its bakeries, and nothing dreadful has hap-
pened to it, and it isn’t proposing to go back on
its tracks; and Cork and other sizeable cities have
been for years eating bread that was brewed the
day before.
About an old-established, washing-place :
The time-worn question of shifting Kenny’s Baths from
their ancient site at St. Kilda (Melbourne) cropped up in
the Victorian Assembly the other day, when a Harbor
Trust Land Resumption Bill was being pushed through.
This is practically a Bill for removing the old baths to
make way for a sea-wall, whereby Melbourne’s populous
marine suburb will be robbed of a long stretch of sea
beach. This sea-wall is part of a plan devised for the
purpose of limiting St. Hilda’s sea beach to the length of
the present Esplanade in the interests of property-owners
and showmen thereabouts. The sea-wall will call
for the abolition of Kenny’s ancient stronghold,
though it will leave the St. Kilda Yacht Club johnnies
in undisturbed possession of their bit of beach adjoining
the baths. And the question, voiced by Maekinnon in
the House, is: “What is the public to do for bathing
facilities at St. Kilda?” Premier Murray replied with
the old statement that the proprietor has been offered a
suitable site for new baths, but this is denied by the man
who is now under notice to quit. He seems bound to be
a loser, whereas the syndicate that owns the only other
baths in the vicinity is bound to be a gainer. And the
public, anyway, will lose an easy means of getting washed.
If a change of site is inevitable, the public, just as much
as Kenny, wants it to be arranged with all possible regard
for bathing facilities. Premier Murray, seemingly, has
only considered the arguments of certain persons who
have the loan of his ear.
* * * *
Randolph Bedford —back once more from
his travels :
I’ve heard a good deal about a combine in Australian
coastal shipping, and it seems to me that a combine is
not always the worst form of business operation. Any-
how, this one doesn’t stop the Australian getting more
value for his money than any other man on earth—as be
should in a country so marvellously productive. At
Yokohama, some months ago, I booked by a Pacific mail
liner, the Korea, to Honolulu and San Francisco. The
Korea is owned by Mr. Harriman, and he ate my meal all
the way, and charged me £3 a day for a shocking menu ;
and thousands of tip-hunters lay privily in wait for me
everywhere. Returning, I had a service twice as good on
a Union (M.L.) boat from Vancouver to Sydney at
£1 a day. And before I left Australia I had
the perfection of ship and service on an Australian
liner —Melbourne to Cairns and back again for £l5;
roughly, 28 days of perfect travelling and accommodation
at 10s. Od. a day. If they had that sort of combine in the
United States they’d canonise it, and put up stained glass
windows to the directors. When I hear some of these
new-chum Australians talking of high prices and cursing
the Labor party, I think of my dollar steak in New York,
and £3 a day for fish and worms to Mr. Harriman, and
4s. 2d. for a telegraph message which would cost me in
Australia one shilling. This is the cheapest land on earth
and the best; even its Shipping Combine oppresses
travellers at 10s. 9d. a day.

“ Snell ” : Toby Claude, the tiniest Ameri-
can actress on any stage, has been criticising the
Australian young man so far as she has seen him.
She objects that he is nothing of a brick in the
matter of glorious raiment. He seems to forget
that he is the most important thing in life, and
that his clothes come next to him. No smart
Murkan youth ever forgets that, says Miss Toby.
He has eight suits of clothes, and four of them
are always away being pressed, so that the wearer
may look just It—the perfect It, without wrinkles
and creases. It’s a comfort to hear we are not
quite as tailor-made as our neighbors. Still, Miss
Claude may live to find that the young Aus-
tralian johnnie is more pressed by his tailor than
appears on first sight.
THE GRASS IN THE GLOAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. related how he had seen numerous
\ oung couples seated on the grass and benches in the
park whose conduct, etc., etc. He could give names.”
Another clergyman : “0, Don’t! ” — Recent Bulletin.
As I walked through the grass in the gloam
I saw how the wicked do seek
To find a dear, stolen delight
In nibbling a she-sinner’s cheek ;
I heard in the hush of the night
The sound of a word which was “ Nyum ” ;
’Twas breathed from the lips of a wight
Whose scandalous ways struck me dumb,
And showed me how Satan doth roam
And lurk in the grass in the gloam !
As T stole through the grass in the gloatn,
The cuddlesome couples I noted ;
I saw how their arms would entwine,
And how on each other they doted.
Gnwrapt in oblivion divine,
They cared not a pin for inspection ;
And the more that I peeped and I peered
The deeper became their infection !
Yea! Just as I sadly had feared,
They hugged and they mugged in the gloam,
As I stole through the grass in the gloam.
Oh! Dear brethren—the grass in the gloam—
Right down from the garden primeval,
Has teemed with the Creatures of Sin,
And swarmed with the Children of Evil.
I cannot see how we can win
Our way to the gates of our yearning
Unless we set fire to the grass,
And smoke out the Lost in the burning.
Yea- smoke out the lad and the lass,
Who laugh on the grass in the gloam,
The glamorous grass in the gloam !
As I stole through the grass in the gloam,
There smote me a bibulous sinner—
Yea ! full in the rear with his boot
(O ! grievous it felt after dinner).
He called me a skunk and a coot,
And likewise a meddlesome vermin.
Oh ! children of Wrath such as he
They scoff at the church and the sermon,
And they fall on the Righteous like Me
Full oft in the gloom of the gloam ;
As we steal through the grass in the gloam !
This Tale of the Grass and the Gloam
I tell it to all who may need it.
I hid them beware of the snare ;
And, brethren, I hope you will heed it.
Ye maidens with gold in your hair
Fight shy of the park and its benches,
For I, even I, have been there
And heard the loud giggle of wenches ;
And have groaned in a deadly despair
At the wickedness there in the gloam,
As I crept through the grass in the gloam !
S. AUSTRALIA.
S. STIGGINS.
RETURN THANKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When on the shy and blushing rose
The clear, teetotal fluid flows
From out the leaky garden hose,
No fulsome compliments it blows
In payment of the debt it owes—
Its fragrant thanks delights my nose.
So, when in some great luncheon tent
The full-fed banqueters give vent
To ev’ry stale, old sentiment,
I mutter as each fossil proses,
“ Would that his thanks were like the Rose’s !”

The Boy Scouts, the famous Red Herring
organisation, seized an advt. for itself out of the
disappearance of Bessie Gibson, a girl who was
last seen reading one of Anthony Hope’s novels
on the rocks at Manly (N.S.W.). The Scouts
who crawl about on their stomachs in the vicinity
of that seaside resort, volunteered to help in the
systematic search which was being carried out
quietly by the police and private sympathisers.
None of these yearned to be photographed in
the act of searching; or, if they did, they
suppressed the yearn and kept strictly to
business. The Kid Scouts, though, were not
that kind of searcher. Last Saturday’s paper
contained two pictures dedicated to their
glorification. The first showed two young-
sters breast-high in scrub; underneath it
appeared the label “On the Trail.” As a matter
of cold fact, there was no trail to be on. The other
illustration was of two lads saluting a youth with
an open note-book who was standing beside a
flagstaff and an immense Union Jack. The
smaller of the saluting pair had rope festooned
about his person as if he had just returned |from a
lynching. The hopeful reader searching for an
explanation of the triumphant tableau, found
beneath it these fateful words: “ The First Re-
port : Nothing.” This country is going to be Hull
of inferior actors presently, when the Boy Scouts
grow up.
“ The Religious Editor ” is dissatisfied :
One of these nights the glittering substance which
climbs up the thermometer steadily from 9 till 3, then
turns with a windy shriek and slithers down the banis-
ters, will fall and break its neck. I was very disap-
pointed when the fatal accident failed to happen on a
recent Tuesday. That morning, when I roamed out to
get the paper, a breath of hot wind stroked my cheek like
a kitten’s paw. As I went in I looked at the thermome-
ter, and saw that the mountaineering fluid had started
for a day’s climb. At 9 o’clock it was scooting past 72'0;
at 11, it had left 88’0 behind ; and at 12.45 it was totter-
ing dizzily at 93'9. A demand for long beers, lemon
squashes, fans, ice, draughts, shade, brevity and
quietness set in and raged in the dusty city, where
the wind at every corner poured itself at you like the
breath of a well-stokeddragon. Restaurantcookswho had
not reckoned on a hot day, cried bitterly into cauldrons of
Irish stew and heaped curry on their heads ; drapers,
with anything warm-looking in their windows, perspired
at the window-dresser and told him puftily that it must
never happen again ; people with immense stocks of hot-
water bottles drank steadily; and hot-pie merchants
went and devoured their own stocks. All clocks seemed
to stop dead, barrel organists were silenced, German
bands became extinct, policemen ran down, fish-shops
screamed, and pints of prawns held indignation meetings
and threatened to leave the country. Miserable Dagoes
sat behind fly-spotted cash registers, and snarled in cray-
fish at one another. Nobody talked ; the art of
gossip blew up with a bang and the populace sat
stunned, taking the count. Suddenly a puff of dust
and a reviving whiff of cool sea-wind hit the nerveless,
panting city, and it sat up and found that 4 o’clock had
stolen upon it. A whirling squadron of ragged grey-
black clouds came galloping up the sky, and on every
road a column of dust raced north. Then the mercury
leaped on the banisters, and flashed downstairs till at
9 o’clock it was at 60'0 —a drop of 33 degrees in five
hours. Out of doors the wind flowed swiftly by at about
30 miles an hour, not like dragon’s breath, but like a
mountain creek after a snow-melting carnival. I sneezed
35 times, slammed the door, kicked the thermometer and
tried to warm myself with whisky and bad language.
Ours is a versatile climate.
* # * #
“Billy B.” ; It is always profitable to
study woman ; but it is most profitable when the
circumstances betray her into removing her mask.
The girl nearest me at the Stadium the other
night was about 20 or 22, well-dressed and evi-
dently of “ the better class.” She and her girl-
companion had arrived early, and were in the
reserved chairs. They spoke very little to
each other, kept their eyes glued upon the
fighting stage, and invited no attention to
themselves. The younger girl was anxious;
her mouth had the dryness which accom-
panies nervous anticipation ; and she swal-
lowed with little gulps that twitched her
throat repeatedly. But when the seconds
ha.d stripped the men, leaving them all skin-
glistening and muscle-rippling beneath the
great electric globes, she smiled softly, and
the two trained animals were unslipped. It
was short and sudden and nasty, that fight;
after the first two rounds, it was not a fight
at all, but was merely one'man throwing all
his science and strength and brutality of
mind-direction to batter his fellow to insen-
sibility. And, as the heavy blows flashed to
the unguarded face, the “hiss” of the
striker’s breath was doubled by the “hiss”
of the girl beside me, who was giving
blow for blow as surely as if she were
within the ropes. Her arms folded, her
body leaning forward, the fingers of the
band nearest me tightened in a clutch of
white blouse ; she hardly moved except when
the failing man reeled beneath his punish-
ment, or dropped to stagger up again.
Then her head moved from side to side as
if she, too, had been stricken, and was
fighting hard to keep the mist back from
her senses. But she was not hurt at what
she saw ; even in the fourth round, before
the referee sensibly and decently stopped
the slogging of a beaten man, she seemed
fascinated with the crude trial of animalism.
As the cheers for the victor died, and the
washing sponges played red in the electric glare,
she rose and passed out with the crowd—the
mother and custodian of the Eternal Savage.

From The Bulletin’s chained Anarchist:
To the unthinking it would appear as if the Fat Man
was the same brand of inhumanity wherever he was
located, but a study of the species will show this to be an
error. I do not mean that he is any less inhuman any-
where, for by his works you shall know him in all
lands and localities. But there are some distinct
differences, which may be observed by even the
dullest and most mentail.v-blind person—always except-
ng a Tory editor and a Chamber of Commerce. Take
the Commonwealth and Maoriland, for instance, and let
us start with New South Wails. Here you have a
prime rascal, with a furtive eye that suggests
the fear of the gaoler. Work is his abhorrence. He
would rather swindle the Customs of 10s. than
build up a bed in Heaven ; he would allow a nation to rot
at his back door rather than bear a hand except to lift its
small change. This next sample is from Victoria. Ob-
serve the enlarged ear (developed through hearing his
banks bursting) and up-turned eye (caused by dodging
falling- securities). Westralia and Queensland produce
Fatmen of perhaps the most dangerous type of all—the
hearty, good-fellow type. But for myself I detest
the Tasmanian type most. You can tell him easily by his
habit of mopping himself with a handkerchief; he is a
vile sweater. South Australia and Maoriland Fats are
almost identical in their apparent Respectability' and
actual mealinesses. They are most commonly found at
the rail of the altar, where the sound of the loud Amen
is, as often as not, the sign of the unrepentant Thief.
SUBURBAN TYPES.
Father shows the visitor the pictures Pauline
painted all by herself.
William Leonard, one of the No License
candidates at last N.S.W. elections, and a fervent
assailant of the Drink Horror, was fined £3O last
week for selling beer in a private house—thus
demonstrating, yet again, the immemorial con-
nection between cold tea and sly grog. It
is cases such as William’s which hearten up
beer-lovers, and cause them to realise that, even
when the licensed publicans are exterminated, the
unlicensed Pharisees will still be there with the
alcoholic goods.
Ihe ravages of timber-getters on the north
coast of N. S. Wales are making sizeable
a, wo °d more and more difficult to obtain
in large quantities, and a heated argu-
ment with “the Crown” on the ques-
tion of supplies is raging. The tree-
hunters have made envious remarks,
concerning the reserves, and it is in
regard to one of these that matters
recently reached a crisis at Bellingen.
This particular reserve was applied
for by a well-to-do syndicate, and it
was whispered into the ear of the
vicinity that the application would be
granted, on the ground that, as
the reserve was
inaccessible, con-
siderable capital
would be required
to work it. The
timber-getters
promptly held an
indignation meet-
ing, and one of
the party under-
took to disprove
the inaccessibility
argument. Yoking up his bul-
locks, he set off for the highest
part of the reserve, hewed down
an immense vegetable and started
back. To his surprise, he found
that a mysterious party had tried
to block his return by felling adult
trees right across his track. How-
ever, with the help of an axe and
some double-edged blasphemy, he
carved his triumphant wav home,
and presented his proof that the
reserve was accessible even to one
r man and a bullock dray. In the
middle of the applau? from his mates,
the Grown arrived and cnarged him with
trespass. But, so far, it hasn’t charged
the other person who chopped down the other
vegetables. In fact, it hasn’t even asked the
other person what his sinful game was.
There were razors flying in the air last
week when the N.S.VV. Chief Secretary instructed
the police not to prosecute Sunday sellers of soft
drinks and fruit. The mildest things that were
said of the new Government were that it was a*
law-breaker, a Sabbath-breaker, a public enemy
and an institution which had sold its soul to the
Devil. As a matter of fact, it is difficult to see
that the situation is very much changed. There
is a rigid law against all Sunday trading. Yet it
is a well-known fact that certain shops have
for a long time had permission to trade on
Sunday, while others haven’t. How one lot
came to be selected in preference to another
was apparently arranged on the same prin-
ciple as the falling of the tower of Siloam—that
famous structure dropped on the people who
happened to be there, and those who weren’t
there it didn’t drop on. When the late Sunday
Brunker was left in charge for a short time
during Premier Reid’s absence he fell like an
avalanche on the Sabbath refreshment; but his
great “reform” only lasted about two weeks.
Apparently, Chief Secretary Macdonell is simply
going to disregard the Sabbatarian law a little more
openly than his predecessors. Probably the
simplest idea would be to get Parliament to
authorise the issue of licenses for Sunday trading
in the light refreshment business, and charge for
them.
The shirt of the late Edward VII. caused
many fervent loyalists to stampede to a church
fair in Launceston (Tas.) last week. The posses-
sion of another person’s shirt entails endless ex-
planation, especially if one wishes to publicly
exhibit it; therefore the advt. that called the
faithful together was careful to explain that the
garment once formed part of the dead Peace-
maker’s first trousseau, and that it was presented
to a cousin of the owner by one of the ladies-in-
waiting. How the lady-in-waiting got it wasn’t
explained ; the pedigree or explanation stopped
short at the threshold or back gate of the palace,
leaving one in an agony of .doubt. The last time
this writer lost a garment, he suspects that it was
taken by a lady-in-washing with pegs in her
mouth ; but there are probably other ways of
moulting a wardrobe.
* * * *
Adelaide’s mayor has been asking Premier
Verran to see if he can’t bring the importance of
the office under the notice of Ring George, so that
the city may in future have a gaud mayor ; and
Verran seems to have promised to tell the Agent-
General to abase himself before the throne and
pray George to be kind. This paper is beginning
to suspect that a set of rules ought to be drafted
on this subject of gaud mayors. There ought
to be, to begin with, an official smeller,
whose job it should be to go round and
sniff drains, etc. Three unsatisfactory sniffs
should disqualify that city for 12 months from
having a lord mayor. Or a scale of points should
be drawn up after the manner of the show-ring.
Twenty-five points might be allowed for the com-
plete absence of perfumery, 10 for the absence of
garbage in the streets, 20 for the presence of
decent pavements, 15 for traffic supervision,
and 50 for public utilities. Or, if that is too
elaborate, every city or town might be en-
titled to have a lord mayor whose inhabitants
had been broken of the habit of spitting on the
footpaths. A city with a lord in the town hall
and 10 miles of spittoons outside seems to this
paper to have a perverted idea of modern reform,
not to speak of civic dignity.

ST. PETER (to applicant): “ We have plenty of

Robert McNab, Maoriland’s ex-Minister
of Lands and foremost historical student, has just
damaged beyond repair the old yarn of a race
between French and English ships for the
sovereignty of the South Island. The yarn was
very circumstantial, too; it told how Governor
Hobson detained the French captain with enter-
tainments, while Stanley, of the British ship
Britomart, slipped off to Akaroa and hoisted the
flag. There is a monument at Akaroa telling all
about it; but now it seems that this monument,
like many others, is a base perverter. Stanley’s
log, Hobson’s despatches, and Lavelli’s despatches
to the French King have all been inspected by the
industrious McNab, and the pretty tale flies off
howling ; for, among other things, it is proved
that British sovereignty had previously been pro-
claimed at Cloudy Bay.
“MY LAWD!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Mayor of Adelaide (Mr. L. Cohen) waited upon the
Premier (Mr. Verran) to-da3’, to discuss certain details in
connection with the proposal to move the Imperial
authorities to issue letters patent to elevate the status of
Mayor of Adelaide to that of Lord Mayor, similar to Mel-
bourne and Sydney.
The Mayor pointed out that the proposal not only con-
cerned the status of the city but even more largely
affected the prestige of the whole State.
Mr. Verran expressed himself in entire sympathy.

In Memoriam advt. from the Barrier
Miner :
GOLAM MAHOMED, known here as GOLAM JOHN
aged about 33 years, passed away on Saturday, September
17, this year, at his own camel camp, North Broken Hill
after a short illness, from inflammation of the bowels!
Two doctors attended him, and the deceased received
best possible medical treatment; but at last he was called
away to his last resting-place, leaving his large number
of friends and relations to mourn the sad loss. By his
courteous manners and good disposition and honest and
straightforward conduct he made himself very popular
and well liked wherever he went ; and particu-
larly in this district he was idolised by his
countrymen and other friends, as he was known’ to be
a man of great strength and powerful muscles, and a
strong wrestler, although he spent most of his time in
attending to his large camel business here. Up to the.
last moments he was conscious, and conversed freely with
those around him, bearing very patiently the severe pain
he was suffering. He took ill on the road at Euriowie
coming to Broken Hill, where the writer met him. The
writer feels the loss and bereavement very keenly as he
loved him more than a brother. But we are ail’God’s
and must return to Him ; we must resign and submit to
His will. Gone, but never forgotten. Kassim. North
Broken Hill, October 20, 1910.
A LOT TO DO YET.
MR. WAYBACK (looking over the moated grange): “ Well, you've got a flash house all right,
but I don t see where the money you’ve spent on the grounds comes in. Why, ’ere's a

“ Miner”: Very little is heard of the
dingo nowadays; yet it is not so long since he
was a very real terror. Although neither so
strong nor so daring as the wolf, he was still
daring enough, sometimes, to attack man himself.
I remember once, during a severe winter in the
early ’6o’s, when the mounted mailman between
Wood’s Point and the Jamieson, ij» Gippsland
(Vic,),; was chased over the snowy ranges by a
pack of dingoes, made desperate by hunger.
Although he was armed, and repeatedly fired his
revolver amongst them, they followed him until
he arrived at the mining camp. On another occa-
sion—outside Coolgardie, in ’94—another mail-
man, this time on a bicycle, was chased for many
miles ; and the rider had the run of his life in
keeping at the head of the starving procession.

“ Leo Lear ” : The Victorian Closer
Settlement Board has pushed its spade into the
empty Western District, and dug out two estates,
which it will proceed to portion out to the earth-
hungry, Eumeralla East, of .10,000 acres, was
part of the vast Staughton heritage. The station
hedges in one side of the land-locked township of
Macarthur, the streets whereof show multitudes of
trees but dashed few inhabitants. Morvan,
8250 acres, close to Branxholme on the Port!
land line, was for years held by a bank ;
then the Phillips family bought it, and
raised a flukey hole to a healthy run,
and not many people will grudge them
the cash they made out of it. Branx-
holme is another of Vic.’s wilting
townships. Both Cumeralla and Mor-
van are within 50 miles of the waiting
piers of Portland, and under a common-
sense railway system the future produce
from them should move thither- wards.
But the chances are that until the
Commonwealth takes over the rail-
ways, or the State decides to charge
equitable rates on them, the produce will
go on moving in the old direction.

“ Williecan” employs a scientist:—
Australia for the white race ? No good to me ! “ Aus-
tralia for Australians ”is my motto now. I was adding- a
little to my currency on a shallow gold lead. Very soon
I found that being underground boss and winding engine
alternately was almost as strenuous as battling for a block
of earth in a Murray land-consultation ; so’l decided to
look around for a partner. The lead had a poor reputa-
tion locally, I found, so f had to fall back on an assisted
immigrant, who was journeying to.nowhere in particular.
Now, I’ve found most “importeds” have a failing,
and mine had his. He was an enthusiastic bug*
hunter, and hunted valiantly and consistently in
between buckets. (I had installed him as the winding
plant.) He soon acquired an extensive collection of the
small game of the neighborhood, but finally, having ex-
hausted the supply, settled down so well to graft that I
was beginning to think I had collected a first prize. Then
one afternoon I fouqd a loaded bucket returning to me
in great haste and without explanation. I managed to,
sidestep instantaneous destruction by l-16th of an inch
after which I clambered upstairs to ascertain the trouble.’
The trouble was careering across country in pursuit
apparently, of his fleeing sanity. He returned excited
and speechless; but presently, in reply to my abrupt
query: “Did he think he was a motor engine instead of
a winding winch? ”he gasped out: “ So’d you chase it,
too, if you’d seen the lovely grasshopper I was after.” I
went straight away and collected my rifle. When I came
back he had gone.
“0.K.”: The latest addition to Wirths’
menagerie impresses one with the utter absurdity
of the hippopotamus as a' zoological specimen.
An elephant in a zoo. or a circus is always turned
to useful account; but a hippo, is a ridiculous
creature. Its head is like unto a huge deformed
coal-scuttle, and its mouth, when open,
seems a wicked waste of space, considering
that the monster is only a moderate eater
of soft stuff—corn food, potatoes, etc. Its
molars may be necessary for grinding vege-
table tucker, but its terrible incisors and
curved tusky teeth are quite superfluous.
They are intended for rooting up long
grass from the ground, and devil a bit of
ground does a circus-bred hippo, ever get a
chance to tear at. Being a preposterous
beast, it sweats blood instead of water.
Anyway, the Wirths’ exhibit breaks out
in gory perspiration during the night and
after she awakes in the morning. The
prospect of a bath excites her to a profuse
perspiration ; then she wades into the water
and washes herself, free of . blood for the
rest of the day. This hippo, will be a trouble- .
some handful to the'Wirths, for she needs
a mighty big tank to be carried around with
her, and the instructions from Germany re
feeding her and driving her into the water
every morning cover two sheets of paper.
Her age is only three years (hippos, can live
fully 30 years in captivity), and she may
grow to be at least 12ft. long and sft. tall
at the shoulders. Thusfar she hasn’t been
tamed to even walk around the circus. She
can do nothing but loaf in .a big cage'and
look impossible. Luckily, her name—Lizzie
—has a special value for Melbourne advt.
purposes. For weeks before her arrival the
Clarke-Meynell advertisements of “Tom
Jones ’’ included a “gag”—“ Has anybody
seen Lizzie?’’—referring to an apochryphal
leech which Cromwell, as Benjamin Part-
ridge, professes to have lost from a bottle.
Wirths’ agent came along and jumped the
claim, so to speak, and now the hippo,
figures as the Lizzie who was originally men
tioned as missing.
“ C.A.8.” : Most people are of opinion
that “ bulldog ” ants attack immediately they" are
disturbed. But—well, this is a true story of a
honeymoon. The newly-married couple, travelling
to Sydney from Carcoar on their honeymoon,
arrived at Blayney to find they had about three-
quarters of an hour to wait for the through train
for Sydney. To fill in time they went for a walk
on the outskirts of the town, and sat down on a
log. Later, they returned to the railway station
and caught the Sydney express. Five minutes
afterwards the bridegroom felt ants biting for a,
wager under his leg-overcoats. He knew the breed
at once, and that they’d see it through unless they
were hauled off. Fortunately, he and his bride
had a compartment to themselves . . . but when he
was shaking the habitations of the ants out of the
window he dropped Them. Worse still, all the
luggage had been sent on ahead. It was the bride
who saved the situation. She rigged him out
in her underskirt, and when the train reached
Bathurst an excited individual dressed in a black
vest and frock coat on the upper decks, and an
old-gold petticoat beneath the loadline, streaked
for the nearest waiting-room in the station. There
he barricaded himself until his distracted and
confused bride went up town, and returned with
the nearest guess size to the Lost Trousers.

“F.G.,” concerning our black brother
Cain :
“Sammy" Mitchell, the new Judge of the Northern
Territory, had, at his first sessions, two cases of murder
which illustrate “ how the dead, men die ’’ in the Never-
never. In one case a white man’s harshness to a luhra
servant had roused her resentment to such an extent
that she appealed to the men of her tribe to kill him.
The plot vvas then and there formed. The lubra was to
secure the white man’s revolver, and this she did by
bringing it away in a billycan. The niggers then started
to round up the victim, and found him near his humpy.
Scenting mischief, he ran into the hut for his revolver.
It was gone. A minute later the pack was on him. One
of the murderers was shot while evading arrest, one es-
caped after arrest, two or three others are still wanted,
and two were sentenced to death. The other case was even
more cold-blooded. A poor devil, whose name is supposed
to be Egan, lost his way while journeying with two horses
from the Queensland border to Borroloola, and got 40
miles off the track into rough ranges. There-he was met
by a buck nigger and two gins, to whom he explained his
position. He showed them that he had ho gun, toma-
hawk or any other weapon, excepting a jagged and
handless razor-blade. He shook his tucker-bags and
billy to show their emptiness ; he begged for succor.
Patting the niggers on head and breast, he said-: “We
be all good friends," and asked them to take him to
Borroloola, where he would repay them. They pro-
mised. But, instead, they took him six miles further
away from the track, and there the buck speared him to
death. It was a difficult crime to trace, but Constables
Holland and Stott collected the murderer, and the Judge
ordered them a special reward for their fine bit of bush-
work.
