“Machete”: “To Babarantiko ” (B.
22/9/’10) denounces the Kanaka as dishonest,
untruthful, treacherous, etc. In the obsolete
term Kanaka he presumably includes all the
varying native races of the Pacific. His wail is
the ordinary agonised yowl of that type of white
who has invariably himself to blame for his
troubles as regards native races. The Pacific nig
is not at all worse than his white brother at pay-
ing his debts. The Chow, it may be remarked, is
an excellent “ pay ”; but this does not endear hirn
to us. As to Cocoanut’s truthfdlness, much de-
pends on how one goes about getting the informa-
tion. The practice, popular with, some people, of
“ belting it out of him,” is not conducive to good
results. Writer has examined hundreds of native
witnesses, and found them, if properly handled,
exceedingly truthful, even when' truth meant
trouble. Theinstances of devotion to white masters
by nativesubordinatesaresomanyand well authen-
ticated that there is no need to refer to Louis
Becke or William Macgregor for endorsement.
At the same time it may be generalised that the
wilder and less civilised our brown brother is the
closer he abides by.the elementary virtues. Homi-
cide is usually his besetting sin. It might lie
worse even than that. All I complain of is that
he occasionally kills the wrong man.

“ Pick ” : If “ Gil ” really wants to know
how long a rabbit can live without water I re-
ply—any time between three days and a week.
Rabbits cannot live without moisture, but, like
sheep and goats, they get enough from the dew
on the grass at night time. I have known sheep
to live for months in unwatered paddocks, and in
Wallaga inlet—a N.S.W. South .Coast arm of the
sea—there is a waterless island on which goats
have lived for years.
DINGO CREEK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Because we live at Dingo Greek, where bears and him yip
grow,
Where life is e’er the same old thing—processionally
slow,
They think, the folk who live in streets, we next to
nothing know.
Because our manners lack the grace otpeople we have
met ;
Because our potent energies in other paths.are set,
Beau Brummel speaks, us harshly re our “ lack of
etiquette.”
But we (that’s Bill and Jim and me, and Mary Jane and
Liz),
Although we’re not Superior we know the Bush’s biz—'
And if our ways are curious ways, thev’re quite as good
as his 1
We do not stride the Block all day, the Corso after tea,
take some other fellow’s goods—and treat them
shamelessly,
Nor do we talk the scandal-talk of Aristocracy.
We do not have to dodge all day from motor-cars and
trams,
Nor step aside at every stride for baby -laden prams,
Nor do we hear the pedants flaunt their polished
epigrams.
We do not have to hurt our eyes surveying dolls and
dudes; •
We do not have to close our doors, lest Babylon intrudes—
With politicians grinding out their dreary platitudes.
We do not live our lives in gasps, nor do we hear the hum
■Of myriad voices in our ears till we ourselves are dumb,
And wonder what this life is worth in Pandemonium.
We do not see the business man with breathless haste go
by,
As though he hadn’t time to live and hardly time to die,
When unsophisticated folk speak up and ask him why.
Ah, no, our lives are loitered out in havens far from
these—
t rom masculines in purple hose and peroxided shes,
-Remote from all the city’s strange and grim philosophies.
But we have time and space to see the world in which
we live ;
We’ve time and space and heart enough to borrow, lend
and give—
And breathe a prayer of pity for the city’s fugitive.
We’ve time to view the spacious skies and walk amidst
the miles
Of giant trees—the forest troops that guard the Bushlaiid
aisles,
Where scarce a thing obstructs our way, and Commerce
ne’er defiles.
Our lives are humdrum lives? They are—and humdrum
folk are we ;
But morn and eve and night and noon the gift is ours to
see
The giving Drama of the Barth with all its pageantry.
We’ve time at night to watch the stars, as did the ancient
seers ;
We’ve time enough to note their grand, illuminous
careers,
Pale flickering specks of light that are high Heaven’s
chandeliers.
Aye, distant from the trams and trains and vehicles that
roar,
Like breakers beating on some bleak and God-forgotten
shore,
Our souls have room to spread their wings ; our dreams
have room to soar.
N.S.W.

“A. William Cann ” disinters some old
memories :
I have been divagating back o’ Barringun, and hence
failed till to-day to see “ Jimmie Pannikin’s "impassioned
protest (B. 6/10/’10) against the scurvy attacks on the
bugle-blowing habits of Sunsetlanders. Says my old pal:
“ Many a weary hour is solaced by bugle-blowing.” So it
is. And they are no purloined, purchased, pawned or
pinched instruments that we big-boned, broad-beamed,
bullock-brained, backblocks battlers are accustomed to
sound. No sir. In the vast majority of cases the bugles are
the musicians’ own property. I wonder if James recalls a
pasear he took with mesome three yearsago. Imay men-
tion that the itinerary was from Crowl Creek by Nymagee
(N.S.W.), thence over Mt. Hopeless to Brewarrina, to
Milparinka and on to Mt. Hogarth (Vic.), to Kyabra (Q.),
and so to ltoma. A slightly divigatious journey, you may
say, and suggestive somewhat of a State Frighter’s ideal
transcontinental railway route ; but we were on a
wattle-forage at the time, and the golden glory of the
bush imposes on its pursuers, all too often, the winding,
uncertain-looking track of one in whom the liquor has
gone to the legs.
To get back to bugle-blowing. In every pub we visited
en route —and they were many—“ Pannikin " and I
blew our bugles deafeningly. Often and often did
we trail into the bar-parlor of some wayback
hostelry and find the locals discoursing on chest-
measurements, bush-lore or what not; and always we
butted into the conversation, produced our trumpets
and blew the comparatively-speaking inarticulate yokels
speechless. It was somewhere in the neighborhood of
Innamincka (Vic.), so far as I remember, that a horde of
beer-fanciers arose in exasperation, while “ Pannikin ”
and I were competing, one with the other, at this sport,
and flung us violently into the street; “for,” as they
vulgarly expressed it, “a brace of blowing, blanky
nuisances.”
After that we arranged to blow not our own, but each
other's bugles. A subtle plan, i’faith ; and it worked like
a charm. Thenceforth, in any centre where the appear-
ance of the inhabitants seemed to warrant it, I used to
sound James’s trumpet and he performed on mine ;
whereby immense kudos and public veneration
accrued to both. “By all means,” as my whilom travel-
mate happily puts it, “ let Sunsetlanders blow their
bugles to the last.” Indeed, it were not merely inhuman
but insanitary to cheek them. There are some I know
who must blow their bugles or burst; and exploded Out-
backers are notoriously unwholesome things to have
lying about the landscape.

“ J. 5.8.” : Be Ambrose Dyson’s swagman
and “Bridge-dwellers” remarks thereon (B.
6/10/’10). There are different styles of helping
Matilda on her way. A strap over each shoulder,
with the swag straight up and down the back, is
the Maoriland style, in spite of what “Bridge-
dweller” says. Horsecollar fashion, namely,
swag rolled long and small in circumference, with
the ends brought round and tied together so that
it resembles a large horsecollar, is the Queensland
style. (N.B. —This requires at least six saddle-
straps.) Two swags, lying horizontally, the one
in front somewhat smaller than the one behind
(S. A. style). The Matilda depicted by Ambrose
(B. l/9/’10) is done up in the N.S.W. fashion. But
there is one defect in the picture : the nose-
bag should hang over the opposite shoulder to
the one which the shoulder-strap crosses. If
Ambrose doubts my word, I’ll lay him three full
sets of ration-bags to two that he can’t carry his
swag in the N.S.W. style as drawn by himself
The nosebag must be tied to the strap encircling
the top of the swag, and come from there across
the back of the neck and over the opposite shoulder,
otherwise the whole contraption falls off as soon
as the owner commences to walk. A well-known
squatter was once accosted by a swaggie with a
request for rations. “If I gave rations to all you
fellows that come along, I’d soon be pushing
the knot myself,” he said. “ Well, take my tip,”
replied Swaggie, “when you make a start, carry
it horse-collar fashion. That’s the easiest way.”
And it is.
* * * *
“Jimmie Pannikin”: Be “The Scape-
goat” and the thrush as the friendliest bird in
the bush (B. 20/10/T0). For the last three
years a thrush (Australian mavis) has built
its nest and hatched its brood in a biscuit
tin (used as a flower-pot) on a bench, at
the door of the Provisional School, Rugby,
Back-o’-Burrowa. So friendly and so
utterly unafraid is the bird, that it does
not leave the nest when the school-
children march in and out, nor when
the little Nature-students stand at the
door and watch and discuss its color,
plumage, habits, etc. If the children
put their hands too often on the edge
of the tin, the sitting-bird gives a quiet
little peck, to show that it does not ap-
prove of too much familiarity. I have
seen the children feeding the bird while
it sat on the nest. I threatened to take
the bird, nest and eggs away with me, just
to see what the bush bairnies would say.
They did not say anything. The horror of
.the thing made them silence-stricken; but
they looked murder.
“Werri”: Nothing disgusts the bush
fisherman more than to be fooled by a turtle*
The amphibian is cunning ; he is an aggravating
nibbler ; he bites slowly and cautiously, cleaning
hook after hook without getting pricked. Some-
times he allows himself to be drawn nearly to the
surface, by way of encouraging the angler to feed
him ; then gives three cheers with his tail and
returns to his lodgings. If the line is cast in a
different spot to dodge him, turtle hunts round
until he finds its address, and presently he is
scratching at the front hook again. He loves
raw meat; he fairly revels in dead cow. I once
counted 45 perched on a carcase floating down the
Richmond (N.S.W.), and the water immediately
around was thick with them. Another time—l
think it was about midsummer—l counted 117
travelling overland, one behind the other, and
miles away from water. They had come from the
river, and were making for the inland lagoons.
There were dead cattle on the way, but none of
the travellers, so far as I could ascertain, stopped
for refreshments. Did anyone ever see a turtle
feeding while on hind ?

A Chinaman came into my pub at Broome with a piece
of string in one hand and a stick in the other, and called
for a dozen bottled beer. He tied six of the bottles by
the necks to one end of the pole and the other half-
dozen to the other end, and trotted off with the lot on
his shoulder. Watched him along to see if he would
, ~ . .. ... , ...
drop one unul he disappeared mto a shop with he
name of Ah Good over the door. Tim remark over the
door was probably sarcastic.
In a Chow’s supper-room I was waited upon by a Jap
girl. There was no ordinary bill of fare, but the menu
was on her cuff. This necessitated her putting her arm
around you while you picked out your dainty. It knows
more ihanone trick, does the Monkey race.

“R. 0. Dent”: Sometimes the country-
bred animal gets confidence-tricked just as badly
as the confiding bushman. A friend of ours in
the backblox once sent us a half-grown kitten,
which, as soon as it got acclimatised to the house,
started to haunt the pantry in a business-like way.
One night it met the mouse it was looking for, and
took it into the hall to play pool with it—or per-
haps the mouse took the cat, I’m not sure which.
The city inou«e humored the country cat cautiously
for a while; then started to edge towards the
drawing-room. By degrees the paw-billiard match
got close to the piano. The guileless cat couldn’t
see what was coming on, so it didn’t head the
mouse off. When the little grey spieler got close
enough, he dived under the piano, slid out the
other side and streaked for home. The country
cat sat by the piano and waited for the mouse to
come out again with the money it had borrowed.
It waited there doggedly until it was put out for
the night, and it was in with the milk in the
morning. For a whole week it hung round that
musical instrument exactly as the swindled bush-
man hangs round the pub through which the
trickster escaped with his savings. I felt darned
sorry for the cat; but as I didn’t know the cat
language I couldn’t explain to it that it had been
done, and that it was no use waiting for the brute
to come back. I’m sure that cat still thinks the
mouse will return some day by the hopeful way
he looks at the piano.
“ Giddap’s ” missing friends :
Anyone seen or heard anything- of “The Pebble ” lately
“ The Pebble ” and his dog, Huek Finn ? “ The Pebble,""
a few years back, was a Gentleman of the Road in South
Australia. The Peninsular copper towns—Moonta, Wal-
laroo and Kadina —were on his visiting list; but he some-
times drifted as far out as Petersburg and Port Augusta.
I’m not so anxious for information re “ The Peb.” as I am
about the dog. I covet that dog. “ The Pebble ” namedi
him Huck Finn partly for brevity and partly because he
was a street waif with no settled abode when he lassoed)
him. Huck’s owner was never troubled with a slack waist-
band whilst the dog was on deck. “The Peb.” would-
stand and gaze pensively in at the open window of a.
butcher’s shop with Huck at his heels, and then the
twain would wander off in search of a camp. Whilst the-
old man was getting the fire going, the dog would return,
to the butcher’s, carefully remove a joirit from a-book
and then scratch gravel back to his master. He never re-
turned empty-mouthed, and always showed a nice dis-
crimination in his choice of joints. Besides a large appe-
tite “ the Peb.” was afflicted with cold feet; so at night
Huck would stretch his woolly body over his master’s
daisy-roots, and all would be bliss. I want that dog—one
that looks after the commissariat by day and acts as a
foot-warmer by night is no slouch of a companion.
* * * *
“ The Second ” : I was taken to a fight,
lately. At least my friend, the Enthusiast, called
it that. There was a square, roped-in platform,,
with a sizzling electric light at each corner, set in.
the middle of a saucer of seats, tenanted by glum
sports, placid philosophers, and an occasional
blood-hungry dervish. About 10 minutes after
the time fixed for the disturbance, a man furtively
approached the platform and threw in four black
puddings. A glad howl went up from the der-
vishes, and a few chilled men clapped. Some
time after this stirring event a fire of applause
broke out; but it was suppressed without the fire
brigade. Four men with battered countenances,,
garbed in red sweaters, pants and braces, and>
bearing towels, bottles, buckets and sundries,,
walked through the crowd. In their wake-
came a fair young man in an old, old dress-
ing-gown. They clambered on to the plat-
form, and a man near me breathed hard
through his nose, as if laboring under strong ex-
citement. The young man took a chair in one
corner, and his attendants hung the wash-
ing on the line. A painful interval fol-
lowed ; then another burst of applause, and
the other competitor approached the square. He,
also, was garbed in an elderly bath-robe, and had.
four followers encumbered with washing and
bottled refreshment. The party ascended the-
platform and took a lease of the opposite corner.
The preparations for the fearful business were-
gone through as slowly as possible, including the
set-to-corners figure and the usual wrangle about
one of the young men’s stays. There was a.
faint flash of interest when one of the red-sweater-
men of the first contingent strolled over and-
thoughtfully stroked the hare, gleaming chest of
his boss’s adversary. Three dervishes howled
together, “Take that oil off !” After much ex-
postulation, one of the gentlemen with the vvash-
lng vigorously rubbed his principal’s shining-
bosom—and Honor was satisfied, or words-
to that effect. A spruce young man with
his hair parted in the middle and American-
magazine shoulders, called the rivals to
him, put his arms round them, dragged
them together, pushed them apart, shook his-
head, gave them his benediction and stepped
back. Someone hit a tea-tray and—the dreadful'
thing began. It turned out to be a family re-
union. The young man who had had his chest
rubbed rushed at the other, fell on his neck, and
put his arms round him. The spruce young man
rushed in and jealously pushed them apart. They
danced joyously awhile, played tiggy, flung their
arms abroad in ecstasy, then rushed together again
and waltzed like Sonia, the Alarm-clock Widow,
with Prince Dan Leno. Nothing was lacking but
the music. I got tired of watching it and looked
round indignantly for the band. There wasn’t any-
Suddenly I heard the dervishes howl. I looked
up and found that the taller young man had
fallen asleep on the floor. Someone counted as
high as “ Eight ” ; then a bit of washing was flung
into the square. The Enthusiast got up and said,
sadly, “Ah, well! better luck next time.” Next,
morning I read in my morning paper that I had
been watching a Thunderbolt. lam not going to
see any more Thunderbolts. If there is a Pork
Pie or a Pillowslip, and it comes out here, may I.
go to see it, But I won’t promise.
SUCH STUFF AS DREAMS ARE MADE OF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To-night I’ll dream because the night is mine
(I spend my nights and money as 1 please),
I’ve just consumed a glass or two of wine ;
I sniff the smell of lobsters on the breeze—
To-night I’ll dream !
To-night is mine and I am out for sport
(Much beer the doctors say induces gout).
But what of that? Have I not drunk a quart?
Also a pint or two of bottled stout ?
To-night rU dream!
The hour is late ; the pubs will soon close up.
And from their gleaming portals will be led
The last few joyous drainers of the Cup,
To gaily prance with lobsters home to bed.
They, too, will dream 1
I spy a Dago fish-shop ’cross the street,
Wherein sad, greenish oysters lie instate.
Ah, now indeed, I feel that I must eat!
A brick-red lobster sprawls upon a plate—
A perfect dream 1
I’ve got that lobster underneath my arm.
I feel him squirming ! He is not yet dead 1
I’ll have a whisky—it will do no harm 1
Then home by undulating streets to bed,
To dream my dream 1

PERSONAL
ITEMS
John Logan Campbell, Auckland’s benefi-
cent brewer, has just added another gift to the
many for which the city has to thank him. A
site was secured by public subscription for a
central kindergarten, and Campbell and his wife
have put up the building and furnished it. The
old gentleman is well into his nineties; yet it is
only his partially-lost eyesight that has latterly
caused him to drop his annual birthday jaunt to
the top of Rangitoto, the harbor’s extinct volcano.
John M. Me Lachlan, formerly a draper
of Auckland (M.L.), has by his will left the
Cornwallis estate, of about 1800 acres, as an
addition to the Waitakerei national park. It was
on this property that one of the first North Island
settlements was established; and here the
McLachlans landed in 1841. The settlement was,
however, a failure. The land had been purchased
by one Mitchell, a missionary, from the Maoris
for some tobacco ; but the tobacco was so bad they
couldn’t smoke it. They repudiated the sale
owing to the vileness of the tobacco, and long dis-
putes followed over the ownership. J. M.
McLachlan finally secured the main part of it,
and has now added his name to those of Hori
Grey, J. L. Campbell, Muckelvie, Knox, Costley
and other Auckland benefactors.
Conan Doyle, the novelist, has made pub-
lic announcement that George V. is, in his
opinion, “the strongest and big-
gest man mentally who ever
ascended the throne of England.”
It is wonderful how the breed
of sovereigns keeps improving.
Alfred the Gieat was some
pumpkins in his wav.
Harold 11. was described
as a man who kept up his
end ofascrap with energy,
and it required more than
two policemen to run
him in. William I. was
spoken of as the greatest
soldier and statesman of
Europe, if not the world.
Henry 11. reconstructed
England, and some items
of his system were so
good that they survive
to this day. Elizabeth
was a red-headed female
who shook things up.
and William 111. didn’t
let the grass grow under
his feet. The late Vic-
toria was described by
a frenzied admirer sis “the
greatest personality the
world has seen since the days
of Jesus Christ,” though
she is hardly ever mentioned
now. Then came Edward
VII., who was understood to
beat the band ; and finally, George
V. bangs Bannagher. If the
royal family keeps improving at)
this rate, it will presently develop
wings, and the second or third
monarch from now will be tin
aeroplane or a condor or a sky-
scraper or something too elevated
to be perceived by the naked eye.
Randolph Bedford’s M.L. lecturing tour is
temporarily off. The call of the Bullfinch sounded
so alluringly in his ears that he betook himself, his
umbrageous hat and his gentle voice to W. A.
with promptitude and a sanguine spirit.
“Kaitangata ” introduces a friend :
The writer of that hangman par (B. 27/10/’iO) errs as to
the late “Nosey Bob’s” aversion to pubs. The bars at
Darlinghurst knew him well, and at the Bondi Cliff
House, when Dunlop kept it, he was a frequent and
favored guest. Many were the marrow-chilling reminis-
cences I heard him indulge in at Bondi over a friendly
beer. He did gloat over the turning-off of murderer Archer,
for the Burton-street outrage ; but in me, who knew the
hideous details of the case, he found a sympathetic spirit.
Robert’s noseless condition was occasioned by a kick from
his favorite cab mare ; and it was the gibes of his fellow
jarvies that drove him to the drop. A kindly soul was
Robert, despite his trade, and an ardent lover of flowers,
and the joy of growing them.
The last chaplain of the awful penal settle-
ment of Port Arthur, Van Diemen’s Land, died
in Victoria on Saturday. He was the Rev.
Roland Haywood, and he carried some grim
memories along with him to the age of 75.
The death of Harry Rawson leaves five ex-
Governors of N. S. Wales living. Belmore, who
served from 1868 to 1872, is now 75 years of age ;
Loftus, with over six years’ service (1879-85), is
93; and Jersey, who found two years quite
enough (1891-93), is 65. Carrington (1885-90) is
in his 68th year. The fifth is the youngster
Beauchamp.
Sol Green, the big bookmaker, admits to
winning a modest £30,000 over Comedy King’s
victory in the Melbourne Cup ; but the long-
suffering army of punters prefers to call it £50,000.
Solomon was not always arrayed in so much glory.
Not so many years ago he was a carter for a
brother-Jew \yho ran a new and second-hand furni-
ture business. And, to his credit, he isn’t ashamed
of his battling either.
Sydney’s gaud-Mayorship comes up for
its annual discussion on December 9. Retiring
Mayor Allen Taylor is being pressed to re-
nominate, and his re-election is a possibility.
R. D. Meagher is known to cast longing eyes at
the chair as a step towards hislife’s work—the re-
storation of his name on the roll of Gents One ;
while A. C. Cocks, who lately won the St.
Leonards Parliamentary seat on the second
ballot, will be an early bidder for the chain and
the rabbit-skin.
Byron Moore, Secretary of the V.R.C., is
likely to retire. Both Bagot and the present
double-barrelled poet (he has also dabbled in fairy
tales) were ignorant of racing. When Bagot
passed out, the turfites—the grand old gamblers
and the last-leggers—came with a rush for the
billet. But they were quickly turned down;
“no racing man need apply.” (Judkins is enter-
ing that up.) This is reckoned as so well-estab-
lished a rule that when Byron Moore retires the
horsey will hardly dare to ask.
Men get their thrills all sorts of ways.
There isone in Gippsland, named Irons, who finds
his delirious joy in coming down with felled trees.
He insists that falling with a high gum is much
more delightful than tumbling out of an airship,
and presents fewer risks. He selects a suitable
limb on the side opposite to the direction in which
the tree is to fall, swings from the lithe branch,
and comes down with obvious enjoyment and so
far without a scratch. But his friends have great
expectations.
F. A. Coghlan is the new Under-Secretary
to the Chief Secretary in Ma State, the clumsily-
worded billet being the old Principal Under-
Secretaryship, in which Critchett Walker formerly
ruled the service with a rod of iron. Nowadays,
the holder of the office has a couple of other billets
thrown in to make him of the requisite weight to
draw his £lOOO a year. F. A. Coghlan is not
T. A. Coghlan, the Agent-General, and is not
even a relation; yet he cannot persuade the
general public that he isn’t in London at the
present moment. The new Under-Secretary
joined the service in 1875, and, judging by his
appearance to-day, must have been about five
years old at the time. .., y.
THE RETURN OF THE LEG-IRONS.
H. D. Morton, the new Independent
member for the Hastings (N.S.W.), is the sort of
man to take the fancy of a country constituency.
He is au excellent speaker, and has forgotten
more about the farmer, his wants and liis ways,
than the average country representative ever
learned. He was the first to make a good financial
tiling out of show jumpers. Desmond and a
dozen mote wonders were secured by him in their
prime, and sent round to scoop up every A. H.
and P. show prize that was going. In his spare
time, he helps to manage Coolnngatta, the Hays’
big place near Berry. Mark Morton, M.L.A., is
a brother; another is Phillip, one-time city
alderman.
A MEMORY OF THE RACING CARNIVAL.
WAITRESS (in all good faith, and without the slightest desire to awaken unpleasant memories):
“ Apple-pie won."
Died in Adelaide Captain “Jack” War-
ren, whose white beard and vigorous language
were known on most mining fields of Australia.
The best mine he ever managed was Broken Hill
Block 10: it paid about a .million in di vs. in his
day. on a paid capital of fewer ha’pence. “ Captain
Jack ” had a rough tongue and a hard fist; but if
you prospected in the right place, you could find
a very soft heart somewhere in that big body of
his.
Melbourne had its Judkins in the genera-
tion before last. His name was Milton. In
1855 he went to Sturt, P.M., and says he : “ Your
Worship, there is a horrible exhibition, called a
Spider Dance, given by a female named Monte/.,
at the Theatre Royal.” Sturt: “Indeed!”
(Judges and magistrates never know anything.)
Milton: “Oh, yes!” Sturt: “Please describe
what you saw.” Milton: “I never saw any-
thing.” Sturt: “ What is your complaint, then ? ”
Milton: “I am informed ” . Sturt: “Oh,
you know, that won’t do in this court. You
must see her.” The agonised Milton went away
streaming with perspiration, and Lola was left in
peace. Coppin seized on the advt. He put on
a burlesque, and impersonated Lola Monte/, in
the Spider Dance. Boys shied pennies at him,
for which he “ pulled ” some in the police court.
The Hon. George Coppin was as touchy over
this reminiscence as Irving was over his having
once acted an Old Woman Witch in an Edin-
burgh pantomine.
Cup Week was the opportunity for a
number of ancients to foregather at a Melbourne
cafe and tell what wild dogs they were in the
“Golden ’Fifties.” The finger of Time tickled
them to happy reminiscences of the adventures
that befell them when rushing for gold, or the
adventures of other people which they had now
commandeered as their own. One old gentleman
spoke of Julia Matthews, the comic-opera lady
(who doesn’t date back more than 40 years, by the
way), whereat several other old gentleman
smacked their lips. Everything in the garden
was lovely when those pioneers were dawgs.
Even “lynching” was a gay joke. The victims
were “ merely run up a gum-tree branch and let
down again.” One venerable raconteur heaved a
sigh of admiration for a bushranger who once
robbed him of £3 12s. 6d. and gave the money
back. This was the reminiscence of parson H. F.
Scott, the present vicar of old St. James’s, in
Little Collins-street, who remembers Melbourne’s
historic tabernacle when all the land thereabouts
was bush, and trained jackasses rang a merry peal
of laughter on Sunday morning by way of sum-
moning worshippers to church.
“Pressman’s” reminiscence :
In spite of his years, the late Gov. Rawson never
owned up to the weaknesses of old age. He had
strong nerves, and I think he often brooded over the
fact that opening Parliament and other soirees was
no way to use them. Shortly before Gov.-General
Northcote left Australia he was anxious to have a
look at the big Barren Jack irrigation dam, and
Rawson was one of a party, arranged by Solemn Lee (then
Minister for Works), for the trip. The morning of arrival
was clear and cold, and I do not remember having seen
the old Admiral looking so fresh as he did when he stood
beside the electric machinery high up on the side of the
precipice. Across the gulch a cable was stretched, from
which swung a box-lift, and when the old mariner was
told that Solemn and De Burgh (the engineer) and one
or two others were going in that box down to where
the workmen looked no bigger than lobsters, he wanted
to go too —wanted it more than anything else he could
think of just at the time. But Solemn and De Burgh
wouldn’t think of it; and when I heard them urging the
responsibility on their shoulders to return to Sydney
with the King’s representative intact, I began to
feel that / didn’t want to go aeroplaning at that height
above sea-level, anyhow. But the old Viking wanted
a thrill real bad, and Solemn sang out, “Let her go,”
just in time to prevent the old man from scrambling
over the tail end of the wooden balloon. The last thing
I observed on the shore platform was the disappointed
look on his face, and the first words we heard as we
soared again to the top was his eager inquiry : “ What
was it like? Did you enjoy it?” Of course, we all said
it was a dandy trip—a feilow couldn’t make weak admis-
sions to that granite-nerved old gentleman—but next
time there is a vacancy in that swooping box-kite it
won’t be me who will fill it up. When we started to
ascend I looked aloft at the cable — and it wasn’t there!
Somebody had replaced it with a cobweb.
Acting Prime Minister W. M. Hughes gets
more of the adventitious advt. than an operatic
star. If his brown cow hasn’t calved, somebody
has fired a writ at him*for assault; and when he
isn’t being sarcastic in Parliament he is defending
a ludicrous police-court action for alleged damage
to a sofa. Only one person reported that his pocket
was picked at Fleminarton ; and, of course, that
one was Hughes. Still, all he lost was a cheque
signed by Frank Tudor ; and he didn’t lose even
that, as somebody picked it up and restored it.
England is getting, or thinks it is getting,
a new view of its George V. A dramatist wrote
a play which was accepted by a London manager.
It was duly forwarded to the Chamberlain, but
that functionary refused his approval, because the
play had references to George IY. in it. The
references do not seem to have been uncompli-
mentary, but the Chamberlain holds that there
can be no mention of George IV. on the stage
while George V. reigns. Harcourt, M. P., has
written to the Times, in a beautifiilly-sarcastic
tone, backing up the dramatist; but the absurd
embargo remains. Apparently it is a nervous fad
of George V., who knows what an infernally bad
egg the previous George was and wants to
smother him up.
A man of small things was one Smith, of
Sydney, who was recently examined in bank-
ruptcy. He was a small man, aged 22. He was
living with his mother—presumably a small
woman. He was out of a job, though he said he
could get work at 15s. or 20s. a week, which sug-
gested that he put a small estimate on his own
value. His wife, from whom he was separated,
was suing him for the small amount of 15s.- He
had gone bankrupt over the very obscure sum of
275. fid., balance due on a suit of clothes. And
almost alongside the tale of these small doings of
Smith’s was a Cablegram about a £40,000,000 job
or monetary shortage in America. They sug-
gested an ant and an Alp;
The less a high official has to do the more
he gets for doing it. Compare Chelmsford, of
N.S.W., with Macquarie, of the same State. One
draws £5OOO a year; the other drew £2OOO. Yet
Macquarie had all Australia on his shoulders. He
was Captain-General of the country and its
dependencies. He was the Parliament, the Land
Board, the Marine Board and the Board of
Health. He managed public works, roads and
bridges, was stock and traffic commissioner, and
architect and street engineer. He was bead of the
police. It was he who rode over and marked out
the streets from Charlotte Place to Dawes Point,
and from Wynvard Square to Darling Harbor. -He
fixed up markets for the pumpkin and cabbage
growers, and personally tested their scales and
prices. For 10 hours a day his work was arduous.
Affixing bis signature to documents he regarded
as the recreation of “off-time.” On the other
hand Chelmsford, with £5OOO, is not the Parlia-
ment or municipal council or health board, or
anything else useful. Advising young girls to
learn cooking, and old girls to avoid putting dead
poultry in their hats, comprises his round of ser-
vices. Affixing his signature cons, nes a little
time, though the Speaker or Chief Justice could
do that part of the business in a few minutes of
each day, and do it just as well.
THE DEAD ADMIRAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where are the wooden ships of ’Fifty-seven V
Where are their shotted guns?
Maybe, O Shipmate, in some sea of Heaven
Their sullen thunder runs.
Never again shall mortal seamen cheer them,
Or watch their topsails sink,
Wond’ring the while to what far fight they steer them
Over the great world’s brink.
The dust lies thick upon the yellow pages
That count their victories ;
With newer ships and guns the struggle rages
On the eternal seas.
He worked them all—from frigate to destroyer ;
Both sail and steam he knew ;
He served all well—the nation, his employer,
His foemen and his crew.
O Ships and Guns ! Like springtime grasses vernal
Ere you can turn again
Time claims them all. This is alone eternal—
The spirit of such men.
N.S.W.

the WILD CAT
COUMN
The States have gone through four months of
their new financial year, and, though the Commonwealth
owes them a bit on account of the Braddon clause, they
ean still show bigger revenue figures than for the cor-
responding months of 1909. For October the record, ran
this way :
Oct., 1909. Oct., 1910.
N. S. Wales .. £1,229,450 £1,286,799 Inc. £57,349
Victoria .. 676,564 814,285 Inc. 137,721
Queensland .. 830,378 393,143 Inc. 62,765
VVestralia .. 240,005 278,841 Inc. 38,836
Tasmania .. 77,425 95,394 Inc. 17,969
The States are still entitled to three-fourths of the net
revenue from Customs and Excise. So far, the Common-
wealth has paid £1,985,957, and has left £1,280,271 to be
made up later on, when the money is available. Until
this payment is made a comparison of the States’ revenues
with those of last year is not worth very much.
Even without this deferred payment N.S.W.
has scored £149,843 more in total revenue this year, Vic-
toria £268,309 more, and Queensland £197,480 more. In
every case the railways are very largely responsible for
the booming revenue. On the one month’s figures the
railway situation is as follows :
N. S. Wales .. £588,243 Inc. £38,403
Victoria .. 394,123 Inc. 29,614
Queensland .. 242,044 Inc. 36,730
Tasmania.. .. 24,342 Inc. 4,000
But though that makes the revenue figures look as
though they were wearing a halo, all the glitter isn’t
gold ; for there has been a big addition to the interest
burden on account of new lines, and, generally, working'
expenses are heavier. While, therefore, the total
revenue from all sources in N. S. Wales in the four
months is £149,843 greater, the ordinary expenditure
has been £256,547 greater than it was in the correspond-
ing four months of last year. What the loan expendi-
ture has been it is impossible to say. In all £581.662 has
been charged to the account, as against £585,196 last
year; but the Wade Government rushed on numerous
works, and, though progress payments have actually been
made, they are not debited to the loan account until the
works have been formally authorised by Parliament.
Victoria, as usual, only shows what is coming in. Queens-
land, which at this time last year, had £55.7,279
saved up towards the year’s interest, has this
year got £791,384 in the stocking. Its loan
expenditure is, however, still booming along. ~~ln four
months last year it was £469,181 ; this year, £563,600,- or
at the shocking rate of £1,690,800 a year. Westralia,
which had been doing a bit better, fell back again list
month ; with an income of £278,841, it paid out £309,982,
and thus brought its accumulated deficit up to £115,236
—not all, however, this year’s. It wants a few Bull-
finches badly.

Oct., 1909.. 177,566 14 „ ~ 112,000 15,000 1,727,472
Apl., 1910.. 193,722 16 „ „ 128,000 18,000 1,775,194
Sixteen per cent. ! Not even in the boom-time round
about 1890 did the Bank of Australasia do such a thing
as that. In 1889 and 1890 the div. went to 15 per cent. ;
and we have to go right back to 1859 to find anything
better. There has been an amazing recovery in the past
15 years. In 1895 the shareholder’s little bite got down to
5 per cent., and stayed there for three ye.yrs ; then it began
to grow, and it has gone on growing until the present
16 per cent, is reached. There is a little uneasiness about
this matter, by the way ; bankers who believe they know
Australian conditions better than the London directors
of the ’Asia and the Union have an uncomfortable feel-
ing with regard to dividends in excess of 10 per cent.
Australian banks which might have paid more on the old
capital have therefore strengthened their position by
issuing new capital, and paying the full 10 per
cent, dividend upon new capital and old alike.
The ’Asia has, on the contrary, got in no new
capital since 1884. Not, of course, that its posi-
tion has not been strengthened during that period.
The formal reserve fund then was £750,000 ; to-day it is
£1,760,000. And the bank has been building up reserves
which don’t appear as such. Its premises account stands
now at only £150,843 ; the real value of the properties is,
no doubt, a million, for in many cases the premises in-
clude offices hired to the public. And there probably is
an internal reserve as well. There certainly was a few
years ago. Out of it the new Sydney premises were paid
for ; it is said that not a penny of that valuable asset was
charged to premises account or shown otherwise in the
balance-sheet.
Naturally, in view of the excellence of Aus-
tralian conditions, there has been no break in the pro-
gress during the past 12 months. This shows the
movement of the chief items :
April, 1909. April, 1910.
Notes .. .. £552,741 £514,008 Dec. £38,733
Bills, etc. .. 2,173,217 2,589,632 Inc. 416,415
Deposits .. 16,179,907 17,000,503 Inc. 820,596
Liquid Assets .. 7,105,402 8,435,058 Inc. 1,329,656
Advances .. 15.005.992 15,021,437-. Inc. 12,445
Premises .. 180,378 150,843 , Dec. 29,535
The least satisfactory feature from the profit-earning
point of view is the very small growth of advances :
the bank has had over £1,300,000 more of the
public’s money and its own accumulated profits
to dispose of, but it has only lent out £12,445.
Evidently it hasn’t been able to find suitable securities.
It might possibly be able to lend out more money on
land ; but most prudent Australian bankers don’t feel too
comfortable about the present price of Australian land.
Anyway, the ’Asia has either kept the bulk of the new
money in the safe, or put it into Government securities,
so that it has no less than 42 per cent, of all its liabilities
represented by these liquid assets. Further, its own
capital and visible assets represent slightly over £1 for
every £6 of the public’s money in the business. Under
those circumstances, the rate of earnings on capital and
reserves.shouldn’t be very.large. Yet it is :
Oct., 1906 ..£l9 11 11 p.c. Oct., 1908 ..£ll 16 9 p.c.
Apl., 1907 .. 15 10 10 „ Apl., 1909 .. 10 15 10 ~
Oct., 1907 .. 12 9 5 ~ Oct., 1909 .. 10 16 9 ~
Apl., 1908 .. 13 4 10 ~ Apl., 1910 .. 11 12 10 „
That means that every £lOO of its own money, aided by
rather less than £6OO of the public’s money (42 per cent,
of which is in liquid assets), is earning £lll2§. lOd. a year.
If, all round, it makes § per cent, clear on the public’s
money, this means again that its Own money is 'eaTiltng
£7 2s. lOd. per cent. Part of the explanation of this very
high return, no doubt, is that the bank has a quantity of
remunerative reserves which are not- visible ; and, more-
over, on account of the undoubted strength of its posi-
tion, it is helped to a very liberal plateful of the cream of
the Australian banking business.

This is a case in which one or two fires more or less make
the profits look bad or good : the co. isn’t big enough to
let the law of averages assert itself properly in so
short a space as a year. The thing that should
give most comfort to shareholders is that, even in
the worst of the past 10 years, a very fair profit has
been left to lay by after paying the dividend. This isn’t,
of course, all due to underwriting. One of the advantages
of the fire-insurance business,is that you don’t hay,e to
invest your capital in goods and pack them on shelves'
where they go on earning nothing while they get eaten
by moths. This co., for instance, has £37,500 of
capital and £36,634 of reserves, or a total of
£74,134 ; and of that lot £69,856 is out on mort-
gage, and last year’s interest was £3553, or very
nearly enough to pay the 10 per cent, dividend. True,
there oughtn’t to be that much out' on .mortgage ; ior jf
the co. were hit hard by a big disaster, it might be very
troublesome to get the money in fast enough to pay the
claims. For the reason mentioned above, the loss rate is
very uneven ; yet, in the worst of the past 10 years, an
underwriting profit has been shown —that is to say, pre-
miums have been more than enough to pay losses and
expenses;—

St. Joseph’s Investment and Building Society

Assets written up £28,588, which amount
was transferred to capital.
1907 .. 3978 4000 272
1908 .. 4438 £6654 £2809 8,555
1909 ... 5071 1420 2858 —' -12,1 b"S
1910 .. 5015 367 2858 14,712
When Sydney’s last land boom raged, nearly 20 years
ago, this society’s shares were £lO paid. There was a
fine air of prosperity about the premises at that time.
Besides its own money the society had lent out about
£330,000 of other people’s. When the other people
—inconsiderate after the manner of other people-
called for their money, it wasn’t there. They asked for
cash, and all they could be offered was a stone —several
stones. These heaps of stones, and lands upon which
other heaps of stones might be put, fell in upon the
society wholesale. The borrowers who had pledged them
as security couldn’t pay what they still owed, and the
society had to take over the depreciated securities. \ The
society had no uncalled capital to get in, so shareholders
were spared the pain of paying up. But they had to agree
to write off £7 of every £lO share; that much was sup-
posed to be lost. Meantime depositors agreed to put
up with 3 p.c. interest. On the whole, things turned out
very well. The country has, of course, been going to the
dogs for years; yet, somehow, people with money have
been giving steadily' increasing prices for properties.
Apparently they reckon that, the Jcennel is a very good
place for a country to go to. In time, therefore—three
years ago, to be more exact—there was a revaluation of
the remaining assets ; and it was found they were under-
valued so much that the shares could be, it was
believed, safely written up £3. So the capital was
restored from £28,588 to £57,175. At'yhat time the de-
posits had all been paid, off except about £70,000. On
the increased capital the average profit', from ordinary
business during the past three years has been an almost
exact 8J per cent.—a pretty good indication that the re-
valuation was not beyond tbbmark. To further support
the same idea, there has beep a steady surplus on the
realisation of properties. Last year’s ordinary trading
profits, as shown by the table, are rather less than those
for the preceding year ; but that is explained by a trans-
fer of £524 to an accrued interest account. This is the
outcome of a stricter system of accounting.
The directors point out now that the reserves
would allow of another 30s. per share being added to the
capital. They are very wise, though, not to do any more
writing-up of that kind just now. It will be time enough
when Sydney has passed through thesluhip that is bound
to follow the present run of good seasons, high prices,
and plethoraof money. If thesociety escapes trouble then,
well and good. If it doesn’t, it will look better to write
off reserves than capital. And, anyhow, there probably
isn’t going to be any great future for building societies of
this pattern, enabling them to pay substantial dividends
on big capital. Starr-Bowketts are cutting in on one
side, and the extension of the functions of the State Bank
is cutting in on the other side ; and the ordinary build-
ing society will not get fat in competing with these two.

In 1903 this co. decided that it had been a short-sighted
ass. All the available coal it had left in the mine was
80,000 tons; and its financial position was as follows:
Liabilities, £5050 ; liquid assets, £6823. Opinion was
divided as to What should be done. There were pref.
shareholders as well as ordinaries ; and, curiously enough,
that saved the situation, for the ordinaries saw that if
the thing were woundup they wouldn’t get anything at all.
They- therefore urged the directors to get busy ; and the
directors did. A new mine was secured at Cessnock ;
and the profit on the old one and from the hire of trucks
was put into-the new one—the Neath. Developments
were very satisfactory, and, by transferring the plant
from the old mine, the new one was equipped without
running far intodebt, On Sept, 90 the position was—Lia-
bilities, £22,085 ■; liquid assets, £25,132. The new mine has
thus been bought, equipped and developed, and a short
branch railway put in, and the financial position is better
than it was when the horrible disclosure Was made in 1903.
Of course, no dividends have been paid meantime ; more-
over £5170 has been received in calls. But, even so, it is
an extraordinary performance. A question has now
arisen regarding dividends; and as counsel couldn’t
answer it, “ friendly proceedings in Equity have now
been commenced By a holder of ordinary shares to
restrain the distribution of these nett profits entirely
amongst the pref. shareholders, and the directors are,
therefore, obliged to carry the amount forward.” As a
matter of fact, the credit balance at profit and loss is
.only £I69S, as. shown above ; while the actual cash in
hand is £1 4s. Bd. It wouldn’t do any harm, therefore,
if the amount were carried forward for a year or two,
especially as the Northern coalfield isn’t at all likely to
have for many a day as thriving a half-year as it has
just passed through ;for in this half-year reserves have
had to be built up after the long strike, in addition to
the supply of current needs. The co. surely doesn’t want
to be a short-sighted ass twice.

The Langridge lost less of its head in the Melbourne boom
than any other building society, and it stands out con-
spicuously for having kept no depositor waiting on the
mat. All things considered, that was a really remark-
able record ; for, of course, this society couldn’t carry
along on ’normal lines with banks, etc., tumbling
all around it, and thousands of properties flung
on the market. Still, it only hid to drop dividends and
make an arrangement with its investing shareholders—a
species of long-term depositors. When the trouble
occurred in 1893 it had £-i1,2690f reserves : it transferred
the lot to a contingency fund, and as losses have been
made on the sale of foreclosed securities, old and new, it
has written the amount off this fund. To-day the fund
stands at £8474 ; but it has been fed to a small extent by
the appropriation of current profits. The reserves in the
table are described as nominal: that is because this con-
tingency fund is included. Without that the reserves
total £13,478. And quite likely some of the contingency
fund is solid too; for the foreclosed properties now amount
to only £24,997, and £8474 looks a good deal more then
enough to provide for any loss on that lot. As the old
assets have been realised the money has been put out in
new advances on building-society terms. This shows the
history since the accounts were presented in their present
form :

-The modesty of the liabilities is still a striking feature of
the accounts. The capital in permanent arid paid shares
is £69,587 ; and the total of all liabilities to the public and
to investing shareholders is only £25,923. Under those
circumstances, the society ought to be able to command
very cheap money. The dividend is not big ; and with
such comparatively little risk as the shareholder has to
take, he can’t expect it otherwise. The society is, how-
ever, working itself into a position to be able to comfort-
ably pay another 1 per cent., unless something very bad
happens to Melbourne.

This little society isn’t to be recommended to anybody
who wants to get rich in 24 hours ; but the small Bris-
bane capitalist-rthe few shillings a man—who
wants a reasonable return with little risk might do many
worse things than give it consideration. The risks are
small because it is not bovpowingrat all on deposit. The
investor’s share is its only form of borrowing ; and that
is the form of investment that was mentioned just now
in the ear of the little man. The society has no foreclosed
properties in hand ; and, though its reserves are hot big,
they are mounting steadily. Business is also moving
along steadily ; advances have shown these progressive
totals—l9oB,'£6s7l ; 1909, £7553 ; 1910, £8457. On its
present appearance and past record, the modest little
thrift-promoter deserves well.

The co. doesn’t tell much about itself ; among other
things, the value of estates in hand isn’t disclosed.
Like practically all trustee cos., it makes one charge on
the capital value or corpus of an estate (from 1 to 2J per
cent.), and an annual charge of 2£ per cent, on income.
Some cos. spread out the corpus commission • over
many years, and thus make the profits look fairly
uniform ; others drop in the big commission in
lumps. Apparently the Tasmanian Permanent is one
of the latter ; hence a single year’s figures are
nothing to go by. The paid capital is £10,000; clients’
interests are protected not only by that and the reserves,
but also by £40,000 of uncalled capital; and of that
£25,000 cannot be called up by the co., but must
as a reserve liability for the special protection of
clients. Of the capital and reserves £5OOO is on deposit
with the Treasury ; and £12,381 is on mortgage. Interest
now very nearly pays the dividend, leaving the profit
from the business to go to reserves. The co. has two
risks—(l) from the misinterpretation of a will or other
instructions; (2) from a slump in its own investments. If
- it escapes these, the steadily increasing reserves.should
assure, at least, the present dividend.

Gordon House is a “ model lodging-house ” property,
which this co. took over from another co. that tried to
mix business and philanthropy. The present co. is only
philanthropic after business hours, so to speak. Cer-
tainly it made a much better start than the old co., for it
took over the property at a low valuation, and the money
it couldn’t pay it got on easy terms from the Savings Bank.
That part of its profits which the co. is not spending on
the premises or dividing among shareholders, it is using
to pay off the mortgage. The following shows the pro-
gress it has made :
1904. 1909. 1910.
Liabilities .. £11,794 £BB7O £B6lO
Cash, etc. .. 32 73 119
Deficit .. £11,762 £8797 £8491
That is an improvement of £3271 in six years, which lifts
(say) £125 of interest off the co.’s shoulders. Dividends
have been rushed along too fast; but if they are not put
any higher, and if Melbourne has a few more good years,
the growth of the reserves and the further disappearance
of debt should put the co. in a position to go on paying a
fair dividend. In view of the probable depreciation of
the property it is a pity, though, that more was not paid
off the mortgage before the dividend was put past 8 per
cent.; for a mortgage, though its appetite may not
attract attention in ordinary times, seems a most vora-
cious lodger when the slump-time comes and there isn’t
much in the cupboard.

Four days after the accounts .closed on March 31, a div.
at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum was paid—the first
this co. ever scored. And even that orphan was a mis-
take. The management is an apparently incurable opti-
mist. “During the period under review the mine has
been hampered in its operations in the first place for
want of miners, and then by ventilation troubles.”
This is putting the vehicle in front of the quadruped.
The ventilation was bad ; hence the miners preferred to
work somewhere else. Some improvement has been made ;
but still the mine isn’t rushed. There is another factor at
work:, however. A practice has grown up at Broken Hill
of shutting down some properties in times when metals
£re cheap and profits poor ; and the cos. will have to pay
for it. Miners will give that kind of field a wide berth ;
and they would be fools if they didn’t. It costs a good
’ few pounds for a man to go and take his family and
chattels to an out-of-the-way mining field ; and if he is
liable to be thrown out of work at every downward plunge
of the metal market, the more prudent the man —and,
generally speaking, the better, the miner—the less dis-
posed he will be to go to the expense. Unfortunately,
other cos. which do struggle on whether the markets be
good or bad, suffer for the delinquencies of the rest.
To get back to the Junction North. When it
isn’t men and ventilation, it is something else. It always
has been something for the last 20 years. The truth is
rarely told. That truth is that the ore, of which there is
a very moderate reserve, is hard and fractious. The mill
recoveries for the past half-year, compared with those of
12 months ago, have been as follows :
Lead. Silver.
Sept., 1909 .. 67 - 0 per cent. 48’0 per cent.
Sept., 1910 .. 70-2 „ 49’7
That is better. But now consider the costs per ton of
ore :
Mining. Milling.
Sept., 1909 .. .. 12s. 3d. ss. Id.
Sept., 1910 .. .. 13s. 7d. 6s. Id.
Thus less than half the silver and but little more than
two-thirds of the lead are recovered at a milling cost of
over 65., as against (say) 3s. 9d. at the South, where the
recoveries are better. Further, on account of the
peculiarly intractable nature of the Junction North ore,
the wear and tear of the plant is exceedingly heavy ; and
this extra cost is riot taken into account in the foregoing-
figures.
The ore treated last “Ijalf-year amounted to
68,584 tons. The manager estimates that there are
322,000 tons standing in the Nos. 9, 10 and 11 levels.
“Below No. 11 a large tonnage of ore will be mined,
but there is not enough information to calculate
it in the reserves.” Twelve months ago it was
said: “The new level at 1334 ft. may be ex-
pected to open up at least 700,000 tons.” The
management has since learned the old lesson about
counting chickens. So a remark drops in casually : “The
main western crosscut advanced from 202 ft. to 308 ft. in
schist, but did not prove the expected ore body.” Then
they went up to 1 the 1134 ft. level and started stabbing
the country with the drill; and if shareholders will turn
to the published plans (section 15) they will see what has
happened. And it w on’t give them any comfort.

The Hydro Electric and Metals, Ltd., with a
capital of £500,000, is being formed by its parent, the
Complex Ore Co., which has been doing a lot of experi-
mental work, on a large test plant in Melbourne, in zinc
electrolysis. The alleged result of these experiments is
that one h.p. produces two tons zinc per annum ; and, if
it’s only a question of horse-power, the export of con-
centrates to foreign smelters—it was nearly 350,000 tons
last year—should see a healthy decrease. For the co. has
rights under a Tasmanian Act to the sole use of the
Great Lake and the waters of the Ouse and Shannon
down to their junction, and the Great Lake is
said to have a potentiality of 100,000 h.p. There
are 25,000 h.p. in use within 75 miles of the generating
station, and the co. says the cost of h.p. in Tas. (mostly
from wood, coal and oil) is £3O to £35 per h.p. per
annum, and that it can sell power'at from £8 to £lO
and make a big profit; Realty, h.p. cost varies according
to its use. Thfe Tasmania mine, at Beaconsfield, for in-
stance, uses 1500 h.p., derived from Newcastle coal, at a
cost of £27 per h.p. per'annUm. But, then, it works all
the way round the clock. The less continuous the load
the greater the proportionate cost.
The co. has contracts for public lighting of
Hobart, Glenorchy, Newtown and Qneenborough, and
with private light and power provided for out of the first
unit of 5000 h.p., there will be only 2000 h.p. left for the
co.’s metallurgical work, at Nor’-West Bay. Capital cost
of the first unit is put at £164,200. The 2000 h.p. thus
available would only treat 200 tons or so of zinc concen-
trates per week. On a basis of 600 tons a week—which
would call for all the power available from the first unit—
the co.’s figures show a return of £178,000 per
annum, allowing for 80 per cent, recovery of
zinc, but not deducting management costs, in-
terest, depreciation, sinking fund, or realisation
costs, which are sizeable items. On the other
hand, lead and silver recoveries should also be sizeable.
The cost of zinc concentrates is taken at £4 per ton at
the works, allowing for a crude concentrate worth from
42 to 45 per cent. 8 per cent, lead, and 15oz. silver.

The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “ Wilfle.v.” It is continuous, and con-
sists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged in a
tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The drum is
covered with a filter cloth supported on grooves on the
drum, which are connected by means of holes to the
interior to allow the liquid to be withdrawn. Through
the trunnions at the ends of the drum the vacuum pipe
passes to the interior. This pipe connects with the
interior of the drum, through which the liquid and air
are withdrawn by .means of a vacuum pump. The filtered
slime is removed by a simple apparatus placed at or
below the level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly
the cake is removed the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolVes is immediately re-immersed in the pulp ready to
receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the sucking in
of air, and consequent loss of vacuum between the time
the cake is removed and a fresh one is forming. The
residual liquid in the slime cake is removed by distribu-
ting water over the surface of the semi-dried cake. Owing
to the fact that the filter is circular in form, it will be
seen that it is continuous in operation, and has no com-
plication of valves to. get out of order ; furthermore,
work is being done continuously during the 'whole revolu-
tion of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of Squth Melbourne, have the sole manufactu-
ring rights of this filter.* [2

Roasting, it is reckoned, will cost 10s., dissolving 55.,
labor 55., smelting residues os., smelting zinc 2s. , refining
bullion 55., electrical energy 6s. and contingencies 3s. 6d.
per ton of concentrates. A plant to treat 600 tons a
week will cost £75,000; and a beginning is to be made
with a 200-ton plant at a cost of £30,000.
The co. has other things in vjew ; in fact, the
uses to which electrical power can be put are unlimited.
Thus, Australia consumes over 5000 tons calcium carbide
a year and 4000 h.p. is required for its manufacture. In
the countries of its origin it costs £6 per ton, and one
h.p. per annum will make lj tons carbide, which sells
wholesale at about £l4 a ton. Every State of Australia
has deposits of high-grade iron ore ; one h.p. produces
three tons pig, and electrical furnace pig is equal
to the best Swedish charcoal product. Converted
to mild steel or rails it is worth (say) £8 10s.
a ton. Ten tons of swamp gum tested at British paper
mills produced high-grade writing paper, and wood suit-
able for pulp for newspaper work exists in large quanti-
ties in Tasmania. A Bull co. is to start manufacture
at Nor’-VVest Bay, and has signed a contract for electric
po\ver. The Hydro Electric and Metals will, it is said,
begin life with £225,000 underwritten, in an issue of
100,000 shares and £125,000 5 per cent, debentures.
* * * *
Some policyholders of the Colonial Mutual Life
have started an agitation for a committee to examine the
society’s affairs. The Colonial Mutual has been in a very
poor way for years ; and it; recently bought the business
of the Standard at a price which must have given the
shareholders a shock. Apparently the deal
was carried through by the Colonial Mutual on the
principle that two bad eggs will make one good chicken
“Which they won’t; not this setting season, anyway.
The weakness of the “mutual” system of life assurance
is that in the case of a wildly careless society like the
Colonial Mutual, everybody’s business is nobody’s busi-
ness ; the policyholder doesn’t feel that he is a share-
holder, because his dividends are not payable before
death ; and management sinks into the hands of careless
nonentities with good names, or old women, or the noisy
minority that always wants a job. And the Colonial
Mutual, having got into a bad way 20 years ago, could
stand that sort of management less even than an office
can usually stand it. An inquiry can’t therefore come
too soon.
Having got the contract for refining the Lyell
blister-copper output, the Electrolytic Smelting and Re-
fining Co. at Port Kembla (N.S. W.) is just-about doubling
part of its plant. Until thisco. started the only people who
refined copper in Australia were the Oobar and Wallaroo
cos., and they only did it for themselves. All the copper-
gold product from Mount Morgan, Mount Lyell, Chillagoe,
Cloncurry and other Australian mines went to foreign
works, mostly American. Even now more than two-
thirds of it goes abroad ; but the proportion will be a good
deal less when the Lyell product goes to the right
place. At the start the yarn was that the work, which
calls for a good deal of skill, couldn’t be done in Australia.
The work not only is being done in Australia, but it is
also being done so well that the refin'ed product is classed
with the world’s best. In fact, there are only two or
three brands which some very particular German electri-
cal works will use ; and the E. S. and It. is one of them.
Nor has there been any wholesale importation either of
men or of machinery. General-manager Magnus was
brought from the'other side ; and, so far as men are con-
cerned, that is about the lot. Foremen are almost
without exception young Australians ; and other
young Australians are being trained. There is a tech-
nical college alongside the works, arid Magnus is said to
be as proud of it as he is of his refinery. Practically
every lad in the works is a student, and youngsters who
saw a .navvy’s life looming ahead of them now see lucra-
tive billets opening up. And the co. helps them to get
those billets by paying their school-fees and looking
kindly on the foremen, etc., who come along and give in-
struction. The fact that the foremen have mostly
climbed up that way is a further incentive.
Up to now there probably hasn’t been much in
the business for the co. But there has been a good deal in
it for Australia. Roughly, every ton of blister copper that
goes out of Australia to be refined does'nirm Australians
out ofw job—‘six directly and three (coal miners, railway
men, etc.) indirectly. The cost of the process is probably
from £4 to £5 per ton. The cos. in the old days paid a
great deal more than that, though. In fact, it is alleged
that, in the old days of the Great Oobar 00., before the
syndicate took over the property, the gold wasn’t paid
for at all. That plum helped to fatten Germany.
And down till quite lately very much larger lumps than
£5 were lopped off the value of Australian gold-copper
bullion by the foreign refiner. The usual thing hap-
pened, however, when Australian works arrived : down
dropped the foreigners’ charges. In other words, up
went the net price realised by the Australian mining cos.
When the Mount Kembla people recently got the Lyell
contract, the representatives of foreign refiners said that
the Australian co. must lose money on it. They admit
that to get that contract they themselves cut right to
the bone. They probably cut deeper even than that.
There are two sorts of “ dumping.” The foreigner can
kill a baby Australian industry by pouring in goods at
under-cost price, expecting to make up the loss when the
baby is dead ; or he can kill it by outbidding the
baby industry for its raw products. The latter
isn’t a scheme that can always be worked ; but when it
can be worked it is dangerous, because the producer
isn’t always a patriot—he believes in selling to the
foreigner if the foreigner will pay a better price, and he
won’t see why the foreigner sometimes pays the better
price. There are, no doubt, a few who would sell to the
foreigner, anyhow, even if the Australian offer was as
good. That sort of man or co. is beyond argument; the
only way to talk to him is with an export duty on his
unfinished products.

The East Greta (N.S.W.) coal shareholders had
some of the trouble they expected at their meeting last
week. The convention lasted from 3 to 7. Ralston,
K. 0., was on hand to do some special pleading for the
directors and others whose proposed deal with the co.
had caused all the trouble; and there was relief all
round when it was whispered that “the Reformers”
hadn’t a criminal prosecution in their mind. Three
directors had to be elected : the ballot resulted in the
choice of Earp, Yuill and Clift; the last two are new men.
* * * *
As far as the Kyloe (N.S.W.) has gone, the lode
in the bottom level isn’t keeping up to its value in the
levels above. The first 45 tons broken out assayed only
5 - l per cent.; and at Kyloe that is perilously close to the
non-paying line.
* * * *
The Grafton copper mine, Cangai (N.S.W.), is
going in for tramway equipment. The tram will supply
the smelter with firewood at (it is hoped) a lower cost,
besides avoiding the constant stoppage of supplies when
the roads become heavy. During September the Cangai
reverbs treated 330 tons ore for 91 tons matte, containing
37 tons copper. Most of the ore was obtained-—nomin-
ally at least —in the course of development work.
* * * *
Main Ridge Deep Lead, Rockley (N.S.W.), has
treated 65 fathoms of wash dirt for 61oz. gold. If that
return represented an average for a large area there
vvould be some froth on the beer ; but unfortunately the
gold is very unevenly distrimited, and the various engi-
neers who have reported on the property agree that costs
are likely to be high. So the beer is flat as Sahara.
Various estimates have been made about the future cost
of mining and treatment if certain alterations are made
to the plant ; but none of these estimates encourage the
hope that the wide areas of poor gravel can be.made to
give a profit. Only the best portions of the lead will pay
to work, even if costs are reduced to Blakemore’s estimate
of 325. per fathom.
* * * *
Queen Bee (N.S.W.) is at last'to get some of
the capital it has been long in need of. The necessary
resolutions have been passed ; and underwriters are at
the back of the issue.
* * * *
Balmain (Sydney Harbor) colliery will be a good
deal heard of before long. It is now in a position to
make a big output, and there probably isn’t better
bunkering coal in Australia.
* * * *
Mount Morgan has got through its change-over
troubles ; and it will now begin to show the value of the
Manj’ Peaks flux. The last three months’ record has been
as follows, taking copper at £5O per ton August,
£49,386 ; Sept., £79,113 ; Oct., £39,918. The quantity of
gold-copper ore put into the furnaces during the month
was 18,644 tons, the best for the half-year. The copper
product was 670 tons ; next month should pass 700 tons ;
and by December the co. should settle down to a steady
800 tons or thereabouts. Hitherto Morgan’s costs have
been high ; but, unless the calculations are all wrong, the
use of Many Peaks metal-bearing flux should pull them
down considerably. Apart from the fact that the co.
will no longer be putting through barren ironstone, the
Peaks stuff contains some sulphur, and that ought to
reduce the hitherto heavy coke bill.
* * * *
Brilliant Deep, Charters Towers, continues to
prospect its section of the Brilliant reef above and below
the 2413 ft. level. Last half-year 953 tons crushed for
£4437. The returns averaged £4 10s. 2d. ; but the last
crushing of 205 tpns only gave £3 12s. 2d. per ton. This
won’t brighten up the situation very milch ; and it wants
brightening, for the co. is in debt.
* * * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, is developing
well on tbe eastern Day Dawn reef. So far the rise above
No. 6, near the Freehold boundary, has proved 220 ft. of
backs, mostly very payable stone. Mills’ Unit'ed was the
first to pick up this eastern section of the Day Dawn.
But, though the reef has been worked so extensively,
the levels below 1600 ft. vertical give little sup-
port to the theory that this reef is pitching rapidly
to the east, which was the opinion put forward some
time since by supporters of the N. B. Freehold.
Recent developments in the Mexican indicate that this
somewhat erratic Day Dawn shoot is pitching to the west,
which was the case with the original Day Dawn reef
(discovered in the Day Dawn P. 0.). The strengthening
of the market for Mexican shares is undoubtedly due to
the opinion that the reef is pitching westwards, and that
No. 4 will open up satisfcictorily. Between the shaft and
the boundary there is a reasonable chance of picking up
this heavily mineralised shoot of stone above No. 4 level.
Whether the Victory or any other lease will get the shoot
beyond the Mexican’s boundary remains to be seen.
* * . * , *
Blucher’s Victory, at the Broughton, Charters
Towers, is in 30,000 shares, all contributing. The last
return from 117 tons gave a good-looking £6 15s. 9d. per
ton. At the third half-yearly meeting there was'£73o in
the till and 30 tons of stone in hand. The recent crush-
ings have come from No. 3 level ; but the reef there has
been erratic and patchy, with a tendency to split up into
leaders. Still, a winze below No. 3 shows a strong reef in
the sink ; so the opening of No. 4 level is being watched
with a good deal of hopefulness. The'Cumberland reef,
in the same locality, has given a small fortune to its
owners. 1
* * * * ,
East Oriental, Gvmpie (Q.), crushed 1125 tons
for 13630z.,' making the third good return on end. A
dividend of 4d. follows. At the mine a tyrge body of
crushing stuff is showing in the intermediate level above
2025 ft. The rich patches of coarse gold and specimens
make in close proximity to slides or crosscourses. The
East Oriental and Scottish Gym,pie are the deepest mines
on the field, but No. 2 S. Great Eastern is sinking its
main shaft to try its luck on the Inglewood reef at depth.
The latest return from South Glanmire and Monkland
was 658 tons for 3280 z. This co. is opening the Inglewood
reef at the9lsft. level, with fairlygood results. It is, how-
ever, a mullocky showat times, so that the stone requires
picking. Work has also been started again pn the Ingle-
wood from the western shaft. The South Glanmir.e is
next to No. 2’S. Great 1 Eastern, so it is naturally in-
terested in the prospecting of the deeper levels. Alto-
gether there is a more hopeful feeling about Gympie,
which latterly )iap. bqen doing a lop, ,of valuable prospect-
ing and development work.
* * * *
Hampden (Q.) has impressed the Baillieus, the
reports from both the cos.’ mines having lately been very
cheerful, and they have either underwritten orgot-under-
writing for the debenture issue to pay for the Duchess
railway. The co.’s financial position is therefore assured.
The Queensland Government has not yet taken
over the management of the Etheridge line (147 miles),
but will shortly do so. This line cost £414,843, or under
£3OOO per mile ; so if Einasleigh and Etheridge go ahead,
the railway should for a time at least show very good re-
sults. The Chillagoe Co. has acquired some lead mines
at Mosquito Creek, and a number of men are now raising
ore for the Chillagoe smelter, which, by,the way, has also
put on more hands to deal with the bigger ship-
ments from Mungana, Einasleigh, etc. Chillagoe has
had such a bad time all these years, and has ac-
cumulated such a pile of debt, that it Can do
very well with more cheerful surroundings. Its
trouble is that the cheerful surroundings have hitherto
only lasted long enough to allow the insiders to get out
on a rise, to come in again later on when Gloom has once
more descended on the co.’s affairs.
* * * *■
Philip Charley, who has been poking holes into
Queensland at Trvphinia, 70 or 80 miles from Rock-
hampton, has put his coal prospects there into a co. It
is to be called the Charley Coal Mines Proprietary,
Ltd. A good slice of the State has been taken over—23
coal-prospecting blocks of a square mile each, and four
mineral leases of 320 acres each. Near by a small co. has
been probing for coal at, Stanley, and has struck it at a
shallow depth. The combustible is believed to be a con-
tinuation of Philip Charley’s finds. Hitherto Rockhamp-
ton has only had sheep-runs in its big back yard ; but it
threatens to some day grow as much coal as wool.
* * * *
The George Syndicate, which holds an option
over some tin properties .about 80 miles north of Charters
Towers, appears to be on a pretty good thing, A condi-
tion of the option is that operations are to be confined to
development work. So far, 150 tons of ore have been
raised ; 50 tons will go 10 per cent., and the whole will
average over-6 per cent. The ore-buying Lemprieres
are largely interested in the option, There is a mill
about eight miles away, on Stockyard Creek, owned by
the late tributor of the mine. He cleaned up about 100
tons the other day for eight tons of tin oxide, which was
sold in the Towers for £lOO per ton. The metal appears
to be carried by an intrusive dyke in granitei
Bananaland’.s Agent-General, reporting from
the Great Fog, advises senders of opal to supply the
stones in matrix form, as “the exporters don’t always
cut opals to meet the ideas of London buyers.'’ Queens-
land sapphires, especially if of light and uniform shades,
are, he says, always picked up at good prices. From
March to the end of July is the best time to hit J. Bull’s
market, as then the dollar-covered American is poking
about with his own • and other 1 people’s expensive
daughters.
* * * *
Things are not as bright on the Central Queens-
land sapphire fields as they might be. Miners are quitting
Sapphiretown, as the old ,surface ground is getting ex-
hausted ; and at Ruby Vale the sinking becomes deeper,
and the output is uneven. The Mines Department has
agreed to help’ to test deeper ground at Sapphiretown,
where hitherto most gems have been got on a false
bottom. The strata beneath is composed of large water-
worn boulders, which require blasting; and tbe gouger’s
private banking account won’t run to that expense.
* * * *
The Bullfinch has got a perch in London. The
new co. is in 500,000 shares, of which 450,000 are issued ;
the other 50,000 are apparently reserved, and it will pro-
bably turn out that some sort ’of option has been given
over them. The little Doolette-Shallcross-Jones syndi-
cate takes 300,000 shares and £lOO,OOO cash ; and that
leaves £50,000 for working capital. The business was
fixed up in London on Wednesday and Thursday, arid
there were some wild dealings in the shares on those
days. Two vyires quoted £5 and £3 ; but when things
settled down on Friday, shares found their level at 355.,
which meant £787,500 for what, after all, is no more than
a surface show. But even that wasn’t enough ;so the
quote was swiftly lashed along to 555.
From Southern Cross : “Such a re-union of the
old Coolgardie deadbeats ! I thought they were gone
for good, most of them, long since. Heaven only knows
where they’ve been dossing. They haven’t had the
chance of'Such a gold-brick flutter for 18 years. Don’t
take it from this that it’s all gold-brick. Two or three
very decent mining centres may get a start.”
. * * * *
This is a fair sample of the Bullfinch madness :
A Melbourne syndicate was formed. The boss prospector
left. Shares rose £l. He wired that he was at Perth.
Another £1 rise. Arrived at Southern Cross. Quoted
£2 higher. The Bulletin hasn’t heard what happened
after that. Most likely the man wired that he was too
late to get anything.
* * * *
Gordon Dickson retires from the Cassilis (Vic.)
with honors, to take up £l2OO a year and, incidentally,
chase gold shows round Rhodesia for a fatman crowd.
His benediction is in the form of a discourse on the Cas-
silis, and it is so inspiring that every shareholder takes it
into his closet to commune over in secret. Dickson, while
at the Cassilis, went for costs-saving as well as gold-savings.
One idea he brought from the West was the Murphy stope
drill to bring into service stone that had been left in the
stopes. He also pitched in the filter press that 8., M.
and Co. use to handle slimes. One way and another, he
reckons up ore reserves in the Cassilfs at 15,000 tons,
from which the co. is to net £7500. Then £ll,OOO is to
come from the slimes and £BOOO from the chlorination
residues. Total £26,500. And profits! ’Ye gods, no
wonder the Dickson boat was blazing in rainbow colors
when the Henley ffite was on! The £26,500 is badly
wanted, for the co. has never got its financial balance
right since the time it struck its electrical scheme.
Nicholas and Coote will both want to see how Gordon’s
figures pan out in the chlorine vat. His successor is
A. M. Drummond, of the Northern Mines (W.A.), so the
B. influence is still felt in the Cassilis parlor.
* * * *
Lansell interests in Bendigo are being shaken
up. The jumping of the Sheepshead block is being
followed up by an application for the forfeiture of
the eastern lease of the Virginia Co., in which the
estate is a big holder, along with another half-
dozen of Bendigo’s magnates.' The co. got the lease
in 1906, and has done little or no work on it since,
though during that time many a good shilling has gone
into the Fatmen’s pockets from the other workings. See-
ing that the block covers the Paddy’s Gully and some of
the Derby side-lines, a bit of the cash surplus ought to
have been used to open up the neglected area. First the
A.M.A. went on the warpath, and now the pegs are in on
behalf of some one else. The co. asks Minister Mcßride
for another chance. The story is that it can’t pick up a
shaft site. Um ! If the co. is shot off the lease it will have
its own supineness to thank for the loss.
