A. 1., Gaffney’s, up in the Vie. mountains, has
hit a floor just when, by the oddest coincidence, its pro-
moter, Georgy Dickenson, had pulled off a pot on-the
Diamond King, and the Bullfinch had struck it rich at
Yilgarn. Dickenson’s chest is out to the breeze, for to
hit a floor at the A.I. may mean more than a
patch. Talk of assays ! In the old days thfe
A. 1., Rose of Denmark and Dempsey’s were so rich
that the whole district had a very good time. The
stroke of a pick may expose another jeweller’s shop. Of
course, it is frightfully patchy. A.I. got on to the rocks
after Field and the Millers raked out the last floor, and
it took a lot.of push to make a Melbourne crowd think
that there was more than machinery leavings on 'the
claim. To hit on another niche of gold in a few months
gives hopes for the other abandoned Woods Point minqs
that once showed more dazzling riches than half a dozen
Bullfinches. ,

The latest shove-off at Bendigo is the New Red,
White and Blue United to work the Sheepshead Extended
lease. The co. is in 40,000 shares of Is. each, and the
promoters take the whole of them. Now, ’tis said that a
favored few are to be let in at 10s. At this point the
Bendigo Stock Exchange pops . up with a pro-
posal to treat the whole lot 'as vendors’ scrip and
prohibit dealing in ; them for six months. If the pro-
moters made them 10s. shares, and issued (say.) 24,000
shares to the public at 10s., and took £lO,OOO-in cash,
the Exchange wouldn’t hang those 24,000 shares up. Yet
there doesn’t seen) much difference between the two cases.
As to the show itself: It has a shaft 1400 ft. deep to tell a
tale of past disappointment. But it also has a Chance of
picking up the continuation of the Central Blue’s shallow
shoot, and with more sinking it may hit the North Blue’s
shoot. So it is a double-barrelled gamble. ■
* * , * *
Maori Queen,-one of the first of Ovens River
(Vic.) dredging cos., paid £2 on its £2O shares last half-
year; and half of the time only'one dredge was working.
The old original No. 1 dredge has been dismantled, and
the machinery has been put on to a new No. 1. Besides
paying the dividends (£3300), the half-year’s operations
allowed £3251 to be,written off the old plant; and even
then there still remained an extra £5O to add to the
profit and loss balance. The net liabilities on September
30 were £1959.

The Tasmanian mining tip of the hour is North
Dundas, where Sligo has been hatching tin eggs for 10
years .past without bringing out a brood till now. The
very latest from London is that John Henry, of Mount
Lyell fame, has planted the Dreadnought sections in the
lap of J. Bull) and that, the hard -stuff is put up for
plant. Bull is said to have jumped at the chance to get
in on bedrock. He only had to put up 10 thou,
to the credit of working capital to rope in 60,000 shares
out of the 80,000 that comprise the bag of tricks. The
market wonders if Bowes Kelly got a hint of Henry’s
luck, or probable luck, for he acquired an option over
Sligo’s blocks. Should Bull be on the feed, will he too try
to fill the British paunch—in return for a nice little cash
consideration and share interest?
* * * *
Mt. Lyell cannp.tafford a sixpenny bonus this
year ; the thing would cost £30,009, for every penny
added to the dividend mops up £5OOO, so that the Is. 3d.
the shareholders get calls for £75,000. For the six
months the furnaces used up 198,000 tons of ore in the
production of 4080 tons of blister. It required an aver-
age of approximately 481 tons of ore to produce a ton of
blister, which shows that the stuff handled has been of
lower grade than usual. It is also probable that the
raising of pyritic ore from underground is proving more
expensive than quarrying from the open cut. Nominally
the big blow still contains a large tonnage of ore, but it
is| not so accessible as it used to be. And the copper
market is sick.

Captain “Teddy” Miles, the Tasmanian, is
having a game with the Tongkah bears. After Currie
and Wharton had dropped in their little pill, Tongkahs
shrivelled to under half a sovereign ; and Captain Teddy
let them shrivel. Then he went along to Siam and
Singapore. The bears were (and fqr that matter still are)
so sure of the reports made for the Baillieus, the Lem-
prieres and other people that they' went on selling shares
gaily. Some of the strongest Tasmanians lost heart and
wouldn’t support the market any more. The puzzle after
that was who was buying? Miles seems: to have given
the tip to two lots of friends—some in Tasmania, the
others in Singapore ; and through the latter there have
been' some solid purchases on Chinese account. -Then
came the new dredges, and with Captain Teddy on the
spot the month’s output of tin was brushed up to 110
tops. Jus,t about that time Captain Ted and his friends
wanted the overdue scrip badly ; and the bears have had
a cold time finding it. From a few shillings the price
was pushed up to 355.' But nothing more seems to be
known of the extent of-the tin-deposit than was the case
when Currie and Wharton compounded their pill. There
never was a doubt that some tin could be got, and got
quickly. The point that still remains unsettled is, What
area is there of it?

Maoriland dredging doesn’t boom as it did a
few years ago ; there are now no phenomenal Hartley and
Riley returns. But a good number of dredges are still at
work, with weekly returns ranging from oOoz. down to
12oz: and 15oz. These are chiefly in Otago and Southland.
Few of the West Coast dredges remain alive.
*■** *■
The coal centre of Blair Athol, near Cler-
mont (Q.), is gradually shaping- itself. About 40
miners are on the field, and.about 1200 tons a week are
being raised. One co. has visions of an electric plant,
and an output of 1000 tons a day. The railway people
use Blair Athol coal along the Central and Mount Morgan
lines, while Rockhampton is already developing a habit
of grilling the family' sausage with it.

A. C. Rowlandson, of the N.S.W. Bookstalls,
whose “ Bookstall ” series now runs to over three dozen
volumes, has the joy of announcing that up to date he
has put off more than 500,000 copies of these “written,
illustrated and printed in Australia ” books. Steele Rudd
is the most prolific contributor to the series with 10
pieces dropped on to Rowlandson’s collection plate. T. E.
Spencer, who is kept pretty busy these days
presiding over N.S.W. Wages Boards, has found
time to add three to his original “How McDougall
Topped the Score.” A. C. Rowlandson doesn’t pretend
to have any abnormal scent for literature; but he is
making an Australian market for good, honest, interesting
and mostly bright Australian stuff ; and dozens of Aus-
tralian writers give him three cheers for offering them the
chance to supply it.

Bless your soul, l don’t mind
how many meals a day 1 cook
so long as the missus isn’t stingy
with the tea —1 must have a cup
first thing in the mornin’, at
eleven o’clock, and another about
three. You’ve no idea what a lot
of work there is in a kitchen —»
there’s hardly a break in the
day, for as soon as one lot’s
cleared away you have to start
gettin’ ready for the next, and
so it goes on from mornin’
* to night.
The meanest thing t know Is
for a missus to get two sorts of
tea —rubbish for the kitchen and
another for inside. Kitchen tea
is nearly always the sort that
leaves a nasty taste in your
mouth, or else it’s no better
than chopped-up straw —no use
to anyone, much less a woman
who has to stand over a stove
all day like me.
1 don’t stand any nonsense
when 1 goes to a new place,
l tells the missus straight l won’t
drink any other tea but R o b u r
No. 1 Grade, and if she doesn’t
agree to get it 1 don’t go —and
do you know after a bit they
all gets to like it just as much
as l do, and lots of them
has said how much obliged they
are to me for introducing such
good tea to ’em, and most of
’em has 'told me it’s the best
they’ve ever had. And you’ll
say so too if ever you make up
your mind to use it.
The Cook.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, November 8, 1910.
My Dear Artni,—
Our late and well-beloved Sir Harry Raw3on
has heard the trumpet sound “from tne hid
battlements of Eternity.” A fortnight since a
Sydney man had a letter from him saying he had
just returned from a trip, and was feeling “never
better in his life.” The only official link between
the late Excellency and Sydney was his honorary
colonelship of the Second Australian Infantry
Regiment. That body has been consequently put
into mourning by its colonel, Ramaciotti.
Mr. Harry Morton, the new member for Hast-
ings and Macleay, is a brother of Mr. Phillip
Morton and one of the trustees of the vast Berry
estate. He used to be a bank manager in the
constituency he now represents, and in that
capacity met his wife, who was one of the Dangars
of Kempsey. I see that Mr. Morton has frankly
informed the Sydney daily papers that he will do
nothing to put the present N.S.W. Labor Govern-
ment out of office —provided it doesn’t do what
there isn’t the slightest chance of it attempt-
ing to do in his time. Truly, the world is
changing. Who, even as recently as five years
back, would have visualised an ex-bank manager,
a Berry estate trustee, openly announcing that he
meant to give his sympathetic support in Parlia-
ment to a Labor Government ?
Nothing like “Salvation Nell” has been seen
at a .T.C. W. theatre in the recollection of your
Akenehi. The chances are that nothing like it
will be seen again after the rags of the naturalistic,
muse are taken off the stage of the Royal. Not,
at any rate, until Sydney grows an equivalent to
the American Bowery or London Whitechapel.
For box-office reasons. Salvation Nell and la bite
humaine she so blindly loves will no doubt depart
hurriedly. Not even the splendid acting of Miss
Grey and Mr. Desmond will keep them going. The
play is a commonplace study of a lot of common-
place, ugly, narrow lives. Salvation Army officers
shine as doers of good deeds in the naughty
under-world. At the close of the play the
audience saw the beast on the edge of his reform-
ation, but it really did not care whether his soul
was saved or not. Is the soul of a carrion crow
worth saving ? And it was exasperating to see
a lovable, good, pretty little woman fairly exuding
love and loyalty to such a mean, insanitary loafer
as the Jim Platt of Mr. Desmond.
It is a singularly humorless play. One would
think that the long bar-room scene would give
chances for humor. The Mr. Dooley with whom
half the world was laughing a few years ago, was
an American saloon-keeper; and Norman Lindsay’s
topers are humorous dogs. Miss Elsie Wilson
as Muriel Odell, an avowed and blatant
Scarlet Woman, is the nearest approach to the
humorous, but of the most grotesque type. “ Oh,
them pants ! ” she exclaims, recalling the days
when she and Nell were sweated workers in
noisome surroundings. “Oh, them pants! Fifteen
buttons up the side and the tape on the ba#k
seam, and all for a few cents a pair.” Then
she hitches up her crimson satin, perfectly-
fitting frock and reveals a crimson-hosed leg
with a gold garter. Another of her gowns is a
black and white stripe with horizontal screams of
emerald satin, a huge emerald and black yell of a
hat and emerald gloves. Her gait is always a
cake-walk, and she remains unconverted and
cheerful, and dressed like a tropical parrot to the
end of the consistently dismal play.
Miss Grey, as the drudge of the saloon-keeper,
is garbed in rags, and carries a pail and scrubbing-
brush. She lodges a grief that “burns worse
than tears drown,” and conveys a sense of physical
and spiritual exhaustion. A wonderful piece of
acting; but, as the actress fainted on the fall of
the curtain, perhaps not all acting.
Some of us were talking of the advisability of
forming a Pessimists’ Club. Presently that stately,
Eiffel Tower actress. Miss Mab Paul, passed by,
and one of the mildest of the pessimists re-
marked : “I do not admire very tall women.”
“No,” agreed the most virulent of the pessim-
ists; “ the less of any of them the better.” Just
as we were about to separate amidst forgings and
utterings of false things about the weather, a
mutual friend heaved up to us; and in reply to
queries about his health replied : “I feel so well
that I don’t know what the deuce is the matter
with me.” Him will we elect vice-president of
the nil admirari club.
Mention of Miss Mab Paid reminds that as the
Countess in the play at the Criterion she is
wearing the most gorgeous stage gowns in Syd-
ney. They are a trifle over the speed-limit; but
the Countess herself is just a little on the swift
side. She is qualifying for putting a hole
through the Decalogue, when her amusing,
improper husband comes back from hav-
ing done so on his own account, and she is
saved. Her first frock is a pale amethyst cloth,
with a hat of a darker shade in velvet. Her
handsomest creation is a ninon in a young shade
of old rose, if you can imagine such a tone, with
bands and pipings of pink satin. It fits the
wearer as if it were painted on her, and
you can see a good deal of Miss Paul
through the transparency of the shoulders. She
wears this caramel of a frock at breakfast. It’s
as bad as being brilliant at the morning meal.
When she goes out to lunch with Preedy, the un-
fortunate buffer or scapegoat, she puts on a black
hat with pink feathers. Her third and last frock
is of pale green satin, with a tunic embroidered
with gold and silver corn cobs, and edged with a
Bullfinch lode of gold in fringe formation.
Two characters in “Mr. Preedy and the Coun-
tess” would break up a pessimists’ club by
preventing the members enjoying their misery.
They are a suburban dentist and a young aristo-
crat, the husband of the Countess. One is such a
humorous bounder, and the other such an inspired
idiot.
Mr. W. K. D’Arey, the Queensland millionaire
—he who owns a private grandstand on Epsom
racecourse, and there entertains dukes and earls
for the Derby, City and Suburban and other im-
portant meetings—has arrived in Australia on a
visit. He is a sort of uncle, by the way, of Mr.
Langford Kirby, one of the main factors in the
success of “Miss Gibbs.” That is to say, Mrs.
D’Arcy, who was Miss Nina Boucicault, of Bris-
bane, is Mr. Kirby’s aunt. Before Mount Morgan
made him very rich Mr. D’Arcy was a Rock-
hampton solicitor.
A fair young stranger within our gates is Lady
Beatrice Clark, wife of Captain Robertson
Clark, whom Sydney knew as private secretary to
the late Sir Harry Rawson. She is a slender girl
of medium height, dark-haired and with a flower-
petal complexion and an uncommon type of face.
Reminiscent of some Tennyson girl’s face—per-
haps his Margaret, as the poet imagined her look-
ing at him through the jasmine leaves. The
newly-weds (fairly newly-weds) have gone up to
the Clark station.
Our friend Lord Kitchener has been helping a
one-time brother officer to do what he himself has
refrained from doing. General Sir Archibald
Hunter, Governor and Commander-in-Chief of
Gibraltar, is the better man for whom the great
best man carried the wedding ring. The lady, on
the face value of her portraits, is a handsome
woman. Lord Inverclyde, her late husband, whom
she married in 1885, was chairman of the Cunard
steamshipco. She has been a widow five years. The
winning widow again I I have a wager of the two
front paws of a monkey (as the delightful British
aristocrat in “Mr. Preedy and the Countess”
says) that Lord Kitchener himself will be har-
pooned by a widow.
Miss Phyllis Hopwood Foldi, the musical mono-
loguist who made a roohiy and secure niche for
herself in London, is back in Sydney after an ab-
sence of three years. She is a fine-looking girl, rosy
as to complexion, with a lot of the ruddy—not to
say red —hair that goes with vivid coloring. She
has nothing but good to say of England as a place
for artistic niche-making, but adds that the artiste
must give the English plenty of time. They
won’t be hurried into accepting the stranger, any
more than they could be induced to drop their
own old favorites even when these have little to
give but wheezings and dodderings. Miss Hop-
wood Foldi’s own success she ascribes mainly to
the Italian following she secured in London. In
a few weeks’ time she will give a recital in
Sydney.
“ Lord Kitchener has at last got a home of his
own in England,” says a friend writing to me.
One would think he’d had to doss on the Embank-
ment up to the time of scouting this home. It
turns out to be Broome Park, a beautiful place
near Folkestone.
The red or fair-haired girl finds a certain con-
solation in the wearing of black. She’s ruddier
than the cherry, or fairer than the lily, by the con-
trast. Miss Mary Laing-Meason. the Maorilander,
now playing in “By Wireless Telegraphy” at
the King’s Theatre in Melbourne* tells that she
has to die in the part, “It is most objectionable
to die in sight of an audience,” ene says,
“especially if you are quite sure that you are
dying after the manner of a cat that has been
shot (by a bad marksman) for chicken-stealing.
‘Passing into the infinite azure of the past’ sug-
gests graceful dying, but I don’t do that. I
just peg out. My consolation is that, being red-
haired, I wear black and plenty of diamonds —the
last loaned me by Beatrice Day.”
Lately someone sent me an English paper with
a musical item marked. As it was unimportant,
I looked through the rest of the journal for in-
teresting items, but got only two heartaches. A
coster, with tears running down his face, pleaded
guilty at the London Sessions to stealing a crate
of bananas. “ One of my children,” he said, “is
dying, and another at home kept crying ‘ Daddy,
I want buppy—l want buppy ’ (bread and
butter). That drove me to do it. Don’t send
me to prison. It will kill my wife if you do.”
Police gave the man a good character, and he
was bound over, and left the dock exclaiming,
“ Thank you kindly, sir.”
Over the page was the tale of a clerk who had
been out of work since January. He was
arraigned for stealing one block of wood from a
lot placed ready for the paving of a suburban
road. The young man pleaded that he had had
nothing to eat since Thursday, and on Saturday
night he went to Barking to see if he could get
some food. A friend gave him 2|d. to buy a loaf,
and he took the block to boil the kettle. He
added :
For days and days I have been without food, and for
weeks and weeks i have had insufficient food. During
the time I was out of work I suffered with rheumatism,
and had only bread and water, and whilst I had con-
tracted fingers I had to walk to the London Hospital,
undergo an operation, and walk back home without a
bite of food. I have applied at the Labor Exchange, and
I have also been to the docks, and have failed to get
work. I have a wife and child.
The unlucky clerk was bound over under the Pro-
bation Act.
Ten years ago those men would have got, pro-
bably, several years’ hard, so I suppose “we are
living into a gentler time” as the late Gladstone
said. Such reading makes one glad that one lives
so far from the place where it is published.
The one unintentionally humorous item in the
paper was the announcement that a number of
Boer war medals and clasps remained unclaimed
at the Royal Arsenal, Woolwich. The authorities
are surprised that these King’s and Queen’s South
African honors should have to seek claimants.
A correspondent mentions that the will has
just been proved in London of “ Madame Jacua-
nita, milliner, of 35 Duke-street, Manchester
Square,” and that Madame J. was in private
life, Lady Mary Graham Montgomery, a daughter
of the late Baronet Moncreiffe and sister of our
Governor-General’s mother. Her estate panned
out at over £26,000. There’s more money in hats
than in rubber.
Professor and Miss David are off for a trip to
Europe next month. The Dr. Welby-Fishers, of
“Strathkyle” Flats —the lady’s hat created a
sensation at the recent races—are also announced
as near departers for foreign parts.
Early trippers to England, where they propose
to stay a good deal longer time than it will take
to fix the crown on George V., are the Austins,
of Austinlee, one of the old wooded estates
on the Liver pool-road, which the motor-cars
churn up nowadays. The Austins and the
Lees of Bathurst have gob themselves related to
about half a thousand of the oldest families
mostly families that run to wool or prize oxen,
with side-lines branching into the Church.
Cable brings the sad news of the death in Lon-
don of Mrs. de la Poer Nugent at the age of 24
years. She was a Miss Smith, daughter of Cap-
tain Smith, of Goderich, and a sister of Captain
Leslie Wilson’s wife. Mr. Nugent is a naval man.
Miss Bertha Brady, one of the acknowledged
belles of Sydney, and daughter of Dr. Brady,
is amongst the latest to be offered the orange
blossom. Her fiance is Captain Prowse, one of
our men-of-war.
From an English exchange
The stewards of the Jockey Club have issued a circular
letter to the clerks of courses of the various race meet-
ings under their jurisdiction, requesting them to pro-
hibit in future the use of the camera in all racecourse
enclosures.
The following supplies the reason :
Lord , a member of the Jockey Club, whilst con-
versing in the paddock with a lady at a Northern race
meeting, was snapshotted, but when reproduced the lady
was described as Lord ’s wife. The real Lady saw
the picture, and was extremely annoyed. So was her
husband, who lodged a complaint with the Jockey Club.
In another case a Colonial lady on a visit to England
was mistaken for the wife of a baronet whose reputation
as a husband was not exactly all it might have been.
We do these things better in Australia. Our
papers which purvey racecourse snapshots care-
fully refrain from labelling their victims, unless
the same are so well known as not to be worth
labelling. ‘‘A Beauteous Trio” (three hobble-
skirts surmounted by as many bee-hives); “The
Favorite Wins ” (picture of two elderly females and
something vaguely resembling a man, peering in-
tently at the Governor’s box); “Society s’Amuse ”
(view of prominent usurer arguing passionately
with his wife and daughter anent the criminal
extravagance of driving to the course in a cab) —
these are the only that a hungry popu-
lace gets of the identities of our sporting folk. It
saves a lot of trouble to snapshotters, editors,
sub-editors and so forth, and brings into pro-
minence, to their vast satisfaction, hosts of
worthy burgesses who would otherwise languish
unknown to fame.
From Freddie Shipman comes a bundle of pic-
tures and press-cuttings to show that Melba is a
boom in Canada. A Halifax photo, shows a
queue stretching back from the Academy of Music
to what looks like forest; and a Toronto paper
says there never has been so much enthusiasm
over any other songbird.
Miss Rosina Buckmann’s work in the Grand
Opera Co. just disbanded ha smade secure her perch
in the professional aviary. She joins the Royal
Comics to sing the Princess part in “The Waltz
Dream ” when the R.C.’s start to waltz their
dream through M.L. Judging by the way
“ Miss Gibbs ” continues to obstruct the traffic,
the tour will not start much this side of Christ-
mas.
Miss Nellie Stewart will have Mr. Harcourt
Beatty with her on her forthcoming small-towns’
tour. Mr. G. Musgrove will take along a dynamo,
and light up the country halls where Sweet Nell
will hawk oranges and subjugate a king.
Miss Lizette Parkes, who is with the Hamilton-
Plimmer co. in Maoriland, has won a beauty prize
in a looks competition. No particulars are to pen,
so one does not know to what extent the youngster
has scored.
One of the characters in “ Our Miss Gibbs ” has
gone to the dogs. At the Ladies’ Kennel Club
show, held at the end of last week, Miss Vera
Ramaciotti got first prize in the Pomeranian pups
section with “Timothy Gibbs,” a bark of great
beauty and yelp power. Tim is pleasanter than
his name-giver at Her Majesty’s, inasmuch as he
does not play the trombone.
There are some subjects—the transcendent
personal virtues of Mr. Wade, the leg-irons of
Mr. Peter Bowling, the hopelessness of backing
horses as a means to wealth, and so on—that con-
stant reiteration has rendered as flat, stale and
unprofitable as a Good Friday bun which has
been held over for a month. Into this class I here
and now relegate the Abruzzi-Elkins engagement.
It is off again. Everything had been fixed
up. Miss Elkins had arranged to forego the
Primitive Methody faith, or whatever it is
they favor in her part of the TJ.S.; and the family
pew had been removed from the Abernethy
Chapel, ready for installation in the R.C. taber-
nacle as soon as word should arrive from
Rome that the Elkins’s claims to enter the Church
had been audited and found correct. When all
that had been done there arrived the sickening
news that the King of Italy proposed to withhold
princely rank from Senator Elkins. It was more
than any self-respecting democratic Yank could
stand. So the slighted parent intimated to his
daughter that if she wedded the Duke she should
do it over a vacant bank-account. Thus the
parties are, once more, where they were two
years ago. When the Duke and Miss Elkins get
engaged again, next month, I shall not notice it.
It is getting on my nerves.
A family complication is threatened in Wes-
tralia; but one of the gentlemen concerned writes
to the local paper to say that it isn’t as bad as the
neighbors said it was going to be :
Hearing that rumor has it that my daughter intends to
marry her grandfather, I wisli to inform your readers,
through your valuable paper, that this statement is abso-
lutely false. The gentleman whom she intends to marry
is no relation by blood. He is my uncle, through marry-
ing my mother’s sister. No doubt this mistake has arisen
through my bringing my family up to call him “Grand-
pa.”
So he isn’t grandpa really; he is only grand-
uncle.
Yours affectionately,
AKENKHI.
The Adelaide police are looking into a
strange case, and don’t know what to make of it.
Between a Saturday and Monday, two rungs in
a ladder on the new Museum wing were sawn
nearly through, three storeys up. The first man
to mount happened to be one with the habit of
gripping the sides, and not the rungs, or it is
pretty certain there would have been a death
in that family. As it was, he escaped with a
scramble and a fright. So far the strange
creature who laid the murderous trap for a chance
comer has escaped altogether.
Monopoly now proposes in Victoria to call
itself the People’s Party. It is an old rule of the
gold-brick profession —after every conviction a
new alias.
Amid the torrents of platitude that flowed
at the recent conference of Women’s “Liberal"’
Associations at Adelaide, this splash of sense and
truth, which occurred in a paper read by a Mrs.
Sorrell, was as welcome as the doxology after a
bad sermon:—
It (the Labor platform) was an appeal to an instinct
they all had, to hetter their condition. She was firmly
convinced that, if a referendum were taken to-morrow on
the Federal Land Tax, with all its iniquitous provisions,
there would be a large majority in favor of it. .. . Those
who voted for Labor candidates were not necessarily
ignorant working" men and women, but amongst the most
intelligent people in the community. To have a chance
of success they must meet intelligence with intelligence.
The Tory Women’s League often lets slip admis-
sions that wild horses would not drag out of a
men’s league. The above is a case in point.
Another was the remark made at a Queensland
conference deploring the deportation of Kanakas—
the unfortunate remark which has kept Senators
St. Cash-book and Sayers explaining ever since.

art was the Bohemia Club lunch on the lawn at
Flenaingtori on Oaks Day. But the Government
House affair aforesaid is the Grand Prix of il •
Holder’s profession. And he was congratulated
on all sides for having so brilliant

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 7,1910.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Cup Day ! When almost every person on the
upper surface of a continent is thinking hard
about one horse-race. There is an electric tingle
in the air, as if a full-powered thunderstorm were
hanging round ; but .when a. hundred and fif tj
thousand odd Cup-gazers crowd together in.one
place, at the one time, it amounts to a cloud-
burst and three earthquakes. Visitors from
well-packed corners of the world are filled with
amazement when they are taken to I’lemington on
Cup Day and shown the Hill. They almost
invariably remark that they are used to a lot of
people, but nowhere have ,they seen such a well-
dressed lot. If the intelligent foreigner neglects
to make this remark, he is written off as a person
of no discernment. More than usual we looked for
the remark this year, Bor it was a blue day, and
there was much pink parasol and scarlet deco-
ration about.
Few bothered about their Cup dresses—the talk
of months—once they had arrived at Flemington.
To get a corner to pack into was the one idea.
Unhappy husbands put general’s work into get-
ting their women folk “ fixed ” before going off to
place their combined half-crowns. If women be-
come such sports the betting paddock will have
to be enlarged. Those unlucky women who had
no imrhediate claim on a man cheerfully bailed
up male acquaintances to carry cash to Patrick
Moses: for them. Four women gave a handful of
half-crowns for one bet to a man familiar to them
at their swaggah tavern. They never saw a
ticket nor a sight of the male creature after.
Now they wail for the tote.
Comedy King was the most conspicuous horse
before the event because of McLachlan’s chess-
board jacket and crimson sleeves and cap. Ira-
falgar—Australian and the favorite—would have
been the popular win, of course; but the gallop
wherein he was just beaten was worth a year of
dulness. To see the two rivals whipping out
from the ruck and thundering together half the
length"of the straight was enough to rouse the
sporting instinct of a hedgehog.
Yet to many people the race doesn’t count.
Just after the finish, in the relaxed wriggling of
the immense crowd* scrambling toward tea, one
woman—she lives in.asporting-Sassiety circle, too
—exultantly remarked : “I had my tea in com-
fort while the race was being run. One Cup is the
same as another —I’ve seen 13.”
Governor Carmichael was too ill, as the result
of a Derby Day chill, to go out to Flemington for
the two-a,nd-a-half minute Cup gallop. Lord
Dick/Nevill is up and about again after his bout
of influenza. He came over to Melbourne early,
intending to do advance-guard work for the .on-
coming army of vice-royalty ; but as soon as he
arrived he acquired a sneeze, and was forced to
go into retirement until the horses were going to
the post for the first race.
Coming back from the course, the flocks of
people ~had a wearisome time. The railways
could pnlv take as many as filled a continuous
line of trains; and on the roads every kind of
cart arid ’bus supplemented the hooting motor.
In the crowds penned up in sections outside the
railway' gates there was endless lamentation.
Thousands of engagements Had perforce* to be
broken, and there was no earthly chance
of telephoning. Prudent people who had de-
parted before the last race, danced with impa-
tience in town waiting, .for guests who never
turned up—or, worse still, raged at the con-
tinued absence of hosts.
The afterglow of Cup Day was the most tired
spectacle imaginable. People thronged the
theatres and flopped into seats booked long ahead;
but they were too tired to speak and too tired to
applaud. Even “The Whip,” full of local color,
could not rouse a band until the end, when the
Whip's jockey, suddenly pulled off his coat and
displayed Comedy King’s colors. Then the dog-
tired racers roared appreciation. All the im-
ported mummers in Melbourne went out, and it
seemed to take the snap out of their evening’s
work. Possibly, though, it was merely the effect
of the yawning audiences.
“I shall wear that for the Oaks,” said the uni-
versal She, proposing. “That” was her very
best frock—her loveliest garment of rejoicing.
“I bet you won’t!” said the falling rain, dis-
posing.' I -translated the subsequent downpour
and uproar this way: “ Confound your Oaks
dress —rilso your dressmaker’s bill ! Ruin your
poor husband, would you ! You’ll just wear your
old coat and skirt; the one you’ve been wearing
to Chapel-street.” But there are She’s who say
“Won’t!” even to the rain. They got them-
selves into their garments of delight, huddled into
cabsandfled tothetrain. “It may clear up!” cried
the optimists. “Do see how that black lace is
running into the peach-colored silk ! ” waned
the pessimists. Many women got as far as Flem-
ington ; then turned back hopelessly. * The sport
was nothing to them. The lift-attendants at the
swagger taverns took many scowling souls toward
Heaven. But as fast as they went aloft, motors,
taxis and kebs rolled back to the taverns and
deposited more at the front door. The brutal
men stayed out in the wet. “They don t care
how my dress is ruined! ” cried the average heart-
broken woman into her consoling cup of after-
noon tea.
Steeplechase Saturday—with a fine assortment
of weather samples—was filled with the hurried
spirit of the Last Day. Most people, neverthe-
less, took a business-like view of things. ‘ Must
wipe out ray other losses” was a sort of motto
everywhere. Women who had moaned about
clothes forgot them. All the other cats had
by this time seen their best frocks either on the
lawn or at garden parties, or in the privacy of
the hotel'bedroom. So they got themselves into
comfortable- gear, and were happy, ordinary
human beings again.
Governor Carmichael has been very ill and
shut up at home. His lady came out. alone .on
Saturday looking distinguished in black. Ihe
Countess wore her favorite white but such a
white ! It was a smart little linen frock, pleated,
with a- cheeky little belt of black patent leather
and white kid. The dress was short, and she was
perfectly shod. Everything thcHonses own m the
shape of an aide came along. The gentlemen
helps don’t mind doing their duty on a race day.
The weather behaved sufficiently well for cosy
little snacks under the Elms, both at lunch-time
and for afternoon tea. The Tom Paynes came
out in a four-horse drag, with horn, and footmen
complete. It was a joyous turnout. Mrs.; Payne
never falters in her worship of the . Horse.
She drives through town with a small
tiger glued on at the back when everyone
else is saying “ Please” most humbly to his Lord-
ship the Shover. This was the only drag I could
see at Fletnington. There was scarcely a carriage.
Motors in neat layers filled the carriage paddock,
which will obviously soon have to be re-christened
the garage grass, or some such thing.
There were a few of the John Madden family in
sight, looking rather cold. The Frank Madden
girls are pretty, but of a different type, and they
dress with the splendor of the stage. One Frank
Madden maiden wore a coolie hat.; This brand of
headgear makes one. look round for a rick-
shaw. Mrs. Duffy got cerise, with brilliant
effect, into her trappings. Mrs. Railway Pait
looked extremely youthful alongside her daughter,
who struck an original note with yellow roses in a
feather boa. Mrs. Charlie Tait (of Allan’s) gained
much subdued applause all over the lawn by the
dainty prettiness of her costume. Mrs. Miller
had a great bun or chignon of fair hair under a
black hat, and a dazzling white linen frock.
Sister Mrs. Kelly hung a black ninon veil over a
Paisley frock. . Your Enid Whiting-Waters flung
the very newest colors —-a royal blue and a deep
rose-crimson—into her hat. Mrs. Adamson had
one pretty blue-eyed daughter along all in blue,
and nibbled food under the vines.
The frocker pure and simple flung on her flim-
siest finery regardless of the weather, then pre-
pared for the possible worst by winding a rubber-
ised grey cloak about her. When the sun shone
she emerged; when the rain threatened she
donned her weather armor and merely showed
summery ankles and the hem of a summer frock.
A man—a mere man—said he has never in his
life seen so many stockings except at an auction
in a softgoods warehouse. Stockings are stockings
nowadays, and the rainy spells of Steeplechase
Day and the deluge of the Oaks brought out the
best of the show. There are legs, too, that one
does not suspect in ordinary, discreet weather.
Garden parties are a natural adjunct of race
week—a necessary safety-valve to let off froeking
steam. Women break their hearts and their
men’s pockets in a wild outburst of glad rags for
Cup time, and, as a general thing, the dresses are
more numerous than the race days. Often the
weather refuses to smile; then there is one
more creation left over. Hence garden parties.
The Governors have been giving them at the rate
of two a week, as hard as they could go. Last
week Stonington got in early with its strawberry
festival and everybody went. There had been
two itemized, garden parties the week before;
then one great outburst in which the visitors from
other States were included. This last happened
on Monday.
Then the G.-G- Dudleys asked all and sundry
to walk on the grass on Friday; but the rain
descended and the winds blew the function to
shreds, and the shreds took refuge in the drawing-
rooms and afternoon-tea-tabled ballroom. Super-
cilious serving-men in purple livery moved
about on plump white-stockinged props, and
gazed from a height upon the rush of those
ravening guests. The elect wore their Oaks
dresses —beautiful things of soft veiled colors.
They trailed these beauty drapings out on to the
terrace, in the one sunshiny interval of the after-
noon, and looked at the water-lilies in the foun-
tain pond.
A lily-of-the-valley type of girl, small and
fair, came south from Sydney to break the
shell of flapperhood and burst into society.
She is Miss Dagmar Koefoed, a Darling
Pointer; and her entrance into the great, big
wonderful world was effected in white satin and
pearls—at the Australian Club Ball. Another
Sydneysider who took the eye elsewhere was Mrs.
Dr.) Dan Kelly, who Cupped in white, with pink
ntroductions and a white hat with ostrich plumes.
The other day Lord Dicky Nevill took all the
Dudley’s house party down the hill to the Botani-
cal Gardens kiosk for lunch. Possibly the Dudleys
just sent the visitors out of the way for feeding
time while the house was being swept for the
party. The visitors’ luck was in, anyhow, for
lunch on the verandah of that house amid the
trees is better than six banquets in the whitest of
marble halls.
Anew little bride who fluttered round.the Cup
in fascinating frocks was Mrs. McMeikan. She
was Miss St. John before she married one of the
well-known Warrnambool clan.
Geach, of the picture shows, swept through
Melbourne like a meteor this race-time. First he
backed Trafalgar for the Cup, and watched him
just miss it; then he shot off to Inverloch, in the

friend Daddy Lohr. He tyas next sighted watch-
ing the Steeplechase on Saturday, after which lie.
is understood to have whirled back to Sydney.
Sounds rather like the programme’s description ot
a humorous film, “ The Busy Mans Holiday.

One always looks for the French woolbuyers at
the Cup, and finds them, as a rule. Apparently,
their trips back to their native land are partly tor
business and partly for the purpose of revisiting
the tailor. Two of the contingent have resumed
housekeeping at St. Kilda, under the same blonde
management as previously, and three of them
are partners in a furnished villa at Tooralt. I he
handsome Lhoest, from Belgium or thereabouts
has not been back to Europe this year, but will
return when the wool season is over. As an exile
from home he seems to make the best of Ine in
irreproachable clothes. He drives a smart motor ;
and a motor, of course, Carries consolation with it.
Quaint restaurant stories are rife at carnival
time. There was the case of the artless brother
and sister, of the squattocracy, who dropped into
a costly refreshment house one afternoon
when it was crowded, and were asked it
they would like a room upstairs, bo they took
their tea and scone tete-a-tete, though they
would have preferred to mingle with the chattering
throng downstairs. Naturally, the gentleman
looked pensive when he found that his' sister s
craving for tea had cost him 10s. extra for the
private room. Another story is of a lady who did
want an apartment upstairs for herself and escort,
and specified “the room that Mr. So-and-so
always has.” The courteous waiter replied:
“ Which Mr.* So-and-so, Miss—the father or the
son? ” She had to explain that the elder gallant
was the one she was thinking of.

neither does it spin; but how pathetic those mtf
lilies looked !
There is a Maori Village-in the Exhibition
Arena, where the dusky ladies and gentlemen go
through a programme of native dances. Maggie
Papakura announces each turn in a plaintive
contralto. Some of the graceful girls dance-to
the familiar strains of a concertina, and keep the
most wonderful time. But when a fat brown la y
seated herself at a wobbling piano, and p anting
her bare foot carefully on the soft pedal, com-
menced to play a hackneyed song, I nearly asked
for my shilling back. The singer was a P ret >’
girl with wonderful dark eyes, and she could
sing. But (Oh, shades of an ancient nice .)
she sang from a bulky bound copy, which she
held tenderly on her hip. Some of the men aie
really very fine, with soft voices and melting
brown eyes—and don’t they know it? I never
had such practised eyes made at me before, lhe
glances were so killing that I wonder I'm alive to
tell the tale. All the whares are thrown open to
the public, and you can inspect the nice little
white beds of the savage brown men ! One or
them—who told me in a soft whisper that his
name was Hera —showed with pride a pale-blue
satin case. I opened it and discovered it con-
tained his sleeping suit.
The Mayoress, in a cunningly embroidered
white linen frock, went, the other hot morning,
to have tea at the new Swastika Club. The club
decoration scheme is all golden browns and rest-
ful lights. Mrs. Malcolm, the club’s president, is
matron of the Nurses’ Association in Melbourne ;
so she has her time filled up. Her brother, Mr.
.T. A. B. Smith, came to help the party along.
Mrs. J. A. B. is the president of the Austral
Salon. Some Sydney visitors, including Mrs.
Rouse, also dropped in for a nibble at the morn-
ing muffin. The club’s membership list is swell-
ing visibly, and there is some prospect of new
rooms being added even at this early stage of its
career.
According to Gaston Cugnet, the I rench air-
man, who lias come to teach Australia how to fly,
the simplicity of an ascent on a monoplane is
astonishing to anyone who tries it for the first
time, and an instructor’s first care is to warn the
novice against losing his head in a shock of sur-
prise. The difficulty comes in guiding the new
animal downwards by degrees to a suitable land-
ing stage, and that can only be overcome by
a strong nerve-effort, seemingly. Timidity, by
making for safety, may lead to .confidence in
motoring, whereas it leads to catastrophe in aero-
planing. Thusfar, the flying machine is only for
the adventurous ; the family aeroplane, built to
carry six passengers beside the driver on an
ordinary picnic excursion, is almost as far from
realisation as ever it was. Cugnet talks buoy-
antly of commercial men flying around in pursuit
of business, but says nothing about the prospects
of pleasure-seekers scooting to the races or taking
week-end trips through the air. To that extent
the flying machine is the least progressive of all
modern marvels. It has been going on for years
without approaching the sphere of practical
picnics.
By the death of Robert Lancelot levers on
Tuesday last, several charitable committees, in-
cluding that of the Women’s Hospital, lost a
valuable member. He was wealthy, and was
always willing to help a subscription list ; but
the mere giving of money did not content him
he supplemented his donations with hard work.
Also, he was an enthusiastic Shakspeanan
student, but kept a corner'nf-his heart for Aus-
tralian literature.

The company that bought “Cliveden,’’ the
huge town house of the late Baronet Claike, is
thinking bard. The idea was to build two more
storeys on to the present mansion, arjd: let the
whole place out in flats. Now experts declare
that the foundations won’t carry two more storeys.
From “ Rosna,” of Adelaide
Premier Verran was invited from his political fastnesses
last week to open the Federal Art Exhibition. Which
reminds rile that it is up to Australian artists to bestir
themselves. Here is our Library Board thirsting to put
pence in their pockets and hang their works in the Gal-
lery and the sole encouragement the body gets is an
offerin'-- of hackneyed old landscapes, seascapes and
horrorscapes that have figured in every capital
in Australia. The most interesting thing in the show is
Norman Carter’s “ Low-toned Harmony,” a live study of
a serious damsel, who may be expected to walk out of the
frame at any minute. Bernard Hall’s ‘ Under the Colos-
seum”—a tragic and half-clad lady lying in a dead faintand
a crunched-up attitude—is impressive, and he ha& also a
fine study of brass and china. Dattilo Rubbo s Dere-
licts” is vigorously managed ; and our own Will Ashton
has half a dozen desirable canvases, chief being Spring
Tide and Full Moon,” a seascape full of force and move-
ment. Rose Macpherson shows a gargoyle and Dutch
pot against a china-filled cabinet that is a wonderful bit
of technique ; also several other clever still-life pictures,
and a portrait with some tone virtues. One Hambidge
sister has an ethereal-looking damsel brooding over a
piano in the dusk ; Gustav Barnes, some sheep in a
pleasant British pasture, and a weird, conventional, pale-
blue gentleman, named Sir Gawain, presiding over the
fountain of tfie Holy Grail. Ford Patterson has painted
us the Gippsland Lakes, for which we hasten to be grate-
ful • Albert Hanson has done various portions of N.b.W. ,
and Edward Davies our own much-vgunted hills.
George Webb commits an exhibition on his own account
this week.

HEART TROUBLE.
Distressing Symptoms that
Cause a lot of Worry
and Anxiety.
Symptoms of heart trouble are frequent in
anaemia and indigestion. “I cant hurry, my
heart thumps so,” is the common expression ot
every anaemic person. In indigestion there are
wind spasms that send darting pains about the
heart, which make the victims often think they
have heart disease. When the blood supply is
built up, or the indigestion cured, the heart symp-
toms naturallv disappear, as there is nothing to
cause them. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured
a great many cases of anaemia and indigestion and
have given sufferers relief from distressing heart
symptoms that caused a lot of worry and anxiety.
They actually make new blood. The following
case shows how valuable they are in heart trouble.
Mrs. Helene Warnock, 75 Mason Street, S.
Yarra, Melbourne, tells how Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills built up her health, and her experience
should prove of interest to other sufferers. Mrs.
Warnock’s own words are : “ Some time back my
health began to fail. My heart began to give me
a great deal of trouble. I had always had a good
constitution previously, but now I noticed my
heart would flutter nearly all the time, and with
any exertion it would begin to thump furiously,
especially if I walked up a hill, and I’d get such a
gone sort of feeling, as if I were going to die. 1
dare not hurry for a tram. Mv heart would pal-
pitate, as if it were going to jump out of its place.
I’d get out of breath at once, and have to stop
every few yards to rest. My breathing would be
so quick and hurried I'd have to gasp. I used to
feel sure my heart was seriously affected. I always
had a cough, apparently from the stomach, and I
was devoid of my strength. My face was always
flushed, without cause. I’d have to take a rest
now and then in the day, I felt so fagged and
tired out. I slept and ate pretty well, but really
the food didn’t seem td do me any good, or nourish
me, and I fell away Considerably. Even a little
bit of housework Was a trouble, I #ot so easily
knocked up. My heart was rrtrfely free from pal-
pitation. My nerves Were so weak, and my blood
so thiti. I was very easily startled, and at times
would feel very worried and irritable. But I
found Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills a perfect tonic.
They began to tone me up from the start. My
nerves steadied down, and my heart began to beat
less irregularly. I bought another box, and felt
better still, so I kept on with four boxes. My
health was quite restored, and I have kept well
ever since. My strength and energy gradually
came back, and I feel better than I have done
for years.” ... f

THE FAT FOWL: “Thank Heaven, there are
always some worms waiting around to he
eaten.”
The new “ People’s” party in Vic. thanks
Heaven that it isn’t like the Labor crowd, which
is governed by a caucus. Then, having snuffled
this pious reflection, it proceeds to state that “all
electors who are not pledged to the Labor party
should enter into a covenant of union binding
them to abide by the decision of the majority
of the new organisation.” There should be a great
career ahead of a party that gives such a fine
demonstration of logic and consistency—and
sfndffle.
' When Labor came in, Capital was, of
course, going to clear out from N. S. Wales.
After waiting for a fortnight, however, the
Government saw that there was still so much of
it 'on the premises that, acting on advice, the
Treasury reduced the rate of interest from 3J
to 3tj per cent. If Capital were flying away, the
Treasury would naturally have to offer better
terms to induce it to stay ; but evidently Capital
wants to stay so badly that the Treasury reckons
it can get all it wants even if it offers less induce-
fnent than the old Government offered.
Last Monday the papers contained seven
consecutive cablegrams about strikes in England,
Wales, France, Spain and the United States;
also about fights, shootings, curses and sundries
connected therewith. Two of the strikes involve
190,000 men. It really looks as if capital will yet
be driven to Australia—the land where strikes are
almost unknown—by these ill-judged doings.
An interesting return was lately issued by
the American Labor Department. It sets forth
that wholesale prices, as measured by the
cost of 257 commodities, have increased
by 7'5 per cent, in 12 months, by 21T
per cent, in 10 years, and by 33‘8 per
cent, in 20 years. From March,
1909, to March, 1910, farm products
increased by 15 per cent., food by
3‘4, clothing by 2’3, drugs and
chemicals by I’B. The significant
point about the figures is that
where the labor cost is greatest—
i.e., where the process of manufac-
ture is most intricate—the increased
cost is least. It is greatest where
the primary industries are con-
cerned. Raw material increased in the
last 12 months by 9 per cent., whereas
manufactured goods only went up by 14
per cent. In America, as elsewhere, the
wage standard in the primary industries
is lowest; so the obvious conclusion is
that the increase is due almost wholly
.to the higher price of lands and the
inflation of rents. Which is a hint to
Australia not to make itself ill with
worry, even when it is told with awful
impressiveness that current legislation will
clip a trifle off the heights to which Aus-
tralian land values are climbing.
An enraged “Liberal” wrote to S. M.
Herald last week, as follows :
Can you tell me whether Mr. G. B. Edwards, Federal
member for North Sydney, was elected as a Liberal or a
Labor supporter ? I was under the impression that it
was as the former, but he is apparently so confused with
national ideas that he cannot remember on which side lie
was elected. lam led to nslr this question by his atti-
tude, first, on the Federal Notes Bill, and recently on
the proposal to amend the Constitution with regard to
trade, commerce, etc.
It is the frankest admission that any “ Liberal ”
has yet made of the fact that Labor stands for
National ideas, and “Liberalism” for parochial
ones.
A number of Sydney “Liberals” are
chasing round after subscriptions towards a
£lO.OOO gratuity which it is proposed to donate
to ex-Premier Wade. The idea is, partly that he
should he recompensed for the alleged financial
loss he has suffered by dropping his Bar work and
taking to politics, partly that he should be placed
in a position to forego the law and fight the Labor
Devil unhampered, even unto the ultimate ditch.
True, they haven’t raised the £lO,OOO yet, and
they are continually bumping against people
who don’t believe Wade is;worth the money as a
leader. They find that there are earnest anti-
Laborites who hold him dear, in fact, at a ten-
pound note. The “Liberals” who are conducting
this campaign are big business men—merchants,
solicitors and the like. They would show their
gratitude to Wade more convincingly for the
spirited battle he put up against the S’osh Menace—
or whatever it was he was supposed to be assailing
—if such of them as are solicitors pledged them-
selves to brief him in every available case, while
such as are potential litigants agreed to give him
first chance to represent their interests in court.
That would be putting their admiration for their
hero’s ability, character and so forth on a really
practical basis.
One of the planks of the new “ People’s ”
party in Victoria runs this way :
(d) Resistance to all attempts by the Federal Legisla-
ture to assume control (through any system of taxation,
penalty, or bonus) of matters left by the Constitution to
the States.
The States have two Houses, and in nearly every
case one of those Houses stands for Propputy.
On the other hand, the Federal Legislature con-
sists of two Houses, both elected by the people.
So, quite naturally, the Victorian “People’s”
party barracks for the system where Propputy
has the pull, and will resist all attempts to give
the people any of the power that Propputy now
possesses.
The Tory minority in the Senate rose Ikst
week and stated with tremendous dignity that, as
the Democratic majority insisted on running
things its own way, it (the Tory minority) would
decline to hold further discussion. It proposed,
in fact, to wrap its mantle round its head, and
sit on the dignified retirement of its tail among
the back seats. There it will say nothing,
but will wear a prophetic expression symboli-
cal of the evil to come. The Tory papers spoke
of this calamity with bated breath. They implied
that the stupendous catastrophe which was bound
to happen through Labor fooling with the thick
end of the gun really had happened. And they
implied that the Government was stunned to dis-
cover what its folly had led to. As a matter of
fact, however, the news that in future Millen
won’t talk so much as he used to do doesn’t seem
to grieve anybody to any great extent.
The Pumpkin Smiths and other ancient
war-horses of the N.S.W. “Liberal” party got
all that was coming to them one recent day when
a certain “ S. Peters” wrote to the S. M. Herald.
“S. Peters” reckoned that it was the old brigade
which brought about Wade’s downfall, and he
spoke with the unobtrusive frankness of a mad
dog:—
Beyond the blatant blast of a few peripatetic political
bvgones and puerile upstarts, nothing was ever done in
the way of organising the Liberals in the country dis-
tricts. ...
The Central League in Sydney seemed to he more like a
mutual admiration society where old Parliamentary dere-
licts were wont to meet and recount their deeds of valor
during contests of years gone by. These worn-out old
veterans were sent out campaigning throughout the
State, and were inherently feeble in every respect. . . .
It is necessary that the Augean stable now exist-
ing should be purified by unceremoniously excluding
the present crowd of political deadbeats, sundowners
and hangers-on, who now infest the cellar headquarters
of the league.
He also threw other bricks and boiling waters at
the dreary “ Liberal ” organisation, and then be
kicked it hard, and dropped a boulder on it, and
buried it and poured asafoetida on its grave.
The voting returns of most of the 90
N.S.W. State electorates have now been officially
analysed. In Annandale, Gordon, Leichhardt,
Middle Harbor, Paddington, Parramatta, St.
Leonards, Waverleyand Woollahra —all suburban
electorates—the women’s vote out-numbered the
men’s. The gallant Col. Onslow’s constituency
polled the biggest hobble-skirt majority 5 *“®
little Dr. Arthur received most she-votes—s/00,
in Middle Harbor. Still, even these things won t
stop the suffragette movement.
A SUGGESTION FOR CHURCH
COMMITTEES.
At the Bowraville (N.S.W.) Church, Rev. Father Dur-
kin, when announcing’ the holding of a ball in aid of the
church funds, took exception to what he described as
the cheek to cheek style of dancing, which he stated was
gaining favor in the district.
N.S.W. Works Minister Griffith’s idea of
State brickyards gets more support the longer it
lives. While he was giving his views, the pros-
pectus of a new brick co. was actually being pre-
pared ; and since then the document has been
issued. The directors—including a leading archi-
tect, a builder and a contractor—estimate the cost
of production at 18s. 6d. per 1000; and the pre-
sent price of bricks at the kilns is quoted at 425. !
Moreover, it seems that the State doesn’t get any
concession; though it could comfortably keep a
Vug yard going, it pays Private Enterprise 425.
for goods which experts say they can produce for
18s. 6d. It’s obviously another case in which
Private Enterprise spells Public Hindrance.

Hear this typical “Liberal” statement
(Argus, 1/11/10) regarding the People’s Party—a
sort of mild political measles that has broken out
in a country town in Victoria :
The draft “ platform ” of the People’s Party, as de-
clared before the Inaugural meeting at Horsham on
Saturday, is really of less importance than the fact that
the organisation itself has been duly formed and sets out
on its career.
Policy, you see, is an unimportant thing. The
“platform” may he as barren as the Sahara;
but so long as there is some sort of “ organisa-
tion ”to whoop for the newspaper is satisfied. It
is merely keeping up appearances, like the bank-
rupt grocer who stocks his window with empty
biscuit tins.
The arduous toil of assisting at a record
Cup carnival proved too much for Premier Weary,
wherefore that fatigued person last week an-
nounced that his Main Roads Board Bill was
postponed sine die . and the usual municipal sub-
sidy would be parcelled out as heretofore the
sturdiest cadgers mostly getting the biggest
whack also, as heretofore,/ The subsidy will
amount to £267,292 this time, and will mostly go
in fattening the value of the property of those
who are fattest already.
Weary's Minister, Baillieu, has the
notion that it would be a good thing to borrow
£5OO 000 and spend it in one large splash on
“roads that would enormously increase the pro-
duetiveness of the adjacent properties.’ Accord-
ing to Baillieu, the annual value of properties in
shires is £7,332.397, and a Id. rate on this amount
would yield £30,550, which is enough to pay
interest and wipe out the loan in 31% years. Well,
if the shires like to raise £30,550 a year for 31%
years they can have £962,325 to spend on their
roads. Baillieu’s idea is that they shrill put
£500,000 on their roads and £462,325 into the
pockets of certain Fat gentlemen who will lend the
money; and £462,325 seems to be a good deal to
pay certain Fat gentlemen for the uncertain joy of
making a splash.
Victorian Treasurer Watt, who struggles
in vain against Tory strangulation of the alleged
“ Liberal ” party, has proposed a flat land-tax of
three farthings, to raise revenue. So the
“Country Party” is on its hind legs once again.
According to the Abgcs :
Each member has his own ideas, but a majority seem
to be agreed that, before there is any large increase in
taxation, the Treasurer should be able to prove to Par-
liament that the revenue is not being charged with
various expenditure of a developmental nature, which
could fairly be met—as it was in the past—out of loan
money, and that the State public service is being ad-
ministered in as effective and economical a manner as
possible.
It is the same old Toryism. Anything rather
than honest taxation. If the State is hard up,
sack the poor devils who work in State employ-
ment ; starve the schools, and let the children go
half educated. If money is needed, go to Cohen
for it, and let the children and the children s
children pay interest for ever. Anything rather
than a tax. " And anything rather than a land tax.
Yet this is the party which was “ going to put
the progressive land tax into force if the federal
Labor party hadn’t gripped the situation !
THE POLICY OF THE STOP-WATCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Argos, in a recent leading article bitterly de-
ploring hasty legist ition in the Federal Parliament,
(Hinted out that the man who drives a motor above the
speed limit is dealt with by the law. “Yet in the fur
more serious matter of furious driving in Parliament
there is no constable with a stop-watch.”
There was no haste when Tories ran
The legislative shandrydan.
■Twas rickettv and very slow,
And went where they would have it go ;
It crept, ahead three feet with pain,
And rolled a full yard back again,
In those good times the Council “ cop ”
| Would take his ticker out of pop
(A stop-watch this, as you might call
A watch that, never went at all);
Or, softly drowsing on a rail,
He timed the motor with a snail.
And so that shandrydan of theirs
Went nowhere much in fifty years !
But now the Labor crowd has been
• - And furbished up the old machine,
A stronger power the villains found
Behold, ye gods, the wheels go round !
And all apostles grey of Shirk
f Shriek horribly to see it work ;
For wise and wary Fat Men know
A legislature should not go
Except when rich men, parsons, kings,
Contented are to pull the strings !

Mount Larcombe Estate.
THE Lease of this well-known Estate having- expired,
the Trustees have been instructed by the bene-
ficiaries, who live in Scotland, to make an early
realisation of the Estate, with a view to a division of the
proceeds. , . , . , .
The property has an area of 14,484 acres, and is highly
improved. It was for many years used as a fattening
propery, but the advent of the Gladstone to Rock-
hampton Railway, which runs right through the Estate,
has brought it within about 14 miles of Gladstone at the
nearest point, and this makes the property very suitable
for dairy farming and other close settlement.
A handsome profit should be available from sub-
division.
For further particulars apply to
The Trustees, Mount Larcombe Estate,
c/o David Stirrat, Esq.,
Mount Alma, via Gladstone :
or J. F. Fitz Gerald & Power,
Solicitors, Brisbane.
