SPORTING
NOTIONS
“0.K.”: Now that the quality of imported
English thoroughbreds has been proved in the principal
w.f.a. race and the great test handicap of the V.It.C.
Spring meeting, it seems permissible for once to adopt
the stinking-fish prejudice, and look for comparative
excellence in the blood that is not native-born. Son-of-
the-Marsh, the Melbourne Stakes winner, is an imported
horse. He was bought for £550 in England, after win-
ning a petty event —a selling race. In this country he
has proved himself the fastest of 10-furlong gallopers,
having beaten the record for that distance ere he took
the Melbourne Stakes. Comedy King was bought as a
yearling in England, together with his dam, and
although he counts as a four-year-old in Australia,
he is about six months younger than the four-
year-olds locally bred. Consequently, the Mel-
bourne Cup victory of'Comedy King, even with only
7st. 111 b. in the saddle, was a fine performance. There
have been other imported horses that didn’t win the
Cup, but none of them were cracks, or nearly cracks, in
their own country. Nevertheless there was one of them
—Voyou, the runner-up to Merriwee in 1899—who would
have" won had he been quite fit for the long journey.
Four days later Voyou won the V.R.C. Handicap in
Smashing style. In the light of the performances of
Comedy King and Voyou it cannot be denied thjit Eng-
lish-bred horses, although lighter-boned and more sprint-
like in appearance, as a rule, than their competitors over
here, are able to stay with the best of them. The
harder ground seems to knock them out more quickly
. than it knocks out the old Australian stock ; but that is •
beside the point. Imported horses have been better-
class performers, on the average, than those of Aus-
tralian pedigree. Sir Foote, an English sprinter, holds the
record for the Newmarket Handicap distance (6 furlongs).
Mention of Sir Foote brings me to the crux of my
argument. He was the sire of Prince Foote, last year’s
Cup winner, and his countryman, Positano, another im-
ported horse, was the sire of three Cup winners in Lord
Nolan, Poseidon and Lord Cardigan. Apologue, who
scored in 1907, was bred in Maoriland ; so the last five
winners of the Melbourne Cup have been sons of horses
born out of this country. The moral is obvious. For
a combination of first-class speed with stamina,
look to the new blood in racing stock. It will
be said that such notable good ones as Trafalgar
and Alawa have an Australian pedigree, but
the pedigree is short in both cases. Alawa is a
grandson of Bill of Portland, an imported sire, and
Trafalgar is a grandson of Carbine, born in Maoriland, of
English parents. The long Australian pedigree is not
worth backing for Cup races nowadays. Sporting re-
porters enthuse over horses that are in a direct line of
descent from Malua or Grand Flaneur, or Chester or what
not; but the punter who wants to enthuse over a win on
Melbourne Cup Day must cultivate a prejudice against
animals whose grandfathers and grandmothers were
mighty performers on the Australian turf.
* * * *
The racing touts of Melbourne, if they had a
sense of the eternal fitness of things, would have drowned
themselves in a tank of beer after Comedy King’s capture
of the Cup. The one thing on which they all agreed was
the insufficiency of Comedy King’s preparation. They
saw the horse doing only ordinary work on the Fleming-
ton track, and never noticed that he was getting harder
every day as the result of the really good gallops l|e was
putting in. Although there had been a hitch in the
preparation of Comedy King whilst he suffered from a
cold, it was 1 always a Cup preparation ; yet the touts
gave the public and the bookmakers to understand that
the, all-important favorite was not being “ strung up ”
sufficiently to justify his position in the unpublished
betting-market. The fact that he ran like a stayer in
Sydney, and had been pronounced a stayer by ofvner and
trainer, weighed nothing with the cocksure toifts as
against the fact that they didn’t see the convalescent
horse being taxed to the utmost Over a long distance or
in sprints. They persistently underrated the value of his
track work untii he had run nowhere in the Melbourne
Stakes, and then on the intervening Monday before the Cup
they noticed that he was “ going strong at the end of a
mile,” or words to that effect. Neither they nor the
newspaper reporters ever gave the hardy-looking horse
credit for being one of the clear-winded sort that doesn’t
take long to recover from a cold, and is better over two
miles than a mile and a half in comparison with the
average high-class racer. The victory of Sol Green's
champion under the circumstances is an object-lesson to
trainers who think that a horse can be quickly converted
from a “miler” into a stayer. Comedy King got an
early start in his Cup preparation, and when he went
amiss he was not thrown right out of condition. He
merely went back a little, and, having recovered what
vital strength he had lost, the muscular animal resumed
his improvement at the point where it had stopped.
Think of the “ good things ” for the Cup that
didn’t finish in the first six ! Tnink of the combination
of circumstances required to make the “good thing”
that actually wins easily. The horse must be a very good
one over two miles, with at least a stone weight in hand,
trained to its best possible condition; it must be fairly
lucky in the avoidance of serious bumps and other inter-
ference, not to mention the risk of an unavoidable fall
through other horses falling immediately in front
of it. t And the jockey must be clever enough to
make things “fairly lucky” for the horse in an
ordinary way. These are essentials to a “dead bird,”
such as Revenue was of yore. This year there
was positively no “dead bird” for the Cup, though it is
practically certain that either of the first two horses
would have won easliy had the other been out of the
race. Comedy King had fully a stone in hand as regards
the handicap, but he was not in the very best of health
and condition. A horse that coughs before and after a
Cup race cannot win without the advantage of a favor-
able handicap. Trafalgar, as the race was run, had bad
luck in losing it. Cyril Bolton, who rode Mutilator, states
that his mount, being quite spun out, rolled against
Trafalgar at a critical point of the struggle, and seri-
ously checked the heavyweight’s effort to overtake the
winner ; yet Trafalgar was only half a neck to the bad at
the finish. Given as clear a run as Comedy King got, the
less favorably weighted horse, being in_the pink of con-
dition, would probably have the race was run.
On the other hand, it is possible that if Comedy King had
not been rushed to the front so soon, the same result
might have been attained in a different way. There was
so little to choose between the winner and runner-up,
that the best judges of racing are equally divided in their
opinions as to whether McLachlan, on Comedy King,
won the Cup by wisejockeyship, or nearly lost it by not
delaying his rush. ” #
What have the V.R.C. and A.J.C. stewards
been thinking of all these years, since the silly old weighing
apparatus was found to be open to fraudulent manipula-
tion ? Any sort of automatic weighing-machine is be-
yond the possibility of being faked by a person secreted
under the platform where it is placed ; nevertheless the
idea of substituting a,n unfakable “scale” was never
entertained by the conservative aldermen of the Turf
until it was thrust upon them by a pony racecourse pro-
prietor. Lord only knows whether there has been any
fraudulent weighing-in at Flemington of recent years,
but a lot of people seem to know that the scales were
worked bv the late Frank Kuhn and his accomplices
within the present century. But that reckless young
rascal was spared out of consideration for the “ respect-
able ” rogues who palpably profited by their knowledge
of the fraud he originated, and the whole thing was
hushed up. _
A coincidence tip for Melbourne Cup is credited'
to a girl in one of the Vic. up-country towns. She re-
marked that a Lord (Nolan) won it in 1908, a Prince
(Foote) in 1909, and nothing less than a King (Comedy)
could be expected to do the trick this year. What price
The Emperor for next year? Owners of unnamed two-
year-olds are advised to rush for the available title. First
come, first served.
* * * *
The sultry, stormy Oaks Day at Flemington
had its only virtue in its vindication of form. Two-year-
old Woolerina (who had finished third for theMaribyrnong
Stakes) won his second race at the meeting, and Beverage
did likewise, whilst Styria confirmed the truth of the
Derby running. Styria’’s victory in the Oaks was only
what punters had a right to expect, but Beverage’s cap-
ture of the Linlithgow Stakes was surprisingly meri-
torious. The best of Derby winners could have done no
more than carry a 101 b. penalty through the mud and
rain and win easily. Beverage gave 171 b. to Hartfell and
241 b. to Indiscreet, and beat them out of sight. Two
Caulfield Cnp winners in Aborigine and Flavinius followed
the Derby colt home. #
Punters had" what" "the horse reporters Call a
“ great” time on Oaks day, inasmuch as five first favor-
ites won. This amounts —on paper—to a terrible doing
for the books ; but it pans out thus for the backer of first
favorite at starting price. For the first race there were
three favorites at 5 to 1, and one of them won. If a
punter backed each of them for £1 he netted k 3 on the;
race. The next winner was a 6 to 4 on chance-; so the
punter won 13s. 4d. For the third race the favorite was;
beaten. The fourth winner was favorite at 9 to 4 against,
so the gain was £2 ss. Next winner (at 2 to 1 on)brought
10 “ bob ” to the lucky man ; and the last favorite was at'
6 to 4 against, so the punter added another 30s. to hisl
total. He thus won £7 18s. 4d., less £l, on the day. Had
all the favorites been beaten he would have lost £B/
What risks these bookmakers take !
* * * *
The first Sheffield Shield match of the season!
had a champagney finish. S. Aus., playing against Vic.,:
wanted 197 to win. Armstrong got three men cheap ;i
and then Clem Hill and Gehrs settled the question. Hilli
left just as the end was in sight. Gehrs wanted 10 tol
make his “century.” But only five were wanted to win,,
and when they were scored the game would stop. Therel
was just one possible chance for his century—and he tookj
it. With a four he made ithe scores a tie. and, with a
clean square-leg sixer into ithe pavilion, he made himself
100 not out. To judge'ifrom this game, Australia has
any quantity of but—allowing Crawford to be
not eligible—most of the test match bowling, except for
Armstrong and Whitty, will have to be supplied by
N.S.W. #
The Afrikander cricketers made their first land-;
ing at Adelaide just as the Victorian XI. arrived. Therd
were other casual visitors, too, so strange combinations
might be seen at the practice-nets : Alan Marshal, of Bris-
bane and Surrey, bowling to Llewellyn, of Hampshire and
Africa ; and Kelleway, of Sydney, to all-comers. Also old
George Giffen stripped, and his famous hanging ball
played havoc with good men 30 years his junior. Mayor
Cohen and E. T. Smith, the 80-year-old &A.C.A. presi-
dent, entertained and welcomed the visitors, and Sher-
well, the African captain, became thoughtful. He had
been told that Melbourne and Sydney would even exceed
Adelaide in hospitality; and, if so, “Please God, vye’ll
never get there ! ” he remarked piously. The 14 business-
like strangers can soon be sorted out. Faulkner, the
crack all-rounder, has a;keen,"shrewd face of the Braund
type. Llewellyn is short and dark ; Schwarz slight and
dark. Vogler has the figure of Hirst and the humor of
Briggs; he, is at. first glance the most dangerous
of '’the bowlers. But the change from matting to
Australian turf is trying. One, at least, of the team
had never,- been on a grass wicket before, so
hard practice has been prescribed. The preliminary
batting was naturally of a shoulder-opening order. There
are some hard hitters, and the tendency to pull every-
thing possible round to square-leg will give Australians
some brisk fielding in that neighborhood. Fitzgerald,
the manager, is a Victorian, a son of the late Nicholas
Fitzgerald, director of breweries and other things.
"*..* * * ;
Whatever interest remains in cricket was con-
centrated on the first appearance of the African “ Spring 1 -
boks ” at Adelaide, and the struggle between Queensland
and N. S. Wales was quite overlooked. Yet it was a closfc
match, Queensland crawling out with a majority of 19
after an especially good display of bowling. It was
a trundlers’ match, and not a century was made by
any batsman. In fact, the highest scores were 67 for
N.S.W. by Gow;, and, 64 by Hufcqheon for Queensland,.
N.S.W. smacked up 21'2 in the first attempt against
Queensland’s 163; but in the second effort the Ma
State could show only 135 to Queensland’s 203. Result, a
win for Bananaland by 19 runs.
S’Africa made a had start at Adelaide against
the State team. The wicket being far from a velvet road,
Hill, who won. the toss, sent the Afrikanders to the
wickets, and there got them down and fell on them.
The first wicket went for three, the second for four, and
the third for 19. It was a rot, and the case of the
strangers looked hopeless. The outlook did not improve
when the whole side vanished for 133. But at the end of
the day, when the South Aus. team had piled up 169 for
nine wickets, the African captain declined to feel crushed.
“ We’re bv no means beaten yet!” he remarked, dog-
gedly. And he wasn’t. The googley bowling was telling,
and Africa was getting more and more accustomed to the
turf wickets. Monday saw the S. Australians add only 14,
and pass out for IS3. The second innings of- the Dark
Continent was a revelation. The bowling was collared
by the scruff of the neck and the slack of the pants.
Strieker smote up 146, and Nourse carried his bat for 136,
and the day ended for 371 for the loss of six wickets.
* ■* * *
With inter-State and international matches
raging and Melbourne horse madness in full blast, local
Sydney cricket is a small, unimportant business just
now—except to the players. On Saturday, the batsmen
took a revenge on the bowlers who had played skittles
with them the two previous Saturdays. No less than
four centuries were recorded —Macartney, P. F. McElhone,
Thomas and Foster putting up 186, 148, 124 and
115 respectively. Two men smacked up 90 and over,
three over 80, six 70 and over, and two 50 and
over. And yet two of the bowlers managed to leave
decent figures on the sands of time. Sivyer, of Mr.
O'Hara’s team, dropped three Petersham wickets for 11
runs, and Andrews, of Petersham, laid out six of Mr.
O’Hara’s merry men for 57 runs. But the name of the
rest was Mud, and their tallies slush. It was the be-
ginning of a round, so there were no results to chronicle.
But the team scores were fairly staggering. Redfern
made 450 against Central Cumberland, Gordon smacked
393 for four against redoubtable Glebe, and YVaverleyput
up 340 against Paddington.
*** * *
Apparently, what the Australians require to
bustle these visitors is an uncommonly fast bowler, and
the question is, where shall he be found? The sudden-
ness and extent of the African improvement was a fearful
thing to contemplate. But it is their bowling that is
going tcv cover this country with woe. The little
band includes no less than seven googley bowlers,
and when last the English were here we found
Bosanquet quite enough trouble. He butchered some
fine young batsmen. With seven devils like these run-
ning loose on the cricket fields thi4 country looks likely
to go to the dogs. For it must be remembered that what
we have seen of. the African bowling has been on a slow
wicket. On a fast one these googley bowlers will be ter-
rific $ and there are, as said before, seven of them, and
they can all make the ball break at about 14 different
angles. Takes some thinking over.
•* * * *
Sherwell’s wicket-keeping is a feature of the
Springboks. In S. Australia's first innings he caught
two, stumped two more, and narrowly missed some
others. This nimbleness, combined with tricky bowling,
is likely to give trouble.
* * * *
The recent city v. country cricket match in
Brisbane was a dreary affair, which the former, although
represented by a weak eleven, won by 10 wickets. The
only bright spots in it were the batting of city-man
Hutcheon, who knocked up 85 runs in 75 minutes, and
the bowling of countryman McCloy, formerly of Padding-
ton (Sydney), but now of Ipswich, who took five wickets
for 86 runs.
* * * *
The Wtfetralian bowling tourists are having a
frightful time. At Melbourne the rubber went against
them by 73, 66 and 54 points; and at Sydney the team
was blown out by 126 to 71, 117 to 89, and 122 to 76 in
one rubber, and forfeited another. Still, they can hunt
Bullfinches, and that’s where they score over the East.
* * * *
South Aus. bowling lords are getting ready for
a high kick this Christmas. Teams from all Australia
and Maoriland will gather in Adelaide, which will
become an Armageddon of the trundlers,in which cham-
pions shall encounter champions, and the kings of the
game will be trampled on in the imsh. *
So Dr. Poidevin is coming home to settle in
Australia, and at time of writing had been talking to the
reporters at Fremantle. He condemned the Australian
tennis people for withdrawing their guarantee because
they ~did- mot; ~ consider the -English players of
international class. If this is correct, the Aus-
tralian tennis crowd deserves to be soundly kicked.
Sport is like diplomacy, and it is England’s funeral if it
sends along indifferent players. Thie Australian tennis
people were not likely to lose the deal, anyhow. Of
course, if the Australian Association objected to any
guarantees, the case would be different; but, to set
one’s self up in judgment on the team-selection of another
nation is too strong.
* * * *
Dr. Poidevin is pessimistic regarding English
cricket. The form is not up to the standard of a few
years ago, he declares, and judging by general appear-
ances, the doctor is right. “They play six days a
week, and only very wealthy men can afford
to play county cricket. There are too many
professionals.” In fact, the gold rot is going to
destroy' English cricket, £ven as it has ruined English
Rugby, and.-aa it rums everything it gets into. The
haughty gold-bug will not associate with the men who
work with either hand or brain, and, being left entirely
to himself and his brainless peers, deterioration sets in,
fatty degeneration supervenes, and the sport passes out.
Looks as if English regeneration will never be possible
till the nation kills off its gold-bugs.
*** . *
After much talk a match between Australian
rower Harry Pearce and William Webb, of Maoriland,
will take place on the Wanganui River on Boxing Day.
Palmerston sport Serpert is putting up £2OO on Pearce,
and £4O is to be allowed for exes. So everything in the
garden is blooming, especially as it is almost certain that
enough cash will be forthcoming after the j\ace to back
the winner against Arnst. Which is good news for big
Dick.
But Maoriland is howling that it has discovered
a new wonder in Arthur Jackson, of Blenheim, who will
one day wipe the floor with Arnst, and all the rest of
them, and reijrn as champion sculler of the world. They
are sanguine, these #
While he was in South Africa, big Jack Arnst
gave ’em a taste of the quality of his hind legs. In a
half-mile sprint and an unpaced pursuit race with the
African crack, F. Venter, Arnst came on top.
* * * *
There is war in Victoria, and the Cyclists’
Union is making frantic attacks on the Victorian League
of Wheelmen. Time was when the League was a badly-
managed affair ; but it has changed its executive, cleaned
out the stable, and set to work to pull things out of the
mud generally. But the Union will have none of its
reforms. It says the League is dead, and calls on it to
submit quietly and decently to burial. The League snorts
that it isn’t dead, and will, if necessary, crush the upstart.
The League represents 600 registered racing cyclists, while
the Union can scrape together barely 100. But, in the ful-
ness of its wisdom, N.S.W. League of Wheelmen has cast in
its lot with the usurping Union, and the fight grows thick.
Also, it grows inconvenient. As the N.S.W. League is
supporting the Union, its members cannot take part in
races held under the auspices of the Victorian League,
neither can Victorian Leaguemen race in N.S.W. unless
they first join the N.S.W. body as new members. The
result is chaos—and professional cycling is in such a
parlous state just now that a little touch of chaos is
likely to kill the whole hog stone-dead.
Guippone, a French motor-cyclist of credit,
renown and derring-do, and master of many records, is
dead smashed through the carelessness or idiocy of a fool
cyclist The fool rode round a training track on the
wrong side, and suddenly recognising his mistake, cut
across the front of Guippone’s car in which the crack
was training for a big race. Rather than kill this blun-
derer, Guippone took a header down the bank, and
found death waiting at the bottom.
Luck is a cynical demon, and the man who uses
a pistol loaded with ball cartridge to start a race is a
William animal. But at North Sydney, on Saturday, a
man fired a “gun” to start a race, and just as he
did so another johnnie popped his face over the
fence, about 50 yards away, and very consider-
ately prevented the bullet going any further,
where it might have done some damage. It is a most,
dangerous thing to use ball cartridges for starting pur-
poses, because it is only once in a thousand shots a man
will be found kind enough to put his face injrontand
prevent a window getting smashed or a girl's “art hat”
being ripped to shreds. But for this “ obstruction ”
something serious might have happened.
* * * *
The Mclntosh would appear to have England
by the scruff of the neck and the hip-pocket, with that
famous gag of his about finding a white champion to lay
out Jack Johnson. So far there is no sign of the encour-
agement bringing out any likely walloper for the big black.
Still, if there is a champion lying around in concealment,
the Mclntosh’s methods are those which will give him a
chance to come out and seize his chance of wrecking the
golden temple Johnson calls his mouth. There is more
than a dark suspicion that England has given up breeding
heavyweight fighters; and by the time the Mclntosh , is
through with his search, that suspicion promises to
develop into a certainty.
* * * *
Kid McCoy, the Melbourne “feather” who was
expected to develop into an Australian destroyer, demon-
strated the gfreafc lack of stopping quality in his method
of assault when he met Sailor Duffy at Melbourne Sport*
Club’s room on Cup eve. Duffy is the best thing pos-
sessed by that section of the British navy which was in
Victorian waters for the spring season ; but the man-of-
war was no class against the professional glove-wielder.
McCoy could jut a stiff left into his dial 15 times a
minute ; but still he failed to stop ye mariner of England
in less than 18. He did not bring Duffv to a aead end
even then ; but the referee called him off his victim, and
declared that the son of the sea had had what any reason-
able man would consider enough, and something over.
Kid McCoy, the winner, is a bright lad, but need not bo
labelled dangerous till he puts about 471 b. of extra
vehemence into each fist.
* * * *
The meeting between Jimmy Clubby (America)
and Bob Bryant (Australia), at the Sydney Stadium last,
week, left nothing to be complained about—except that,
it was billed as a fight or boxing contest. It was neithei
one nor the other; it was merely an exhibition of spar-
ring oh the part of Clabby. And taken as that, it was
worth the journey and the price of admission. There
is no doubt of the Yankee welter-weight’s cleverness.
His extraordinary length or sinuosity of arm con-
trolled by a judgment that appears to be kept on the
ice, enables him to bring off many freak wallops. At
least, that was the case against Bryant. But Brya,nt
doesn’t count. A more intelligent and less deplorably
rattled man would have taken overwhelming advantage
of the ridiculous positions into which this freak-sparring"
frequently brought Clabby ; on the other hand, a more
intelligent and less rattled man would probably not have
been given the generous invitations that were offered by
the Yankee. In the seventh round he went down and
took the count.
It will be a great disappointment if Clabby is
not extended while he is in this country. He certainly
showed at the Stadium ttiat he has a stock of remarkable
fight-goods if anybody demands them. Unfortunately
the very names of the Americans forwarded by the
Mclnsplosh seem to have inspired such reverence
amongst the poor string Australia has produced re-
cently, that fight-paralysis has frozen their joints.
* * * *
An explorer’s story which appeared recently in
the Journal, Alberta (Canada) When we reached
Grouard the telegraph construction gang was putting the
finishing touches on the line to that point. Working
with an axe in advance of this construction gang was all
that remained of Frank Slavin, rival of Sullivan and
other old-time idols of the ring. When interviewed,
Slavin did not say he was training to get back into shajpe
to tear the laurels from black Jack Johnson. He was just
working to get a living.” The busy stoush-merchant
who thinks It will Last For Ever is counselled to nail the
above excerpt over the prie-dieu in his boudoir.
* * * *
N.S.W. Motor Boat Club’s bouse at Rose Bay,
Sydney, will be opened on November 26. The finishing
touches are now being laid on, and a sub-cominittee has
been authorised to wander round and splash much
wealth in furniture. The same committee will also
attend to the laying out of the grounds, the storage for
petrol, and the arrangement of the lockers that will be
let for a merely nominal sum.
THE COMEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve a golden lining within my vest,
And a tenner’s a dainty toy,
For a Comedy King has played his jest
And the end thereof is joy.
There were lashins of laughter and heaps of glee
When the fiftieth Cup was run,
But the cream of the comedy was, for me,
That I got twenty to one !
We watched the neddy with doubts and fears,
And his price was unco short ;
We turned our money, and lent our ears
To this and to that report.
“ He’s got rheumatics ! ” (the punter quak,es);
“ He’s coughing ! ” (and hopes are-done);
He ran in the ruck in the Melbourne Stakes—
And I got twenty to one !
The day of the Comedy dawns serene
With a blazing- sky above,
And I am the millionth packed sardine
In the Ring where the punters shove.
Now hark ! the children of Moses roar
The odds for this horse of fun,
Fourteens —then twelves —then tens—no more !
And I got twenty to one !
The yell of a hundred thousand throats
Goes up to the smitten skj r ;
There’s the sudden flashing of silken coats,
And the king of the joke sweeps by.
Though some their Gargantuan stakes will mete
By the quarter, cental and ton,
My joy in the comedy’s just as sweet,
For I GOT TWKNTY TO ONB !
VICTORIA.

COUNTERFEIT COINAGE PLANT.
£2OO Reward.
HEREAS certain imitations of gold coins have
.recently been passed into circulation at Broken
Hill, in the State of New South Wales, NOTICE is hereby
Sfiven that a Reward of Two hundred pounds will be paid
by the Government of the Commonwealth for information
leading to the apprehension of the coiners and the dis-
covery of the plant used in the manufacture of the coins
referred to. The Commonwealth Government will request
the State Government to extend a full pardon to any
accomplice who will give the desired information. This
offer will remain open for three months from this date.
(Sgd.) ANDREW FISHER, Treasurer.

Sydney General Hospital will be 100 years
old next year. The building contract was made
by Governor Macquarie with D’Arcy Went-
worth, Blaxcell and Riley,' and the price was
paid in rum; hence for many years the institu-
tion was known as the Rum Hospital. Two of
the buildings erected a hundred years ago still
Stand—the Parliament Houses, which were sur-
geons’ quarters, and the Mint, in which the
coroner, as well as the hospital officials, had
J officeS and quarters. The main hospital was the
centre building of the,.three. —Before the erec-
tion of the hospital buildings .John Blaxland
owned an acre there. Mrs. Macquarie wanted
the land badly for hospital purposes. Blaxland
was in England at the time, in connection with
the- Johnston court-martial, and Mrs. Blaxland
was induced to part with the ground on the pro-
mise that Mr. Blaxland should have a g ood ex-
change on his return. Mrs. Macquarie had men-
tioned “the whole of Miller’s Point” as a fair
■thing to give; but Blaxland complained bitterly
afterwards that all he did get was “a paltry £4OO
for the finest acre in the city.”
“ Waihora ” begs to differ :
“ Henry H.” has some kind things to say about Auck-
land Harbor, and incidentally remarks : “ It is deeper
and more spacious than Sydney’s.” Well, if that is so, it
does not say much for Sydney’s cherished possession.
Auckland Harbor is beautiful to look at —high tide pre-
ferred—but, as a site for a naval base, is not in it with
some ocher Maoriland harbors. The approach channel,
Rangitoto, has an average depth of 31ft. at low watgr,
and this shallow area stretches for three miles. The.
anchorage ground shows 42ft. at low tide ; but at the'
wharves only 28ft. can be discovered. Nine months ago
the big liner Kaipara, drawing 27ft., struck heavily in
mid-channel whilst clearing the Heads, and was badly
ripped. Several other vessels have also touched in the
same locality. Auckland’s big dock—the Calliope—is not
capable of taking a modern battleship ; therefore,
the local authority is debating whether to enlarge
it, or ask the Admiralty to build smaller ships.
The deepest-draught vessel that ever sailed out
of Auckland was the Powerful, drawing 29ft., and she
felt her way out carefully at high water. (The rise and
fall of the tide is approximately 10ft.) The fault of Auck-
land harbor is that it is flat-bottomed, and resembles a
lagoon on a large scale. In the Hauraki Gulf, and within
50 miles of Auckland, there are, however, two magnificent
harbors—Bon Accord, at' Kawau Island (the islartd homo
of the late Hori Grey), and Port Fitzroy, at the Great
Barrier Island. PorO Fitzroy is landlocked, and they
measure the depth there by fathoms, not by feet
Whangaroa, bn the north-east coast, has also a claim
All of them, anyway, are ahead of Auckland.

Every Farmer His Own
Thresherman.
m
'ltijilj
Without a “Staling” Threshing Outfit, you get your grain threshed when it suits
your local Thresherman.
With one of these handy Outfits you thresh when it suits uou.
Threshes up to 25 bushels wheat, or 50 bushels oats per hour.
“Gets to business” inside 30 seconds, saves all hauling of wood and water, and
does away with all fire risk.
Dispenses with the horde of men that have to be employed and fed while working
the steam outfits.
Enables you to market your grain whenever you see fit, and thereby realize the
best price of the season.
Costs you £205 cash—You’ve sometimes lest a larger sum through missing the
market by wailing for the Threshing Gang.
If you can’t spare the cash we will sell you a “Sterling” Outfit on such Easy
Terms that the money you would otherwise pay the Thresherman will cover your instal-
ment, and, at the completion of your payments, you will not only have the Outfit to
show for the money invested, but will have made a substantial sum, quite apart from
your Threshing Profits, by the use of the 6h.p. “1.H.C.” Engine for such jobs as chaff-
cutting, pumping, wood sawing, etc.
Owners of “Sterling ” Outfits often thresh enough for their neighbours to repay
their entire investment.

The Inky Way.
“ N.0.T.0.N. Lines ”: The uninitiated; reading “ O.N.
Lines’s ” recent remarks on the Sydney Inky Way and the
cheap reporter trouble, might think that Sydney press-
men are not well off compared with scribes in other
centres. If “O.N. Lines” is “onlines” he is at least paid
for whatever he can get past the sub. ; but if Tasmanian
scribblers were on the same footing the newspaper pro-
prietor would think seriously of putting up the shutters.
Onlines! Why, there are juniors in Tasmania—juniors
who take senior engagements too —existing on from £1
to 30s. a week. One daily paper has been known to
pay a so-called “ cadet ” 10s. When one of these wretched
youths asked for a rise some time ago, he received a
reply that drove him to the alternative of punching
tickets on the trams. One daily recently had three
reporters receiving less than 30s. per week. Senior men
are paid a living wage of a kind ; none the less, it is a
fact that a Parliamentary staff man on one paper
was so poorly paid that a rival sheet thought
to buy his services for £2 10s. a week. Fortu-
nately, shorthand pressmen who are well in with
the Parliamentary powers, make an occasional haul out
of Select Committee and Commission work—hard graft
and money well earned. But now, even that source of
revenue has been cut into ; for When a man puts in an
hour or two at a select committee meeting in the morn-
ing, for which a guinea sitting fee and the usual transcript
rates are paid, at least one paper firm collects half the
guinea. And after earning this for his employer, the
reporter takes his turn at the afternoon and evening
sessions of Parliament, and does the fair day’s work that
is invariably insisted on.
* * * *
The Australasian Traveller is a sarcastic drummer.
In.its October issue it published a half-page eulogy of
Tait, chief cigar of the Victorian railways, in.view of his
early retirement. . “The legacy he leaves behind him,”
says the Traveller, “is of inestimable value.” And
right across the page are a couple of graphic pictures of
part of the legacy—two derailed and wrecked trains, one
at Bochara and one about four miles on the Maryborough
side of Clunes. “Nothing more unmistakable, short of
entire demolition," it is explained, “ could be imagined.”
The illustrations are published as “ two powerful adver-
tisements for the U.C.T.A.A.’s accident insurance policy,"
but as an illuminated address of farewell to Tait nothing
could be half as powerful or more exasperating.
* * * • *
“Martin”: Re “M. L. Cressman’s ” remarks (B.
20/10/’10) anent freelance writers, I see he has mentioned
my story, “Übique, the Scientific Bushranger,” in con-
nection with the M.L. syndicate market. If he considers
the stony tracks of Sydney any easier for the freelance
writer to travel than those of M.L. and Tasmania, he had
belter forget it. Since perpetrating “ Übique” (a matter
of 12 months or so ago) I have earned about £2 journal-
istically. The irony of the position (in my case) is, that
a well-known Labor organ that skites about encouraging
and developing Australian industry is at present pur-
chasing articles from the English and American” syndi-
cates referred to by “ Pressman.” There is no excuse for
side-stepping local' talent; for, apart from my unworthy-
self, I could mention dozens of better writers than the
much-lauded Americans who (to me) seem to be able to
do nothing except the U.S. Civil War, or the orthodox
slang yarn, which is Greek to anyone unversed in the
fried-coon slang dictionary.
* * * *
“Riverina” : I have solved the problem of the editorial
“we”! Lately I went through several snippet publica-
tions, and it suddenly became clear to me. The word
means a pair of scissors.

A tripe merchant who died in Manchester (Eng.) the
other day left a fortune of- close upon a million. There is
money in tripe. Still, none of the successful London
■ dramatists are quite so well off.
THE FRESH-BAKED DAILIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A newspaper for hungry people, which can be eaten
after being read, thus affording nourishment for the
body as well as the mind, is published in Paris, and is
called The Regal. It is printed with an ink guaranteed
non-poisonous, on thin sheets of dough.—News item.
Ev’ry morning in the tramcar, as my paper I peruse,
I keep asking in my whiskers, “Where the blazes is the
news ?
Ev’ry second cable-item in this weary' rag I read
In last evening’s penny Bloodstain ere I tottered off to
bed ! „
Then its politics are drivel and its humor is a sham ;
All the sporting information isn’t worth a tinker’s d —n !
Where’s the value of my penny in this lump of paste and
type?.
Absolutely'nothing in it but a lot of mouldy tripe?”
There’s a cupboard in our cottage full of daily papers
old;
Years ago they were an asset, for they could be saved
and sold;
But the refuse of the printer finds no ready market npw,
For the butcher cannot use it for concealing murdered
cow; ...
And the customer no longer likes to read upon the steak
That “staunch Liberals are paying this fair land must
soon awake
To the fact that Pitt and Hampden were inferior to
Wade.”
People will not eat these statements—of infection they’re
afraid.
Why in thunder can’t the D.T. and the Argus and the
N KWS
Print on dough instead of paper? We could make them
into stews
When we’d finished hunting through them for the facts
they don’t contain !
They might feed the reader’s waistcoat, if they cannot
feed his brain,
And give value for the penny that we fling them as we go
For the morning train or ferry in a pallid lump of dough.
We would not feel quite defrauded if, insteadof putting
by
One by one the Advertiser, we could with it make a pie !
In these whizzing days of hustle and these nights of
vicious haste
Ev’ry blessed soul is wmrking for the wiping out of waste;
Yet each day' the greedy sawmills of the forests take a
ffnlp,
And the logs go pouring pressvvard as a cataract of pulp ;
Frothy novels, dreary dailies from the bookstall stare at
me,
Ev’ry page of them a fragment of some noble murdered
tree!
See.that pile of doubtful grammar and that ghastly pile
of words ?
Half a year ago it flourished as a home of singing-birds !
Is it seemlv, think you, reader, that an ancient, useful
tree
Should be turned into a Herald or an Age or a D.T. ?
NO! (Let’s shout it all together in one frantic chorus !)
NO.' ! !
Friends, the time is ripe for printing these excrescences
on dough !
Let us keep our primal forests, for they bring the useful
• rain - - •
And extract our morning daily from the yearly crop of
grain !
It will help to save the country, and to all ’twill be a
, boon
From the Premier to the dosser in his printed dough
cocoon.
Nevermore need I go hungry when, for fear of being'late,
I desert the breakfast table and my bacon-laden plate ;
At the bookstall 1 will get a Daily Blither, from the boy
With a little pat of butter or a steaming saveloy ;
Then upon it gaily browsing to my waiting toil I’ll go
Mumbling, “Yes, it’s worth the money now they’re
printing it on dough ! ”
Can’t you hear the runners shouting “Fresh-baked
’Erald ! ” “’Ot D.T.! ” ?
Brothers, let us eat our dailies ! Spare the Brain and
save the Tree !
N.S.W.

BCSIIC G (Loifdon* X Pianos
TO INTENDING PIANO BUYERS.
Having discovered a piano with the essential qualifications of pure tone and durability
of construction, and with the added advantage of a practical scientific tuning system, you
naturally begin to consider the question of the appearance of the instrument.
You want one that gives some outside indication of its value. There are plenty of
showy pianos to be had, but in our whole range of twenty-four designs we have nothing of
the kind. We have aimed at a high standard of excellence in case designing, and our
models are absolutely correct in detail, whatever the style, whether Renaissance, Rococo,
Empire, or other period. The Beale pianos are art productions as well as first-class
musical instruments. They were awarded a Gold Medal at the Franco-British
Exhibition by the first furniture experts of Europe for the excellence of the cabinet-work,
and the high quality of workmanship which it showed. A Gold Medal was also
awarded for the factory organisation under which such work was produced. With the
exceptional distinction of the Grand Prix (for tonal qualities), and the next highest award,
Diploma of Honor (for patent frame and tuning system), these coveted prizes are a
greater number of awards than were ever made to one piano manufacturer at any exhibition.

IMPORTANT SUBDIVISIONS SALE
OF THE WELL-KNOWN
WOLAROI ESTATE,
Orange District, N.S.W.
Magnificent Orchard, Vineyard, Dairying and Market Garden Lands, within two miles of
Orange Railway Station, accessible by good roads.
Adjoining East Orange, and in close proximity to Well wood, Suma Park and Rosehill Estates.
TITLE TORRENS. NO SURVEY FEES.
Almost the last opportunity for acquiring rich agricultural land close to the thriving town of Orange,
the Metropolis of the West.
1553 ACRES, Subdivided into 29 Blocks.
AREAS 23 to 119 ACRES. Highly Improved, Nearly All Cleared.
FOR AUCTION SALE AT THE FORESTERS’ HALL, ORANGE,
Thursday November 24, 1910, at 2 p.m.
The soil is a rich volcanic and black loam, capable of intense cultivation, yielding a high return
to the farmer. The town of Orange furnishes a permanent market for all produce grown in the
vicinity, while as an important Railway Junction it commands the towns east and west, and also
the branch lines to Condobolin and Forbes.
Average rainfall 40 inches annually, ensuring certain crops.
The climate is regarded as the most perfect in Australia, the elevation above sea level being
3000 feet.
MAin road to Iceley runs through the centre of the property, and some of the blocks are
approached from the Bathurst Road.
Permanent water in the Summer Hill Creek running through the subdivision, and otherwise
permanently watered.
The proper ty is offered by the Vendors (The Orange Freehold Land Company, Ltd., Registered
Office, 35 Paling's Buildings, Ash Street, Sydney).
TERMS :10 per cent, deposit; no further payment for 12 months. Balance in six equal annual
payments, with interest at per cent, per annum.
The Auctioneers strongly recommend the subdivision to the attention of those desirous of
securing a small, rich, agricultural property in a progressive and reliable district, and with an
assured market close at hand. Inspection particularly invited. Wolaroi affords an almost last
opportunity of acquiring a farming property near to Orange.
Solicitors : McLACHLAN & MURRAY, Sydney and Orange.
PLANS and ALL PARTICULARS FROM THE AUCTIONEERS,
J. S. LEEDS & CO., Orange.
ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS
OF THE
Bodaivgora Gold Mines
NO LIABILITY.
To be Registered under the Companies Act, 1899-1900.
Capital: £25,000, in 25,000 SHARES OF £1 EACH.
12,500 Shares fully paid up are to be issued to the Vendors as part payment for
their interest in the property.
12,500 Shares are available for issue, payable Two Shillings and Sixpence on
Application, Two Shillings and Sixpence on Allotment, and the balance
in calls as and when required, but not exceeding Two Shillings per
share per month.
(The whole of these Shares have been underwritten before the issue of this Prospectus.)
Directors (not more than FIVE or less than THREE):
Provisional: A. E. SAVAGE, Esq.; O. H. KERRY, Esq.;
H. MONTGOMERIE HAMILTON, Esq. ; and E. 11. ALCOCK, E q.
Bankers: BANK OF NEW SOUTH WALES at SYDNEY and WELLINGTON.
Solicitors: A. j. TAYLOR and GREENWELL, Citizens’ Chambers, Moore Street, Sydney.
Consulting Engineers: SAVAGE & ASHCROFT, Castlereagh House, Castlereagh Street, Sydney.
Brokers: <T. E. DUCKER, 135 Pitt Street, Sydney; R. CRAN, Yaralla Chambeis,
Pitt Street, Sydney ; W. SOUTAR, Post Office Chambers, Pitt Street, Sydney.
Seerctarit and Office: R. D. HARTWELL, Castlereagh House, Castlereagh Street, Sydney.
The Company is being formed to acquire a gold-mining property containing about 154 acres,
known as the “Dick’s Reef,’’ at Bodangora, near Wellington, N.S.W., together with a 20-head
battery already erected in a situation convenient to the mine.
The Dick’s Reef mine has been worked down to the 500 ft, level, but was closed down owing to
the death of the late owner. After being idle for some considerable time the present owners acquired
the property, had the mine unwatered, sampled and reported upon by Messrs. Savage and Ashcroft,
whose report and assay plan are printed in the prospectus.
These reports show that 6000 tons of payable stone have been left standing above the 500 ft. level,
while the reef is exposed on the bottom levels for 1060 ft. in length, the whole of which is of payable
size and value, while there is every indication that payable stone will extend beyond these measure-
ments.
Taking into consideration the great depth and extent of the adjoining Mitchell's Creek Mine,
there is every prospect of the Dick’s Reef property being profitably worked for a long time to come.
Copies of the prospectus, giving further particulars, may be obtained from the Brokers, or from
the Secretary.

. , )bbi , ' Bananaland has a law dealing
with the sale of land on which rates have been
ever^ U timL ng fh 01 ' S6Ven yea , rs or more - and nearly
every time there is a sale there is also a big
bother. It isn’t a bad law in the main • the
trouble is that the land is advertised so poorly-
merely as subdivision so-and-so, of allotment
ves-do, of section 617.” The average cottage
ver^m/T 1 fc v educat f d U P to plan-reading, and

recently bought at the over-due rate sale for £4
re-sold to the clerk of the Board for £2O, and sold
by him for £4O ; and even that was only equal to
50 per cent, of the council’s valuation. As an
amendment, I propose that the council’s valuation
be the reserve; and if the allotment doesn’t fetch
the money, then it must pass automatically into
the possession of the Council.
Died at Gatton, aged 80, W. A. Cross,
who was one of Brassey’s engineers during the
building of the railway line from Grandchester to
Toowoomba in the middle Ws. After that
and a few other jobs had been finished he joined
the Queensland Railway Department, and stayed
there till, in his old age, he settled down to the
cultivation of the cow and the cucumber at
(jratton.
Another much-boomed mission enterprise
has had some hard clods of truth thrown at it in
Queensland. A while ago the P.M. at Cairns
v , ,sl . ted Yarrabah and threw the first lot of
clods, there was a howl of indignation, where-
e Bananaland Government instructed
Ohief Protector of Aborigines Howard to go and
make a full and exhaustive report on the place,
the report was given to the Assembly the other
day, and it amounts to a still larger shower of
clods, including these: The blacks are not
satisfied with the food and clothing, and are anxious
to get away; there has been woeful mismanagement
from an industrial point of view during the 18
years the mission has been in existence ; an air of
indolence pervades the place; no real effort has
been made to produce even enough food for the
inmates; there has been want of efficient super-
vision ; several boats have been lost through care-
lessness ; and there should be a large income
instead of an annual deficit of about £lBOO a year.
After which, it is about up to the Government to
decide on looking after Black Brother itself.

Minister for Public Instruction Barnes has
issued a set of 10 maxims or commandments to be
hung on the walls of schools in Bananaland for
the guidance of the young. The Decalogue of
oinai was getting a bit out of date, so Barnes, who
is a pumpkin and potato merchant, has issued a
Decalogue of his own. It was Barnes who in-
formed the Assembly some time ago that William
Kidston had been drummed out of his native
town of Falkirk—a statement without a shadow
of foundation. In the old Decalogue there was
a certain injunction against “bearing false witness
against thy neighbor.” This is probably whv
Barnes doesn’t like the old Decalogue.
Private Enterprise doesn’t run much to
lighthouses and such-like useful but unproductive
goods; still Queensland has had one sample
for 20 years past. The light is at Canaipa, on
Russell Island, near the southern end of Moreton
Bay, and belongs to Mrs. Wills. For most of the
time Mrs. Wills has supplied the oil at her own
expense, but within the last few years the Queens-
land Marine Department has furnished it. A
week or so ago a number of shipping people who
i* part Ba y reckoned that Mrs.
Wills had at least earned a formal thank-you ; so
the lady has now an illuminated address to hang
in the parlor.
“San Remo”: Not long ago, when an
extra wallop was being given the immigration
drum, considerable dust was raised concerning the
prevalence of the married-couple-without-encum-
brance evil. Remember that ? Very well. Now,
the Springsure Hospital is a Government-subsi*
dised institution, and the other day it advertised
as follows: —
Applications, with testimonials, will be received from
Married Couples (without encumbrance), the woman to
undertake the duties of cook and laundress, the man to
undertake the duties of general servant, to the above
institution, at a joint salary of £ll7 per annum and keep.
I want to know what Kidston is going to do about
this, and I don’t think I ought to be kept waiting
. more than seven daj r s for an answer.
They don’t beat about the bush in the
woolly West. Recently a Winton (Q.) man adver-
tised that his wife had left him, and that he
would not be responsible for any debts contracted
by her. Hereupon madame promptly retaliated
(also per ad vt.) that she had never yet been able
to get a bob’s worth of credit in his name any-
where. This is the kind of thing that makes the
country press quite readable at times.
Moore, P.M., of Brisbane, was rather
astounded recently to hear a young scamp of 17
beg to be kindly awarded six months in prison,
inasmuch as he, the scamp aforesaid, “ was afraid
of getting a bad name on the streets, and wanted
a chance of getting a good fresh character in
gaol.” The Bananaland ex-prisoner, advertising
for a job in future, is evidently going to mention
proudly: “ Highest references from Boggo-road
and St. Helena.”
The new Anglican cathedral in Brisbane has
its foundations in a State endowment. This way.
Many years ago the Church of England received
from the State a grant of land (then of trifling
value) in George-street. On this a pro-cathedral
was erected, and when the Morgan Government
wanted the land for a public purpose the Angli-
cans scooped in over £30,000 from the sale—a
handsome contribution towards the new taber-
nacle. Unearned increment and the late Bishop
Webber have been mighty cathedral builders in
Brisbane.
“ If ever you raise memorials in this great
State, let it be to the memory of your pioneers.”
So said Sinclair, one of the Scottish Commis-
sioners, at Brisbane recently. Already a
“scheme ” is “ understood to be under considera-
tion and, as might have been expected, the
scheme is “to erect some suitable memorial
near the entrance to the University.” The
Britisher is hidebound on the subject of “monu-
ments.” Mention the subject, and his instant
idea is a big piece of stone with a figure on it
beckoning to some prickly pear or other object on
a distant hill. Occasionally someone battles for a
scholarship or a fund to give education, art or
medicine a lift; but for astart it is always the lump
of stone and the bit of lettering and the cartload
of palaver.
Mrs. Rumor says that both Bob Philp
and Joe Lesina will not be starters for the next
Parliament. In each case there are solid reasons
for scratching. Philp’s Townsville majority has
been dwindling these many elections ; and the
whirling Lesina is a party of one now. Philp is
the lather of the House; also the parent of
many deficits.

The Gossips who gloat o’er a poor woman’s shame,
lheir fellows who hasten to blab it
The Neros who fiddle while Rome is a’-flame,
The Cadgers who spit on a brother’s fair fame,
Are nothing but Creatures of Habit.
QUEENSLAND.
FURNESS BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP
Brisbane, November 5, 1910.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The Brisbane Liedertafel is suffering from tired
feeling. At its final concert last Wednesday, in
the very faded and untidy Centennial Hall, it sang
upon a stage much in need of a polish, and without
so much as a palm to hide its dinginess. The
chorus was a trifle dingy, too, at times; and the
audience—well, as the society columns say, it
was en suite. Why this very excellent society
should be falling off I don’t know, but
hope the malady is only temporary. There
were redeeming features in the programme,
mind you. “ The Red King ” by the choir,
with Mr. Crisp and Secretary Harris as
soloists, went finely. Mrs. Gilbert Wilson sang
with all her old seductiveness, and Percy Brier’s
concerto with the orchestra was a thing to
dream of. Percy is worthy of larger honors than
Brisbane has to give.
Andrew Black will assist the Brisbane Musical
Union at its final song recital next week.
There’s heen trouble in the Amen Corner. Last
week the Bible in State Schools Bill was being
mauled in the Uppercut House (which, by the
way, achieved a record by sitting from 3.30
p.m. to 6.45 next morning), and the gal-
leries were filled by Judkinses and Mrs. J.’s.
These strange people, not content with listening,
insisted upon interjecting, and at last Clerk
Costin hailed a bobby. “See here, constable,”
hissed that usually mild little man, “you must
not let those women, or the men either, say ‘Lord,
love him ! ’ when Mr. Brentnall favors the Bill, or
‘ Lord, have mercy on him ! ’ when Mr. T. C.
Bierne condemns it; and they must' not talk, and
must sit down and not hang (ve the rail. It’s
very disconcerting to members.” So the boy in
blue padded up the stairs, where his presence
chilled the godly, and Mr. Bierne didn’t get
prayed for any more. But he and Peter Murphy,
A. J. Thynne and F. McDonnell put up a rattling
fight before the thing went through its second
reading. There is still hope that a lot of amend-
ing will be done in committee.
One of the most popular officers in the Educa-
tion office nearly found himself at Charon’s ford a
few days ago. He is a cheerful, kindly soul, with
no affection for red tape; so why an infuriated
female should have selected him as a victim of
unsatisfied wrath I don’t know. Anyway, she
trotted into his office, and, after an affable “good
day,” pulled out a gun and fired. The bullet
missed his eye, but made a gaping hole in
the wall. The suffering lady is now being taken
care of.
Mrs. E. A. Cullen, back again from Europe,
tells startling tales of hobble skirts and of hats that
hide you to the waist; but she seems to have dis-
covered a rag or two of a far less objectionable
cut.
A native of Papua, brought down for a holiday
by his boss, was asked last week what he liked
most in Australia. Without a moment’s hesita-
tion, he plumped for the bagpipes that welcomed
the Scotch Commission. Now, I wonder if that’s
the influence of Papua’s old Governor, our Wee
Macgregor, coming out ?
Billanderson’s co. has been starting five horses
in what looks like a trotting event on the boards
of His Majesty’s Theatre this week, to the peril
of the stalls and the big drum. No equine has
come over to date, but some of the escapes have
looked so narrow that an ant couldn’t have
crawled over them. Harrie Ireland, reformed in
character since last here, now makes a hobble-
skirted and most attractive heroine.
The Anglican Church here is in for a row some-
thing like the one the Presbyterians had over the
Frackelton business. The cause of the trouble
is a small person of advanced views and an uncon-
ventional way of expressing them. Perhaps that
wouldn’t matter much ; but he gets a full church
to listen to him, and it isn’t far from the new
cathedral either. Anyhow, the powers that be
declare that, in spite of the congregation, he must
go. The curious part of the business is that he is
to be allowed to continue in office until June next.
Evidently they haven’t considered what a power
of harm may be done to the sheep before then.
Mrs. Claud Munro (she was pretty Kitty More-
liead) is down from her Ingham home with a
bouncing boy. Talk about the dreadful North,
where the white woman cannot live! Mrs.
Munro has her matchless skin and color quite
unimpaired, as you will be able to see for your-
self, as she flees to the Blue Mountains with this
epistle. Baby has had the prevailing whoop,
and calls for a little bracing up.
The first outpouring of the Voice in the new
Cathedral occurred when Miss Townshend took
the Rev. Bertie Peregrine Walker, rector of
Gayndah,. for better or worse. Dr. Donaldson
put on his red robes, and Archdeacon Rivers
assisted, while Mr. Sampson made the new organ
swell out into the wedding-march. The bridal
gown was a simple satin, adorned with some nice
diamonds and emeralds.
_ fo geydom is much put out. Young
I. W. McCawley, “ a mere boy of 28,” has been
appointed Crown Solicitor, in succession to the
late George Hellicar. Old fogeydom can’t, how-
ever, say that McCawley’s feet have not grown
big enough for the boots, because he has been
acting-C.b. for six months. The brainy young
man, who is a barrister, has been in the Govern
ment service almost since his infancy.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.

THE CHILDREN’S ENEMY.
A Safe, Sure and Reliable Specific for WORMS in CHILDREN and ADULTS.
THE symptoms of the presence of worms are very pronounced ; but should the indications prove incorrect, a course of COMSTOCK’S “ DEAD SHOT ”
WORM PELLETS will do no harm whatever. An examination of the stools is the surest way to determine the presence of worms, and when found
treatment should start at once to rid the system of the parasites.
The Long Thread Worm measures from one inch to two inches in length, the upper portion being thin and the lower two-thirds about twice as thick.
This variety also infests the large intestines and the rectum.
The common Thread Worm derives its name from its thread-like appearance. The average length is about one-third ot an inch, this description
applying to the female worm. The male worms are much smaller, and do not exhibit half the length of the females. The favorite situation of the Thread Worm
is the large bowel which forms the terminal portion of the intestines. These worms cause severe itching at the lower passage and picking at the nose. Ansemia,
restlessness, irritability r md loss of appetite are also frequent symptoms.
The Round ’VVwrrn in its appearance, save in color, of course, somewhat resembles the earth worm in shape. As in the case of the Thread Worms, the
female worm very much exceeds the male worm in size. Whilst the Thread Worm inhabits the large intestine of children, the Round Worm is found in the small
bowel, or that portion of the vq-estine which immediately comes off from the stomach and makes up the succeeding 20 feet in length of the digestive tube.
Adults, of course, are frequent! Iso affected with Round Worms, and it may be added that they are also subject to be troubled with Thread Worms.
COMSTOCK’S “ DEAD v SHOT ” WORM PELLETS are a purely vegetable preparation compounded on a scientific principle for the eradication of
worms which infest the intestines. They contain specific properties which destroy the worms, but cause no injury to the stomach or bowels, and do not
weaken the system. The pellets arej prepared in the form of a confection or lolly, have no disagreeable taste or smell; but, on the contrary, inspire children to
take them as they would an ordinary lozenge. Obtainable from all chemists, or direct from The W. H. Comstock Co. Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Church Hill, Sydney;
and Farish Street, Wellington, N.Z. Post free on receipt of price, is. 6d. per packetsix packets, Bs. 6d.

A. G. Fox : The story of the female chain-
makers in England is absolutely true, and there are
dozens of other sweated industries, some even worse,
there was an Exhibition of Sweated Industries in London
a few years ago, and the catalogue was a horror. As to
why the Cradley Heath women “don’t come to Australia
as immigrants ” : (I) They haven’t the money. (2) Even
if their passages were paid from London they couldn’t
get to London, nor feed themselves for a day there—they
haven’t the means to get to the next town. (3) They
haven’t clothes for the voyage—practically their whole
wardrobe is what they stand up in. (4) They haven’t the
spare pennj’ with which to write to the Agent-General
for information about Australia, and many of them can’t
write. (5) They haven’t the spare penny to buv the paper
with the Agent-General’s advertisementin it so that they
may learn about Australia—besides, many of them can’t
read. (6) They are difficult people to approach by lec-
tures, for they are too busy making chains to attend
lectures. (7) They hardly know that there is such a
place as Australia ; as a rule, they have never seen the
sea, and all the world outside theiT little circle is a mist.
(8) If they came here, landing in rags and penniless—
what would happen next ? They know nothing of country
life in any shape or form. They know just about nothing
of domestic service ; life in a practically unfurnished
hovel, and the habit of sleeping on straw, and the
cookery which consists of getting meals of weak tea,
bread, dripping and a casual bacon-rind, doesn’t teach
domestic economy. In fact, most of them only know
how to make chains H.D.McP. : No, the Federal Con-
stitution doesn’t prescribe any fiscal policy for the Com-
monwealth—neither Freetrade, Protection nor Revenue
Tariffism.... Bellerive : A fine example of aNo License
poet’s frenzy. Here it is :
IN THE HORRORS OF DRINK.
Back, ye fierce reptiles
Glide back to thy lair
Shrieked a wretched drunkard
In Frenzied Despair
Forsaken by his once
Loving children and wife
Left to die in the horrors
In the prime of life
He whirled a beer bottle
And with an hideous yell
Shattered it to pieces
And prostrate fell
The unfortunate drunkard’s
Grim sufferings were o’er <
His pallid corpse was discovered
Outstretched on the floor.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.
J. Murphy: Please send address to accountant
W.8.A.: Not much to go on ; but what there is seems
promising.
J. Weary (of Vie.) is progressing happily
along the line of least resistance. The likelihood of
Upper House hostility to his Housing Bill has
led him to shelve that useful measure. Nowa-
days, Weary is always taking that way out of a
possible scrap.
“Miles”: Contrast the German style of
preparing for war with the British. A few
weeks ago the Emperor sent to the Burgomaster
of Danzig; informing him that the city must
billet 500 officers and 15,000 soldiers for the
manoeuvres. The response was joyful, both by
burgomaster and citizens. No less than 90,000
soldiers were altogether billeted in Danzig and
the region about. The people like it. Their sons
will have a picnic another time. No rowdies.
Then turn to England—afraid to quarter soldiers
on anybody! Which means, biz ? At those
manoeuvres, by the way, aeroplanes and airships
totally failed in reconnoitring. The occupants
were too easily deceived by bogus works. The
machines always failed to discover a line of
attack, being necessarily too high up. So far the
aeroplane, heavier than air, beats the airship,
lighter than air. The aeroplane can mount the
highest, and thus destroy an airship, as things go.

HALF-YEARLY REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS OF THE BANK OF AUSTRALASIA,
With the Accounts for the Half-year to April 11,1910.
Presented to the Proprietors at the Half-yearly General Meeting held on Thursday, October 6,1910.
REPORT,
THE Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance-Sheet as at 11th April last, with the Profit Account for
the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and Colonial rates a,nd taxes,
for all bad and doubtful debts, and for a 10 per cent, bonus to the Staff, the net profit for the half-year
amounted to £193,722 16s. lOd.
To this sum must he added £17,471 11s 4d. brought forward from the previous half-year, making a total of
£211,194 Bs. 2d., out of which the Directors have declared a Dividend for the half-year at the rate of 12 per cent,
per annum, or £2 Bs. per share, and a Bonus of 16s. per share, free of income tax.
The Dividend and Bonus will absorb £128,000, and the Directors have further appropriated £50,000 to the
Reserve Fund, and £lB,OOO in reduction of the cost of Bank Premises. There will then remain £15,194 Bs. 2d. to be
carried forward to the next account.
The Dividend and Bonus will be payable, in London and in the Colonies, on 7th October.
Since the date of the last Report new Branches have been opened at VValwa, in Victoria ; Junee, in New South
Wales ; Charleston, in Queensland ; Wirrabara, in South Australia; Beverley, Gnowangerup and Northam, in
Western Australia ; and Dannevirke, Te Aroha, Te Kuiti and Whangamomona, in New Zealand. Agencies have also
been opened at Fish Creek, Kiewa and Stony Creek, in Victoria ; and the Branches at Davyhurst and Peak Hill, in
Western Australia, have been closed. ANDREW R. SCOBLE, Chairman.
4 Threadneedle Street, London, 26th September, 1910.

“ J. 5.8.” on the Polite Inventor :
The politest squatter who ever addressed a crowd of
shearers was the late Wolseley, of shearing: machine fame,
a nd onetime owner of Euroka, Walgett (N.S.W.). When
engaging his shearers he used to say : “Now, njen, I
want all the wool and no skin. They won’t buy skin in
England.” He used to insist upon the penner-up giving
a Preliminary performance of his “barking” abilities,
■Tin unless he considered the “ bark ” to be of first-rate
would dismiss the aspirant with the remark :
Oh, my good man, you can’t ‘ bark.’ One of you men
eoine here who can ‘ baric.’ ” Over each man’s stand was
a concern like a billiard-marking board to show how many
times a man has “ starred ”at Devil’s Pool. When the slide
disclosed the third star, it was good enough to go up and
get your money. It was the troubles Wolseley met with at
shearing times that produced the shearing machine. He
invented it with the idea of reducing the price of shear-
ing, but the shearer of to-day, instead of cursing, blesses
■ it. Wolseley—a brother of" Garnet, by the way—once
sacked the famous Jerry Gerard ; but, on finding he
owed the station store £l, wanted him to go back and
work it out. “Oh, never mind,” said Jerry, “you can
give it to the hospital,” “But,” quoth Wolseley, “you
owe me a pound.” “Yes, yes, I understand,” replied
Jerry ; “ but I never trouble about these small amounts.
Give it to the hospital.” It took Wolseley about half an
hour to think the problem out.

REXONA, the Rapid Healer, is an Ointment which is specially
prepared for Burns and Scalds.
If applied promptly it eases the pain at once.
For a common burn, simply rub on a little Rexona, and repeat
again at night, and after a few applications you will find it quite
healed.
If a burn is more severe, use Rexona freely. Spread it on clean
O
linen, and wrap round the in-
jured part in such a way as
to fully exclude the air.
This dressing should he
changed frequently, and the
Rexona applied directly to
the burn or scald.
Not only does Rexona ease
the pain and cause the sore to
heal, but, being an antiseptic
and germicide of the highest
order, it keeps the wound
healthy, and prevents all
danger of blood-poisoning.
Burns and scalds are acci-
dents which are liable to
happen in every household.
Unless you have a tin of
Rexona in your home now
at the present time, you are not prepared against such dangers.
Be wise, be prepared. Get a tin to-day.
The little 12-months-old baby of Mrs. Ella Halliday, Margaret Street,
Norwood, S.A., had a wonderful recovery from a severe burn by the use of
Rexona. Mrs. Halliday writes as follows:

stove and got a terrible burn. The poor little dear cried for hours, the pain was so
bad. It had been bad for three weeks. I had tried all kinds of Ointments, but
none seemed to do him any good, and he seemed to be getting worse every day. I
• did not know what to do with him until I heard of this new Ointment called
Rexona. I tried it, and the first application did him a lot of good. After two
more applications baby's burn was quite cured without leaving a mark. It seemed
to draw the pain out, and had a great soothing effect.”
Rexona is a tried and proved specific for Burns, Scalds, Running Sores,
Bad Legs, Eczema, Piles, Chilblains, Varicose Veins, Chapped Hands, Sore

“A POLICY FOR THE COMMONWEALTH”
