The Gamblers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PETER was seated on the stable roof,
eating bread and jam. By this you
would know it was the time of afternoon.
From that lofty position he could command an
admirable view of his world, and fling stones at
most of it with considerable accuracy.
Just over the roof to the right was the Wes-
leyan Chapel, so near that it was mere boredom
to lob stones on it, and so high that it formed a
standard of comparison by which Peter measured
such minor things as fowl-sheds or fences, or any
other things which a Humane Society erects for
small boys to climb upon.
From the roof he could look down upon the
yard, and suffer sore temptation to pelt at fowls.
He could look upon his father’s house and the
groom’s room alike, the groom’s room being
eminently the more desirable dwelling of the two.
There was the kitchen garden to speculate upon,
and the front garden—valuable because of several
good climbing trees. And beyond that lay the
less important portion of Peter’s world comprised
in the township of Redheap.
Peter finished off his piece, and looked about
for an excuse to become active again. There was
a stone on the roof, so he threw that at the parson’s
horse, in a perfunctory manner, as though it were
a trifling duty which he had overlooked; and
then, finding no other sins of omission in the way
of stones, he had a look through the spy-hole.
The spy-hole was a small orifice cut in the roof to
look at robbers through. At present it merely
commanded a very dim view of the loft, but there
was a certain exhilaration in knowing that, if
robbers were to come into the loft, he could see
them easily through that hole. He had a very
thin shingle, nailed by a very small nail, sticking
•out into the yard, and this he dignified in his
mind as “ the plank.’’ It was for pirates to walk
on, it being a fact well known to Peter that
walking the plank was one of the invariable
habits of pirates. There was also an iron bolt
tied to a piece of string to swing round the head,
a bottle with the end knocked out to make loud
sounds with, and a tin tobacco box containing
two screws, a piece of lead, a chop bone, a dead
lizard, and a piece of soap, though what they
were doing on the roof was rather a mystery.
No doubt, there were occasions when any boy
might find a screw, or a piece of lead, or a chop
bone, or even a putrifying lizard handy to have
about him ; but the presence of the soap was
wholly inexplicable.
However, there being no signs of robbers about,
Peter took a tour round the roof and discovered
another hole that looked into the buggy sued.
The groom was in there piucking a towl, so
Peter was enabled to spit on his friend’s hat
without difficulty.
The groom endured this passing office of friend-
ship with an agreeable humor till he had occasion
to remove his hat to scratch his head, upon which
he saw tit to become a trifle dignified about it.
“Drop gobbin’on a bloke, d’yer see, or I’ll
take an’ warm yer,” he said ; so Peter obligingly
desisted and fell to practising a complicated
whistle that involved the aid of two rather grimy
fingers.
He was deeply absorbed in this business when he
was disturbed by a shrill yodelling sound, about as
difficult to locate as the chirp of a locust. Peter
instantly set up a piercing yodel in answer, and a
small boy of about Peter’s age, in an Eton suit
and a large straw hat, darted into the lane and
anxiously searched the landscape.
“ What-o, Jo-ho ! ” yelled Peter.
After a vacant period J o located his friend, and
gave a howl of recognition.
“Peto, what-o!” he screamed.
“ Come up,” shouted Peter.
Jo shook his head violently, and holding up
three fingers pointed down the flat and yodelled
exultantly.
Peter, to whom this pantomime was evidently
intelligible, ran straight at the edge of the roof,
cast his legs over, found a projecting nail with
one toe, and came down with a rush on the
fowlhouse.
‘“How much?” he asked, breathlessly, scram-
. bling over the fence.
“Thrummer,” said Joe, showing the coin, and
; the two small boys ran off together, in such evi-
*dent concord as clearly showed that there was
regarding the question of ‘‘thrummers.”
The flat ran down by the local gasworus, and
i. beyond that to the Chinese camp. Half-way
down there was a house with a high hedge and a
. white gate, and from behind the white gate, for
;>;all the world as if he had been waiting for the
imprecise moment, there darted a small tubby lad in
long trousers, who bolted straight across with the
evident intention of cutting off Peter and Jo be-
fore they reached the corner.
Peter and Jo pulled up instantly. Peter gave
an exhibition of passion on the spot, by snatching
up a rock and dashing it into the gutter.
‘‘Three cakes of Fyshang ain’t enough for
three,” he said ferociously. ** They’reon’y enough
for one and a half.”
“ ’Nless we chuck for it,” said Jo doubtfully.
“’N’ then we might on’y get two, or one,” said
Peter, in anguish at the idea.
The situation was certainly desperate, and they
conferred anxiously. Then they set off briskly
and met the tubby lad at the corner with well-
acted astonishment. Peter was particularly
effusive in the warmth of his greeting.
“Good old Bill-o,’’ he said, patting the tubby
lad repeatedly on the back.
Bill was evidently too keen a diplomat to be
deceived by a mere pat on the back. He had the
brow of a logician, and he appeared to be endowed
with the faculty of profound contemplation.
In Peter and Jo’s attitude before him there was
something clearly deprecatory, as it might be of
two laymen in the presence of an astute lawyer.
Bill eyed them up and down, as though arrang-
ing in his mind the various heads of his impend-
ing cross-examination, and then put a leading
• question.
“ Where yer going ? ”
“ Peter’s goin’ for their cow,” said Jo.
Bill turned to Peter, and waited for him to con-
firm this statement.
“I’m goin’ for our cow,” said Peter, without
looking at Bill.
It s a bit off having to look for cows,” added
• Jo, without looking at anybody.
It s a bit off if he’s fell, down a hole,” said
Peter to Jo.
“He’s a fair terror for failin’down holes, vour
• ■cow, said Jo to Peter.
They paused and looked nnxioudy at Bill. Bill
looked particularly profoundjand.made a chirrup-
ing sound. , . , -r, ,
He looked so verv profound that Peter was im-
pelled to try and put the whole responsibility of
the affair on to the cow.
“ He’s-the rottenest cow out, our cow, he said.
“ I suppose he’s about five miles up the diggings,
or lost, or something. Me an’ Jo 11 have to find
him. On’y, don’t you come, Bill, he added,
generously, “because, you know, it am t up to
much fun lookin’for cows.”
At this, Bill livened up. He laughed subtly.
He came close up to Peter and chirruped in his
fa< “ Your cow’s up the paddock,” he said.
“ He ain’t,” roared Peter, enraged.
Bill chirruped like a fruit garden full of spar-
rows, and there was quite an uproar for a moment,
because Peter had to hoot for all he was worth to
cover his confusion.
But Bill refused to allow Peter’s hoots to mate-
rially affect the situation.
“ You can’t kid me about your cow, he said;
and he kept that obvious fact so persistently before
the company that Peter was utterly unable to con-
trol his passion.
“All right !” he roared. “ You won t get none
of our toffee—y’ see ! ”
At that magic word there was an instant change
in Bill’s attitude.
He stopped chirruping and looked perturbed,
and Peter, having fatally put his own pot on,
looked perturbed too.
But Bill was equal to the emergency, and the
first indication of his profound powers of diplo-
macy was a distinctly conciliatory note in his
chirrup.
His next move was to pat Peter on the back.
This was so far successful as to remove Peter’s
scowl and cause an expression of doubt to take
possession of his countenance.
His next was to produce from his pocket a half-
eaten pear, which he held up in an inviting
manner before the company.
Peter and Joe regarded the pear, and an in-
voluntary movement of their lips showed that
they were deeply interested. Peter, however,
did his best to maintain a firm and dignified
attitude.
“Pears ain’t up to much,” he said.
Bill laughed in a manner that insinuated that
there were pears and pears, and that this pear in
particular was one of the rarest. Then he took a
bite of that pear, and munched with such an ex-
pression of enjoyment that Peter’s dignity melted
away like butter in the sun.
“Sharin’s, Bill,” he suggested humbly.
Bill thoughtfully took a few bites while con-
sidering this proposition, and then showed his
appreciation of the delicacy of the situation by a
hint.
“ What price toffee? ” he said.
At this Peter and Joe hurriedly explained that
the toffee was as yet a mere delightful prospect,
but Bill was disposed to speculate on the strength
of the threepence.
So the pear was shared and the small boys ate
it, and then ran off amicably to the camp, yodel-
ling and prancing and jumping all the gutters.
The Chinese camp was a miserable collection of
shanties in the last stages of decay. The roofs
bulged in and the walls bulged out, and where the
whole affair threatened to collapse the sides were
propped up with saplings.
The roofs were piled up with rocks to keep the
wind from blowing them off, the general rule
being that the more decrepit the roof, the more
enormous the rocks required to keep it on. There
were holes in all the shanties, some of which
were patched with tin, while some were not.
Some of the doors owned enormous padlocks,
while the house was entered by a hole in the wall.
There were some old Chinamen so infatuated in
their perversity as to prefer dwelling in a desper-
ate ruin with half a roof and two walls, while
houses in comparatively good repair were utterly
deserted.
A number of old Chinamen were seated in the
roadway, nursing little half-caste children. They
appeared to be entirely at the command of a
shock-headed lady in deshabille, who was smoking
a short pipe in the doorway of the only prosper-
ous-looking shanty in the camp.
Bestowing familiar greeting on the old nursing
Chinamen by name, the three small boys pushed
open the door of an aged shanty and called loudly
on the name of Sin Kum.
“ Wha’ for?” roared a voice from somewhere in
the rear of the premises.
“ He’s cookin’,” said Peter, hurriedly. “ See if
his tin’s about anywhere.”
There was a simultaneous rush for Sin Kum’s
bedroom —a dank, evil-smelling kennel almost in
total darkness, containing a dungheap of a mat-
tress and an opium tray. They found an old,
rusty meat-saw and a basket; but nothing in the
shape of food except a jar containing an evil-
looking preserve, the aroma of which justified a
suspicion that it might be poisoned and conse-
quently unsafe to eat.
Disappointed in this matter, they trailed in
procession to tne back yard, where they found Sin
Kum, a morose old Chinaman—if the word may
be applied to any old Chinaman by way of dis-
tinction —holding a post-mortem on the remains of
a fowl.
“ What-o, Sin Kum. How yer popping?”
said the small boys with brotherly familiarity.
Sin Kum dissected a portion of the fowl’s in-
terior, and then indicated Peter imperatively
with the chopper.
“You buy?” he demanded.
“No, him buy,” said Peter, indicating Jo.
“ How muchee you buy?” inquired Sin Kum
of Jo.
“Tee lee pen,” said Joe, showing the three-
pence.
Sin Kum was apparently satisfied, by the sight
of this wealth, that the object of the visit was
honorable.
“ All li—bim-bye,” he said, and returned to his
post-mortem, the small boys squatting round him
to watch the operation at their ease. They were
very soon interested, and anxious for information
regarding the fowl.
“ What you do with him, Sin Kum?” inquired
Bill, indicating an unseemly mess of fowl’s in-
nards, which Sin Kum had preserved in a pot —
“ You eat him?”
“ Eat him—all li—welly ni,” said Sin Kum.
Peter esteemed this reply a matter for humor,
and laughed heartily.
“By cripes! I wouldn’t like to have to eat
him,” he said, hilariously.
“Chows’ll eat anything,” remarked Joe, as a
piece of general information.
“ They’ll eat cats any day,” said'Peter.
“They’ll eat dogs, too, when they’re handy,”
said Bill.
Peter took exception to this statement as being
improbable, and instantly he and Bill were
involved in a frantic argument.
“It’s the known fact that they do eat dogs,”
shouted Bill. “It can be proved.”
“Prove it.”
“ Prove it yerself.”

“ Ah Foo’s got a dog, and so’s Jimmy Ah Wah,
and they don’t eat them,” roared Peter. “So
that proves it.’’
“Old Monkey John’s gotter dog—on’y he’s
dead,” remarked Jo, as an additional piece of
evidence for the defence.
But as Bill persisted m refusing to accept Peter
or Jo as authority on the habits of Chinamen, the
matter was put before Sin Kum as an expert.
“You eatem dogs, any day?” demanded Peter
of Sin Kum.
“You likee welly ni dogs, Sin Kum?” said
Bill, persuasively.
“ No fee—no likee dlogs,” remarked Sin Kum,
absorbed in his post-mortem.
“Narked yer,” roared Peter to Bill in an
ecstasy of self-congratulation.
“ He does eat dogs—he eats them any day he
likes,” shouted Bill. “loughter know, because
I read it in a book.”
“ I reckon Sin Kum ougliter know a bit more
about Chows than you,” said Peter, contemptu-
ously.
“ You think yer know a fat lot about Chows,”
said Bill. “You must be a Chow.”
Fortunately. Jo caused a diversion as Bill
raised this delicate point of discussion. He had
wandered into a small outhouse and evidently
found something of an interesting nature. Bill
and Peter hastily entered the outhouse, and found
Jo trying to secrete a small melon up his waist-
coat.
With their assistance he got it mostly up, where
it protruded in a most enormous and undignified
bulge, refusing to admit of more than two buttons
fastening.
“ No one’d notice it,” said Peter, looking side-
ways at the melon, to see if it appeared smaller
that way.
“ By cripes, it feels a bit tight,” said Jo, whose
countenance was almost purple in his effort to
breathe with facility under the melon.
“See if it fits me better,” said Bill. So the
melon was tried in Bill’s waistcoat with even less
success. They tried it in Peter’s, and burst two
buttons. They tried it up the backs of their
coats, and it merely fell out again. Then Bill
conceived a brilliant idea.
"Cut it in half,” he said. It was done, and
the melon was disposed of—half under Jo’s and
half under Bill’s waistcoat.
They found some incense sticks which they took
to light crackers with. They took some tinsel off
a dilapidated Joss, who seemed to scowl in im-
potent indignation over the state of his shrine ;
and they took a carrot. They would have taken
a pumpkin but for the utter impossibility of
secreting it with any hope of success.
This eminently satisfactory business finished,
they sallied out and found Sin Kum, for reasons
doubtless known to himself, pushing pieces of
cane into the fowl. He put the fowl into a pan,
and the pan into the outhouse, and was then free
to attend to business.
“ How muchee you buy ? ” he demanded.
“ No buy—shake, Sin Kum,” said Jo.
“Shake—all li’,” said Sin Kum, with evident
approval, and led the way inside. In the palmy
days of the diggings, when even Chinamen had
money to burn, Sin Kum’s house had been a cook-
shop in rather a large way. There were still the
ruins of numerous benches and tables, and on one of
these Sin Kum placed a china bowl containing
three dice.
“Tee lee shot, Sin Kum,” said Joe, putting
down the threepence.
“ Tee lee shot—tee lee pen—all li,” chanted Sin
Kum.
He changed the threepenny piece for three cop-
pers, and retired to the back, whence, after a
mysterious absence, he returned with a biscuit
tin, which was hailed exultantly.
“ Let’s see how much there’s in him, Sin Kum,
old bloke,” said Bill, affectionately.
Sin Kum saw fit to refuse the request, and also,
for reasons probably known to himself, kept a
tight hold of the tin during the whole of the
business.
It was adjudged that Peter should try his luck
first, he having commanded Fortune on the last
occasion, and there was a breathless pause while
Sin Kum threw.
“Thirteen,” counted Bill. “Pete ought to
beat thirteen.”
Peter frowned portentously, picked up the dice,
and threw eighteen. Instantly the three small
boys set up a terrific shouting.
“ Sudden death, Sin Kum—sudden death!” they
yelled, and Bill made a desperate effort to get at
the tin.
“No fee—tee lee shot,” protested Sin Kum,
indignantly.
“Itelfyou sudden death,” yelled Peter. “I
said it—gimme me cake.”
"Give him his cake,” chanted Bill and Jo in
chorus.
“ All li,” said Sin Kum, stolidly, taking up the
basin. “Nothlow.”
This strategic decision at once quelled the dis-
turbance.
“ All right,” said Peter indignantly, “we give
in,” and the business was resumed.
“Fourteen,” counted Bill anxiously, when Sin
Kum had thrown.
Peter drew a deep breath, and encouraged by
his friend to “go it,” at length went it, with a
most satisfactory result.
“Fifteen,” howled Bill ecstatically. “Done
him easy.”
There was a great deal of undignified triumph
a t Sin Kum’s expense, while he produced a. cake
of peanut toffee, dusted the flour from it into the
tin, and deposited it on the table, where the three
small boys exulted over it.
They were jubilant when the throwing began
again, and Sin Kum stolid. At the end of the
throw he was still stolid, but the small boys were
not so jubilant.
“That’ll show yer what luck is,” said Bill dis-
gustedly, as Sin Kum pouched the copper.
“ Anyhow, I got oneouter him,” said Peter.
“ He ain|t a very big one,” said Bill, measuring
the cake with his thumb.
“ I suppose you kid yourself yer could get one
as big as a house,” sneered Peter; and after they
had left that question still unsettled, there was a
fresh wrangle over who should throw next.
“ I ain’t goin’ to chance it,” remarked Jo firmly.
“ I killed a robin Thursday week.”
Bill and Peter quite agreed that it would be
mere recklessness to tempt Fortune after such a
mishap ; but there was a prolonged and heated
argument before they could settle who was
best qualified to risk the second penny.
“Oh, all right, then,” said Peter at length;
“you reckon you know a bit about the game.
Beat him —that’s all I say ! Beat him ! ”
Bill clearly felt that the responsibility was on
him to accomplish that desirable end. It was
clear, also, that he was not going to take any un-
necessary risks. He spat over both shoulders,
and then on both his boots. He made a great
fuss about cleaning some flour out of the bowl,
and when at length he took up the dice in his
hand, delayed such an unconscionable time before
he could bring himself to make the plunge that
Peter’s patience was exhausted.
“ Throw 1 Why don’t yer throw ? ” he yelled
suddenly, and so upset the serious nature of Bill’s
calculations that he promptly fell into a passion.
“ Yer’ve put me clean off now,” he said ; and,
just to prove it, he went through the whole per-
formance again, with a few additions. But the
result clearly justified a little extra trouble of that
sort, for he threw 16, and nearly went out of his
mind with satisfaction.
“It on’y shows yer,” he said warningly to
Peter; and Peter, having handsomely acknow-
ledged his error, they requested Sin Kum to have
a throw.
He threw 16, and a perfect howl of anguish
greeted his performance.
“ It on’y shows you what luck Chows have got,”
said Peter; and Bill’s preparations before he could
be induced to throw again were unending. But
all he turned up was a miserable five, while Sin
Kum threw 16 without an effort.
There was a mighty fuss between Bill and
Peter over this, and Bill suggested that, as Peter
seemed to think he knew everything, he’d better
have a try himself. Having failed to induce
Peter to take his place, B 11 insisted on Sin Kum
throwing first, and there was a breathless silence
while he threw.
“ Seve.n,”said Bill, greatly relieved ; “lougliter
beat that easy.”
As one assured of Fortune, he threw carelessly,
and Fortune, with a fiendish grin, turned him up
a despicable four.
“Now, I hope yer think yer funny,” said
Peter, enraged. “Yer can’t beat seven.”
Bill was furious. He fell on the penny, and
made a desperate effort to balance with Fate by
knocking over the bowl, and then protesting to
Heaven that Sin Kum had only thrown four.
“Sevum—by Cli ! ” roared Sin Kum, enraged
at this base deception. “ Wha’for vou call’em
fo’ ? ”
“Ep—gee—som—say!—Four!” shouted Bill,
counting on his fingers. “ You throwem four, me
throwem four, so throw again, or you won’t get
yer penny.”
“You cheatem, by Cli!” yelled Sin Kum.
“Four—no fee—sevum, by Cli ! Wlia’ for you
cheat?”
“ Wha’ for—l never,” shouted Bill. “It was a
tie.”
Sin Kum spread out the dice in a fury and
burst into impassioned oration.
“One shot — sixeyteen, tie, all li. Two shot,
sixeyteen, fi, all li. Tee lee shot, Severn, four—
you knockein d’lice — you takem pen —wha’ for ? ”
Bill tried so desperately to howl down this
lucid conception of the affair that Sin Kum, as
the saying goes, did his block in.
He aimed a frantic blow at Bill and missed (
him, and then he turned on Peter, who
was trying to get at the tin, and gave him
a sharp rap on his head with his fist. Such
was the pandemonium at this—Peter and Bill
sparring away like clockwork just out of Sin
Kum’s reach, and Jo making offers at Sin Kum’s
skull with a piece of board—that one old China-
man looked in at the door and said “ Wha’ for? ”
Then, seeing it was merely a business affair of
Sin Kum’s, he went away again, and several
voices said “ Wha’ for?” from various parts of the
camp in tones of indignant remonstrance.
“ All li,” roared Sin Kum at last, in despera-
tion. “You allee same lallikin—you dam’ bad
feller —you cheat, by Cli! No thlow, by Cli ! ”
and he banged the basin recklessly into the tin,
banged down the lid and glared ferociously at
Bill, as though he contemplated banging the lot
at his head.
At this ultimatum Bill and Peter ceased spar-
ring and Jo lowered the board.
“All right, then,” said Jo. “Give him his
bloomin’ penny.” So the penny was handed to
Sin Kum, who took it without thanks for such
tardy justice, and produced the basin again.
“Bill’s luck’s out,” said Peter. “it ain’t any
use hiin.thr.owin’ again.”
“I was just gettiu’ me hand in,” said Bill.
“If he hadn’t got that last shot-Fd a won him
easy.”
Peter forbore to suggest that this statement
verged on the platitude.
“The fact is, your Ihck’sout,” he said, deci-
sively, and the case being too desperate for argu-
ment, it was decided best for Peter to throw.
He spat on his hands and made a great show of
resolution. There was no humble propitiation of
the gods of Chance about his attitude. There
was defiance and contempt in it, both for those
ungracious deities and for Sin Kum, their officiat-
ing High Priest. ; Such behavior marks the
desperate gambler.
“ Sudden death,” he said, arrogantly. “ None
of yer blooming old tee lee shots this time.”
Sin Kum merely assured himself that the
penny was in position, and threw.
“Seven,” said Peter, derisively. “I always
beat seven.”
He threw six !! !
Exactly at the same moment Sin Kum grabbed
at the penny, and Bill made a frenzied rush at the
tin. Sin Kum got the penny, and Bill got a
handful of flour. He might just as well have got
a handful of cakes while he was about it, for in
addition to the flour, he got a swinging punch on
the head from Sin Kum’s bony fist.
For a moment things were so busy that Sin
Kum had hardly time to yell. Before lie had
quite finished dealing with Bill, Peter had fas-
tened his clutches on the tin, and while Sin Kum
was attending to him, Jo got the board into posi-
tion and hit him a sounding crack on the skull
with it.
Tne ear-splitting yell that greeted this piece of
strategy aroused a mighty chorus of “ Wha’ for ?”
from all over the camp, and Sin Kum ran down
the passage with the tin shouting “Wlia’ for?”
himself.
When he ran back again he was still shouting
“Wha’for?” but the urgency of his desire for
information was considerably emphasised by the
addition of a long bamboo.
This was the signal for the visitors to depart,
and they departed in a mighty hurry, and Bill,
who had not quite finished his fandango over Sin
Kum’s punch, got pushed in the back as he ran
out at the door.
Sin Kum chased them well out of the camp,
and they ran all the way to the Bed Bridge! on'
their own account. It was about a mile away
and they ran there, partly because of the bamboo
and partly because the deafening transit of occa-
sional trains was considered greatly exhilarating
to the enjoyment of food.
But the melon proved to be unripe, and the pro-
cess of dividing the cake of toffee into three parts
of mathematical equality, and the exceeding
smallness of the parts when divided, caused mis-
anthropy to distinguish the proceedings under
the Red Bridge. But they ate the one
cake of toffee, and they ate the melon, and
then they ate the carrot. Then, there being
nothing else to eat, they sat under the
bridge till the five o’clock train went overhead,
and though the passing of the five o’clock train
was deafening to an extreme degree, it hardly
compensated for the lack of those cakes of Fy-
Shang toffee squandered at the altar of the gods
of Chance.
